Oath in Option of Defect and Exceptions
Discussing when the judge administers oaths without the plaintiff's request

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on administering an oath in khiyar al-'ayb and identify the three exceptional cases where the judge administers an oath without the plaintiff's request.

Core Idea
In khiyar al-'ayb, the judge may administer an oath to the buyer without the seller's claim to protect the judgment from reversal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a critical procedural point in the law of option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). The author presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the judge should require the buyer to swear an oath when the seller has not alleged that the right to return has been waived. The preferred view, following Abu Yusuf, is to administer the oath, and this is part of a broader set of exceptional cases where oaths are administered without the plaintiff's request.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural issue
What is the legal question being disputed? Determine the specific scenario: the buyer wants to return the item due to a defect, but the seller has not claimed that the right to return has been dropped. Should the judge administer an oath to the buyer?
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute between the early view and Abu Yusuf.
2
Understand the opposing views
State the early view: the buyer is not made to swear because the oath is meant to cut off litigation, but here it would initiate it. Then state Abu Yusuf's view: the buyer should swear to protect the judge's judgment from being overturned later.
Abu Yusuf's reasoning is to safeguard the integrity of the judicial decision.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind each view
Explain the logic: the early view relies on the general principle that oaths are only requested by the claimant. Abu Yusuf prioritizes the practical need to prevent reversal and sees the oath as a protective measure.
The text notes that most judges follow Abu Yusuf.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under the early view, the buyer would not swear, leaving the judgment vulnerable. Under Abu Yusuf's view, the buyer swears, strengthening the judgment.
The preferred view is adopted to ensure finality.
5
List the three exceptional cases
The passage mentions three cases where the judge administers an oath without the plaintiff's request: (1) khiyar al-'ayb, (2) maintenance from an absent husband's wealth, and (3) preemption. Place this in context.
These exceptions are explicitly listed in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى جارية وادعى أنها خنثى يحلف البائع لأنه لا ينظر إليه الرجال ولا النساء إلى هنا ما في فتح القدير تبعاً لما في المعراج وفيه : ولو أراد المشتري الرد ولم يدع البائع عليه شيئاً يسقطه لم يحلف المشتري لأن التحليف لقطع الخصومة وفيه إنشاؤها. وعند أبي يوسف يحلف صيانة لقضائه عن النقض لو ظهر ذلك في ثاني الحال بالله ما علم بالعيب حين اشتراه ولا رضي به ولا عرضه على البيع، وأكثر القضاة يحلفون بالله ما سقط حقك في الرد بالعيب من الوجه الذي يدعيه نصاً ولا دلالة وهو الصحيح، وأحب إلي أن يستحلفه وإن لم يدع ، ولو ادعى سقوط حق الرد يحلف اتفاقاً .اهـ. وقدمنا أن خيار العيب على التراخي ولو خاصم ثم ترك ثم عاد . وخاصم فله الرد كما في المعراج أيضاً. وذكر في الخلاصة والبزازية أن القاضي لا يستحلف الخصم بدون طلب المدعي إلا في مسائل منها : خيار العيب وقد ذكرناه الثانية النفقة في مال الغائب لا يقضي بها حتى يستحلف المرأة الثالثة الشفعة لا يقضي بها حتى يستحلف الشفيع وكتبناها في الفوائد الفقهية مفصلة .
And if he bought a slave-girl and claimed she is a hermaphrodite, the seller takes an oath because neither men nor women can see her. So far is what is in Fath al-Qadir following al-Mi'raj, and in it: And if the buyer wants to return and the seller has not claimed anything that would drop [his right], the buyer is not made to swear because the oath is to cut off litigation, and in it is initiating it. According to Abu Yusuf, he swears to protect his judgment from reversal if it later appears: 'By Allah, I did not know of the defect when I bought her, nor was I pleased with it, nor did I offer it for sale.' Most judges make him swear: 'By Allah, your right to return due to the defect has not been dropped in the manner he claims, explicitly or implicitly.' And that is the correct view, and I prefer that he be made to swear even if he does not claim [the right]. And if he claims the right to return is dropped, he swears by agreement. End. And we have stated that the option of defect is without delay, and if he litigates then leaves then returns and litigates, he has the right to return as in al-Mi'raj as well. And it is mentioned in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya that the judge does not make the opponent swear without the plaintiff's request except in certain cases: one is the option of defect which we have mentioned; second is maintenance from the wealth of an absent person, which is not decreed until the woman swears; third is preemption, which is not decreed until the preemptor swears, and we have written them in detail in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyya.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths (yamin) in khiyar al-'ayb. It begins with a case of a female slave claimed to be a hermaphrodite: since neither men nor women can examine her, the seller's statement is accepted with an oath. The main discussion is about whether the judge should administer an oath to the buyer when the seller has not claimed that the right to return has been dropped. Normally, the judge does not initiate an oath without the plaintiff's request. However, in khiyar al-'ayb, most judges (and the preferred view) require an oath from the buyer, following Abu Yusuf, to protect the judgment from reversal. The text lists three exceptions where the judge administers an oath without the plaintiff's request: (1) khiyar al-'ayb, (2) spousal maintenance from an absent husband, and (3) preemption. This shows the special treatment of khiyar al-'ayb in Hanafi procedure.

Key Terms
تحليف (tahlif): The act of administering an oath to a party in a dispute.
سقوط حق الرد (suqut haqq al-radd): The dropping or waiver of the right to return due to defect (e.g., by use or consent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which case does the judge administer an oath without the plaintiff's request?
A) All cases of sale disputes.
B) Only in khiyar al-'ayb.
C) In khiyar al-'ayb, maintenance from absent person, and preemption.
D) Only when the seller requests it.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-'ayb, the judge administers an oath to the buyer without the seller's claim to protect the judgment; this is one of three exceptional cases where oaths are given without the plaintiff's request.

Women's Testimony for Hidden Defects in Sales
Explaining the evidentiary value of women's testimony for hidden defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the admissibility of women's testimony for hidden defects before and after delivery, and apply the intra-madhhab variant positions.

Core Idea
One woman's testimony suffices for litigation, but return requires two witnesses—except before delivery where later Hanafi authorities allow return on one woman's word.

In Hanafi sales law, hidden defects like qarn and ratq are invisible to men, only discoverable by women. This creates an evidentiary challenge. The classical Hanafi masters developed a nuanced rule distinguishing between the right to initiate litigation and the right to demand return. In this lesson, we examine the key positions from the Hanafi school on the testimony of women for such defects, noting the important difference between pre- and post-delivery scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect type
Recognize that hidden defects like qarn (vaginal bone obstruction) and ratq (vaginal closure) are invisible to men, requiring women's testimony.
These are examples of defects that 'men cannot see'.
2
Distinguish between litigation and return
In Hanafi procedure, one woman's testimony establishes the right to sue (tawajjuh al-khusumah) but not necessarily the right to return the item (radd). Return generally requires two just witnesses.
This distinction is central to the ruling.
3
Classify the temporal context
The ruling depends on whether the defect is discovered before delivery (qabla al-qabd) or after delivery (ba'da al-qabd). Pre-delivery has more lenient rules.
After delivery, return is not allowed by women's testimony alone.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the differences: Abu Yusuf's earlier view (no return before delivery), his later view (return before delivery), Muhammad's later view (return before delivery), and Muhammad's narration in al-Nawadir (return even after delivery).
The later views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are preferred.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that for non-arising hidden defects before delivery, return is allowed by women's testimony. After delivery, only litigation is established; return requires the seller's oath or two witnesses.
This is based on the principle 'al-‘amal ‘ala al-muta’akhkhir' (action is on the later).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن القاضي إنما يحتاج إلى قول الأطباء عند عدم علمه بالعيب، أما إذا كان من ذوي المعرفة نظر بنفسه كما في البزازية ونظر أمين القاضي كهو كما في البدائع واشتراط العدلين منهم إنما هو للرد وإن أخبر واحد عدل توجهت الخصومة فيحلف البائع كما فيها أيضاً ولكن في أدب القاضي أن المرأة تكفي لا لأجل إثبات العيب والرد به بل لأجل توجه الخصومة على البائع، أو يحمل على ما قبل القبض كما يفيده ما في الخانية حيث قال : وفيما لا ينظره الرجال كالقرن والرتق ونحوه اختلفت فيه الروايات وآخر ما روي عن محمد إن كان ذلك قبل القبض وهو عيب لا يحدث يرد بشهادة النساء وهو قول أبي يوسف الأخير، والحبل يثبت بقول النساء في حق الخصومة ولا يرد بشهادتين اهـ. وكأنه احترز بقوله لا يحدث عن نحو الحبل، وبه علم أن ما مر عن الخلاصة وغيرها من عدم الفسخ قبل القبض قول أبي يوسف الأول والعمل على المتأخر. وعلى هذا فقول المؤلف ردت عليه بقولهما محمول أيضاً على ما قبل القبض بدليل ما في شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان حيث قال : إن كان بعد القبض لا يرد بشهادة النساء بالاتفاق لكن يخلف البائع، فإن حلف لا يرد، وإن نكل ترد عليه بنكوله، وإن كان قبل القبض ذكر الخصاف أن على قول أبي يوسف ترد من غير يمين البائع . وقال محمد: لا ترد حتى يحلف البائع. وعن محمد في النوادر ؛ شهادة النساء فيما لا يطلع عليه الرجال حجة للرد وإن كان بعد القبض .اهـ. وفي مجموعة صمتي أفندي عن نقد الفتاوى : ما لا ينظر إليه الرجال كالقرن والرتق إذا أخبرت امرأة واحدة به يثبت العيب في حق الخصومة لا في الرد في ظاهر الرواية اهـ. ومثله في الخانية قوله : (ولكن في أدب القاضي ما يخالفه) قال في البزازية وفي المقبوض للقابض ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة فقبض أحدهما ووجد بأحدهما عيباً أخذهما أو أن ما يخالفه وفيها : لو أخبرت امرأة بأنها حامل وامرأتان بالعدم صحت الخصومة ولا يقبل قول النافية، فإن قال البائع ليست لها بصارة اختار القاضي ذات بصارة اهـ. وقدمنا أن للبائع يمتنع من القبول مع علمه بالعيب حتى يقضى عليه ليتعدى إلى بائعه، وقد صرح به في البزازية أيضاً. وفي تهذيب القلانسي: لو أقام البائع بينة أنه حدث عند المشتري وأقام المشتري البينة أنه كان معيباً في يد البائع تقبل بينة المشتري اهـ
Then know that the judge only needs the statement of physicians when he himself is unaware of the defect. But if he is among those with knowledge, he looks by himself as in al-Bazzaziyya. And the view of the judge's trustee is like his own as in al-Bada'i'. And the condition of two just [witnesses] among them is only for the return. If one just person informs, litigation is directed and the seller swears as in it as well. However, in Adab al-Qadi, one woman suffices not to establish the defect and return by it, but to direct litigation against the seller. Or it is interpreted as before delivery, as indicated in al-Khaniyya where he said: As for what men cannot see, like qarn (a vaginal defect) and ratq (closure of vagina) and similar, the narrations differ. The latest from Muhammad: If that is before delivery and it is a defect that does not arise, it is returned by the testimony of women, and that is the later ruling of Abu Yusuf. Pregnancy is established by the word of women for the purpose of litigation, but not returned by two testimonies. End. And it seems he avoided by his statement 'does not arise' things like pregnancy. And from this it is known that what passed from al-Khulasa and others about no rescission before delivery is the earlier ruling of Abu Yusuf, and action is on the later. On this basis, the author's statement 'rebutted by their statement' is also interpreted as before delivery, evidence from Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Qadikhan where he said: If after delivery, it is not returned by the testimony of women by agreement, but the seller is given the oath; if he swears, no return; if he refuses, it is returned due to his refusal. And if before delivery, al-Khassaf mentioned that according to Abu Yusuf it is returned without the seller's oath. And Muhammad said: It is not returned until the seller swears. And from Muhammad in al-Nawadir: The testimony of women for what men cannot see is proof for return even if after delivery. End. And in Majmu'a Samti Afandi from Naqd al-Fatawa: What men cannot see like qarn and ratq, if one woman informs of it, the defect is established in terms of litigation not in terms of return in the apparent narration. End. And similar in al-Khaniyya his statement: (But in Adab al-Qadi what contradicts it) He said in al-Bazzaziyya: And in al-Maqbud li'l-qabid, and if he bought two slaves in one transaction, took possession of one and found a defect in one, he takes both or... what contradicts it. And in it: If a woman informs that she is pregnant and two women inform that she is not, litigation is valid and the statement of the negators is not accepted. If the seller says she has no insight, the judge chooses one with insight. End. And we have stated that the seller may refuse acceptance while knowing the defect until judgment is passed against him so that it may extend to his own seller. And it is explicitly stated in al-Bazzaziyya as well. And in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: If the seller brings proof that it occurred at the buyer's, and the buyer brings proof that it was defective in the seller's hand, the buyer's proof is accepted. End.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This is a detailed discussion on the evidentiary value of women's testimony for hidden defects (e.g., qarn, ratq) in khiyar al-'ayb. The passage distinguishes between pre-delivery and post-delivery scenarios. The general Hanafi rule is that for defects not visible to men, the testimony of one woman is sufficient to establish the right to litigate (tawajjuh al-khusumah) but not to actually return the item. For return, two just witnesses (male or female?) are required. However, there are multiple narrations: The latest from Imam Muhammad states that for non-arising defects before delivery, return is allowed by women's testimony (following Abu Yusuf's later view). After delivery, the majority opinion is that return is not allowed by women's testimony alone, but the seller is given the oath. If he refuses the oath, the item is returned. There is also a narration from Muhammad in al-Nawadir that women's testimony is proof for return even after delivery. The passage also clarifies that pregnancy is established by women's testimony for litigation but not for return. Finally, if both parties bring evidence (seller claims defect arose at buyer's, buyer claims it existed at seller's), the buyer's proof is preferred. This reflects the Hanafi inclination to protect the buyer's rights when ambiguity exists.

Key Terms
قرن (qarn): A vaginal defect where there is a bone or flesh obstruction, visible only to women.
رتق (ratq): A defect where the vaginal opening is closed, preventing intercourse, only detectable by women.
توجه الخصومة (tawajjuh al-khusumah): The initiation of litigation against the seller, giving the right to sue but not necessarily to return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later view of Abu Yusuf, if a buyer discovers a hidden defect (qarn) before delivery and only one woman testifies, what happens?
A) The buyer can return the item based on her testimony.
B) The seller swears and the item is not returned.
C) The defect is established for litigation but not for return.
D) The testimony is rejected entirely.

Key Takeaway
For hidden defects not visible to men, one woman's testimony initiates litigation, but return generally requires two witnesses; however, before delivery, later Hanafi authorities allow return on one woman's word.

The Principle That the Recipient's Statement is Accepted (Al-Qawl Li'l-Qabid)
Explains the maxim and its exceptions in khiyar cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of al-qawl li'l-qabid, distinguish its application in khiyar al-shart versus khiyar al-'ayb, and identify when mutual oaths (tahāluf) are required.

Core Idea
The recipient's word is taken about what he received, except when returning for defect.

In sale disputes, determining whose statement prevails is critical. The Hanafi school establishes a default: the recipient (qābiḍ) is believed about the quantity, quality, and identity of what he received. However, exceptions arise, especially when the buyer returns goods under an option. This lesson unpacks the principle and its nuances, including the intra-Hanafi debate on mutual oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its scope
State the maxim 'al-qawl fi qadr al-maqbud li'l-qabid' and note that it applies to any recipient (amin or dhamin).
The principle is absolute in the text but context limits it to khiyar al-'ayb initially.
2
Apply to defect scenario
If buyer finds defect and seller claims he sold more, the buyer's statement is taken.
This is the straightforward application.
3
Distinguish options of condition vs. defect
When returning under khiyar al-shart or khiyar al-ru'ya, the buyer's identification is accepted; under khiyar al-'ayb, the seller's identification is accepted.
Reason: in defect, the buyer is claimant; in condition/seeing, the buyer is defending his possession.
4
Examine mutual oaths for price disputes
If seller and buyer differ on price type, amount, or quality, they take mutual oaths. Before possession it is qiyas; after possession it is contrary to qiyas (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) or in line with qiyas (Muhammad).
The majority practice accepts mutual oaths after possession despite being contrary to qiyas.
5
Handle disputes about description
If they differ on the sold item's description (e.g., scribe or baker), the seller's word is taken without mutual oaths.
This is because the buyer's claim is an addition to the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والقول في قدر المقبوض للقابض) لأنه هو المنكر لما يدعيه المدعي أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان أميناً أو ضميناً كالغاصب وإن كان المقام مخصصاً لما يتعلق بالعيب فلو اشترى جارية وتسلمها ثم وجد بها عيباً فقال البائع بعتكها وأخرى معها وقال المشتري وحدها فالقول للمشتري. ولو حذف المنصف قوله في مقدار المقبوض لكان أولى لأن القول للقابض فيما قبضه مطلقاً مقداراً أو صفة أو تعييناً، فلو جاء ليرد المبيع بخيار شرط أو رؤية فقال البائع ليس هو المبيع فالقول للمشتري في تعيينه بخلاف ما إذا جاء ليرده بخيار عيب فإن القول للبائع كما في العمادية، وفرق بينهما في فتح القدير وإذا اختلفا في تعيين الزق فالقول للمشتري كما في الظهيرية، وإذا اشترى عبدين أحدهما بألف حالة والآخر بألف إلى سنة صفقة أو صفقتين فوجد بأحدهما عيباً فرده ثم اختلفا فقال البائع رددت ما ثمنه أجل وقال المشتري ما كان ثمنه عاجلاً فالقول للبائع، سواء هلك ما في يد المشتري أو لا ولا تحالف ولو كان الثمنان مختلفين فرد أحدهما أدب القاضي : الذي يرجع فيه إلى الأطباء لا يثبت في حق توجه الخصومة ما لم يتفق عدلان بخلاف ما لا يطلع عليه الرجال حيث يثبت بقول المرأة الواحدة في حق الخصومة لا في حق الرد. قوله : (لأن القول للقابض فيما قبضه مطلقاً الخ البائع والمشتري إذا اختلفا في جنس الثمن أنه دراهم أو دنانير أو في قدره أنه ألف أو ألفان أو في صفته أنه صحاح أو جياد أو زيوف مكسرة والسلعة قائمة بعينها فإنهما يتحالفان؛ إن اختلفا قبل قبض المشتري فالتحالف على وفاق القياس، وإن بعد القبض فالتحالف على خلاف القياس، فالقياس أن لا يحلف البائع وهو قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، فأما على قول محمد فالتحالف بعد القبض على وفاق القياس وبه أخذ بشر بن غياث والكرخي. وإذا وقع الاختلاف في المبيع فالتحالف قبل نقد الثمن على وفاق القياس عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ؛ كذا في الظهيرية . ثم ذكر كيفية التحالف ثم قال : وإن اختلفا في وصف من أوصاف المبيع فقال المشتري اشتريت منك هذا العبد على أنه كاتب أو على أنه خباز وقال البائع لم اشترط شيئاً فالقول قول البائع ولا يتحالفان .اهـ. وسنذكر هنا أيضاً ما إذا اختلفا في طوله وعرضه فتأمل ذلك مع ما ذكره هنا . قوله : (بخلاف ما إذا جاء ليرده بخيار عيب الخ) قال الرملي قال في جامع الفصولين أقول : الأصل أن القول في التعيين للمملك حتى لو أراد رده بعيب فقال ليس المبيع هذا يصدق البائع مع يمينه، فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يكون القول للبائع في مسألة خيار الشرط أيضاً، والأصل الآخر أن القول للقابض في قدر المقبوض وتعيينه وصفته فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يكون القول للمشتري في مسألة خيار
His statement: 'And the say regarding the measure of what is received is for the recipient' because he is the denier of what the claimant claims. It is absolute, so it includes whether he is an amin or a dhamin like a usurper, even though the context is specific to what relates to the defect. For if he bought a slave-girl and took possession then found a defect, and the seller said 'I sold you her and another with her' and the buyer said 'alone', then the say is the buyer's. If the editor had omitted his statement 'in the measure of what is received' it would have been better because the say is for the recipient regarding what he received absolutely in measure, description, or identification. So if he comes to return the sold item under an option of condition or seeing, and the seller says 'that is not the sold item', then the say is the buyer's in identification, unlike if he comes to return it under an option of defect, for then the say is the seller's as in al-'Imadiyya. And a distinction is made between them in Fath al-Qadir. And if they differ about the identification of the vessel, the say is the buyer's as in al-Zahira. And if he bought two slaves, one for a thousand cash and the other for a thousand on credit, in one transaction or two, and he found a defect in one and returned it, then they differed: the seller said 'you returned the one whose price was deferred' and the buyer said 'it was the one whose price was cash', then the say is the seller's, whether what was in the buyer's hand perished or not, and no mutual oath. And if the two prices were different and he returned one... Adab al-Qadi: what is referred back to physicians is not established in terms of directing litigation unless two just [witnesses] agree, unlike what men cannot see, where it is established by the word of one woman for litigation, not for return. His statement: 'Because the say is for the recipient regarding what he received absolutely...' The seller and buyer, if they differ about the type of the price whether it is dirhams or dinars, or its amount whether a thousand or two thousand, or its quality whether sound or good or counterfeit broken, and the goods are still in existence, then they both make oath. If they differ before the buyer's possession, the mutual oath is in accordance with analogy; if after possession, the mutual oath is contrary to analogy. So analogy is that the seller does not take an oath, which is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. As for according to Muhammad, mutual oath after possession is in accordance with analogy, and Bishr ibn Ghiyath and al-Karkhi adopted that. And if the difference is about the sold item, mutual oath before payment is in accordance with analogy according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; thus in al-Zahira. Then he mentioned the manner of mutual oath, then said: And if they differ about a description among the descriptions of the sold item, and the buyer says 'I bought this slave from you on condition that he is a scribe or a baker' and the seller said 'I did not stipulate anything', then the say is the seller's and they do not take mutual oath. End. And we will also mention here what if they differ about its length and width, so consider that with what he mentioned here. His statement: 'Unlike if he comes to return it under option of defect...' Al-Ramli said: In Jami' al-Fusulayn, I say: The principle is that the say about identification is for the owner [the seller], so even if he wants to return it due to a defect and says 'this is not the item sold', the seller is believed with his oath. On this basis, the say should be for the seller in the matter of option of condition as well. And the other principle is that the say is for the recipient regarding the measure, identification, and description of what he received. On this basis, the say should be for the buyer in the matter of option...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-qawl fi qadr al-maqbud li'l-qabid' (the say regarding the amount of what is received is for the recipient). This applies broadly to any custodian, including a trustee (amin) or even a usurper (ghasib), but here it is applied in the context of khiyar al-'ayb. The buyer (qabid) is the defendant when the seller claims he received more, so his statement is taken. The text distinguishes between different scenarios: when returning under option of condition or seeing, the buyer's identification is accepted; but when returning under option of defect, the seller's identification is accepted (since the buyer is the claimant). A detailed discussion follows on mutual oaths (tahlif) when the parties differ about the price (type, amount, quality). The preferred view is that after possession, mutual oaths are contrary to analogy (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) but allowed. Muhammad says it is in line with analogy. For disputes about the object's description, the seller's statement is accepted and no mutual oath is required. The passage ends with a quote from al-Ramli explaining the two competing principles: one says the seller has the say in identification (since he is the original owner), the other says the recipient has the say in what he received. The application to option of defect vs. option of condition is nuanced.

Key Terms
القول للقابض (al-qawl li'l-qabid): The legal principle that the statement of the person who has taken possession is accepted regarding the quantity and quality of what he received.
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by both the seller and buyer when they disagree about the price or the object after a sale.
خيار شرط (khiyar al-shart): Option of condition: a right to rescind the sale within a specified period agreed upon in the contract.
خيار عيب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the buyer's right to return goods for a hidden defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer returns an item under option of condition (khiyar al-shart). The seller says 'that is not the item I sold'. Whose identification is accepted?
A) Buyer's, because he is the recipient.
B) Seller's, because he is the owner.
C) Both take mutual oaths.
D) The judge decides based on evidence.

Key Takeaway
The recipient's statement is accepted about what he received, but when returning for defect, the seller's identification prevails because the buyer is the claimant.

The Rule of the Receiver's Say: Al-Qawl li-l-Qabid
Explanation of a procedural rule and its exceptions in sales law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of al-qawl li-l-qabid, identify its exception in khiyar al-'ayb, and distinguish between khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-'ayb in identification disputes.

Core Idea
In disputes over received goods, the buyer's word is taken, except when returning a defective item—then the seller identifies it.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains a foundational procedural rule in Hanafi sales law: when buyer and seller quarrel over the quantity or identity of goods already in the buyer's hands, the buyer's statement is decisive. The reasoning is procedural—the buyer is the defendant, and the seller is the claimant. However, an important carve-out appears when the buyer seeks to return the item due to a defect (khiyar al-'ayb): here the seller's identification of the item is accepted. This lesson unpacks the rule, its rationale, its exceptions, and its application in multiple scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the statement: 'And the saying (the decisive statement) regarding the quantity of the received item is for the receiver.' Understand that this is a procedural principle for dispute resolution before a qadi.
The rule applies whether the buyer is a trustee or a guarantor (like a usurper).
2
Understand the procedural basis
The rule's rationale: the receiver is the defendant (muddai'alayh) because the seller claims more than what the buyer admits. The defendant's denial with an oath is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
This is based on the maxim 'al-bayyina 'ala man idda'a wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara'.
3
Note the exception in khiyar al-'ayb
When the buyer seeks to return the item due to a defect, and the seller disputes that this is the sold item, the seller's word is taken. The reason: the defect option does not retroactively nullify ownership until exercised, so the seller remains the owner and his identification prevails.
Contrast with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection), where the buyer's identification is accepted.
4
Distinguish the two scenarios
The commentator in Fath al-Qadir differentiates: in khiyar al-shart, the contract is suspended, so the buyer's possession is more authoritative. In khiyar al-'ayb, ownership remains with seller until rescission, so seller identifies.
This distinction is crucial for applying the rule correctly.
5
Apply to practical scenarios
Work through the examples in the text: (a) dispute over a jar's identity → buyer's word; (b) two slaves with different payment terms, one returned defective → seller's word on which slave; (c) dispute over price of the returned item → buyer's word if prices differ.
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Muhammad requires mutual oath in price disputes after possession; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول في قدر المقبوض للقابض لأنه هو المنكر لما يدعيه المدعي أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان أميناً أو ضميناً كالغاصب وإن كان المقام مخصصاً لما يتعلق بالعيب فلو اشترى جارية وتسلمها ثم وجد بها عيباً فقال البائع بعتكها وأخرى معها وقال المشتري وحدها فالقول للمشتري. ولو حذف المنصف قوله في مقدار المقبوض لكان أولى لأن القول للقابض فيما قبضه مطلقاً مقداراً أو صفة أو تعييناً، فلو جاء ليرد المبيع بخيار شرط أو رؤية فقال البائع ليس هو المبيع فالقول للمشتري في تعيينه بخلاف ما إذا جاء ليرده بخيار عيب فإن القول للبائع كما في العمادية، وفرق بينهما في فتح القدير وإذا اختلفا في تعيين الزق فالقول للمشتري كما في الظهيرية، وإذا اشترى عبدين أحدهما بألف حالة والآخر بألف إلى سنة صفقة أو صفقتين فوجد بأحدهما عيباً فرده ثم اختلفا فقال البائع رددت ما ثمنه أجل وقال المشتري ما كان ثمنه عاجلاً فالقول للبائع، سواء هلك ما في يد المشتري أو لا ولا تحالف ولو كان الثمنان مختلفين فرد أحدهما بعيب فادعى البائع أن ثمن المردود كذا وعكس المشتري فالقول للمشتري؛ كذا في الظهيرية .
And the saying (the decisive statement) regarding the quantity of the received item is for the receiver, because he is the denier of what the claimant claims. This is general, so it includes whether he is a trustee or a guarantor like a usurper, even though the context is specific to what relates to defects. If he bought a slave girl and took possession, then found a defect, and the seller said: 'I sold her to you with another,' and the buyer said: 'Alone,' then the saying is for the buyer. And if the editor had omitted his saying 'regarding the quantity of the received item' it would be better because the saying is for the receiver regarding what he received absolutely—whether quantity, description, or identification. So if he comes to return the sold item due to an option of stipulation or option of inspection, and the seller says: 'This is not the sold item,' then the saying is for the buyer in its identification, unlike if he comes to return it due to an option of defect, then the saying is for the seller, as in the 'Imadiyya. And they distinguished between them in Fath al-Qadir. And if they differ regarding the identification of the jar, then the saying is for the buyer as in al-Zahiriya. And if he bought two slaves, one for a thousand cash and the other for a thousand on credit, in one lot or two lots, and he found a defect in one and returned it, then they differ: the seller says 'you returned the one whose price was deferred' and the buyer says 'it was the one whose price was prompt,' then the saying is for the seller, whether what is in the buyer's hand perished or not, and no mutual oath. And if the two prices were different and he returned one due to a defect, and the seller claimed that the price of the returned item was such, and the buyer the opposite, then the saying is for the buyer; thus in al-Zahiriya.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining a fundamental principle in sales law: when a buyer and seller dispute the quantity or description of goods already received by the buyer, the buyer's word is taken. The reason is procedural: the buyer is the defendant (muddai'alayh) since the seller is claiming something beyond what the buyer admits. The Hanafi jurists extend this rule to all cases where there is a dispute over the received good, whether regarding its quantity, quality, or identity. However, a key exception arises in khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect): if the buyer seeks to return the item due to a defect and the seller disputes that this is the sold item, the seller's word is accepted. This differs from khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) and khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection), where the buyer's identification prevails. The distinction lies in the nature of the defect: the buyer's right to rescind due to defect does not nullify ownership in the meantime, so the seller remains the owner who can identify his property. In contrast, khiyar al-shart suspends the contract, so the buyer's possession is more authoritative. The passage also covers various scenarios: disputes over the price (cash vs. credit) when returning a defective slave, and the rule that no mutual oath is required if the other item perishes. The authority of this ruling is from the core Hanafi works (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms
القول في قدر المقبوض للقابض (al-qawl fī qadr al-maqbūḍ li-l-qābiḍ): A procedural legal principle meaning that in disputes about the quantity or identity of goods already received by the buyer, the buyer's statement is taken as true over the seller's.
خيار العيب (khiyār al-ʿayb): The option to rescind a sale upon discovering a pre-existing defect in the purchased item, provided the defect was not known at the time of contract.
تعيين (taʿyīn): Identification of which specific item among multiple is the subject of the dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over the identity of a defective item being returned, who has the decisive word in the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer, because he is the receiver.
B) The seller, because the right to rescind does not nullify ownership.
C) Both parties take an oath.
D) The qadi decides based on evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that the receiver's word is taken regarding received goods has a critical exception in khiyar al-'ayb, where the seller identifies the item due to ownership not being suspended.

Who Has the Say in Sales Disputes over Defect and Price
Rulings on which party's word (al-qawl) is accepted in three different sales disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of al-qawl (whose statement is accepted) in three different sales disputes: price disagreement, identity of the sold item, and dimensions of the item.

Core Idea
The say (al-qawl) in sales disputes depends on possession and knowledge: the buyer's word on price, the seller's on identity and dimensions.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents several rulings on who has the burden of proof in sales disputes. Understanding these nuances is critical for anyone involved in sales litigation. The general principle is that the word is given to the denier (munkir), but specific contexts modify it. We'll explore three distinct cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general context
All disputes arise after a sale with a defect (khiyar al-'ayb). The buyer claims a defect and wants to return, but a disagreement occurs about the price or the identity of the item.
2
First case: price dispute
If the seller claims the price of the returned item is a certain amount and the buyer claims a different amount, the buyer's word is taken. This is from al-Zahiriyya.
The buyer is the one who possesses the item and is the denier of the seller's price claim.
3
Second case: identity dispute (two slaves)
When one slave was bought, another was gifted, and one dies, the dispute is over which slave was the sold one. The seller's word is taken.
The seller is more knowledgeable about the identity of the sold item.
4
Consequence of the second case: mutual oath (tahāluf) and compensation
If the seller retracts the gift, the buyer gets back his price, and the seller gets the value of the dead slave after mutual oaths.
Tahāluf occurs when both parties swear, leading to rescission.
5
Third case: dimension dispute
If they differ on the length or width of the sold item, the seller's word is taken.
The seller has better knowledge of the item's specifications at the time of sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع/ باب خيار العيب
بربح بعيب فادعى البائع أن ثمن المردود كذا وعكس المشتري فالقول للمشتري؛ كذا في الظهيرية . ومن مسائل الجامع الكبير : لو اشترى عبداً بألف وقبضه ووهب البائع له عبداً آخر وسلمه فمات أحد العبدين ثم أراد المشتري رد الباقي بعيب فادعى البائع أن المبيع هو الهالك والباقي هو الهبة وعكس المشتري ولا بينة فالقول للبائع ولو لم يجد عيباً وإنما أراد الواهب الرجوع وقال الحي هو الموهوب وأنكر المشتري فالقول للبائع، فإذا رجع فيه رجع المشتري بالثمن المدفوع، إذا رجع رجع البائع بقيمة العبد الميت بعد التحالف، وإذا اختلفا في طول المبيع وعرضه فالقول للبائع وتمامه في الظهيرية من فصل الاختلافات من البيوع.
The book of sales / Chapter of the option of defect. With profit and defect, if the seller claims that the price of the returned item is such-and-such and the buyer claims the opposite, the say is the buyer's; as in al-Zahiriyya. And from the issues of al-Jami' al-Kabir: If he bought a slave for a thousand and took possession, and the seller gifted him another slave and handed it over, then one of the two slaves died, then the buyer wanted to return the remaining one due to a defect, and the seller claimed that the sold one is the dead one and the surviving one is the gift, and the buyer claims the opposite, and there is no evidence, then the say is the seller's. And if he did not find a defect but only wanted the giver to retract [the gift], and he said the living one is the gift and the buyer denied, then the say is the seller's. Then if he goes back in it, the buyer takes back the paid price. If he goes back, the seller takes back the value of the dead slave after mutual oath (taḥāluf). And if they differ in the length and width of the sold item, then the say is the seller's, and its completion is in al-Zahiriyya from the section of disputes from sales.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings from the Hanafi school regarding who has the say (al-qawl) in disputes that arise in sales with the option of defect. The first ruling: when the buyer returns an item due to a defect and claims that the seller demanded a different price for the returned item, the buyer's word is taken. This is from al-Zahiriyya. Then from al-Jami' al-Kabir: a complex case where one slave was bought and another was gifted, then one dies. When the buyer wants to return the remaining slave due to defect, and the seller claims the sold slave is the dead one, the seller's word is accepted. Similarly, if the buyer wants to return the gift due to a claim of defect but the gift-giver retracts, the seller's word stands. After returning, the buyer gets back the price paid, and the seller gets compensation for the dead slave after mutual oaths. Finally, a dispute over dimensions: the seller's word is taken. All these rules are about evidence: who has the burden of proof? In Hanafi fiqh, the rule is that the word is given to the one who has possession or the one who denies the claim (munkir). Here, in the first case, the buyer is the possessor, so his word is accepted. In the second case, the seller is the one who knows the identity of the sold item better, so his word is accepted. The mention of 'taḥāluf' (mutual oath) indicates that if both parties swear, then the contract is rescinded and compensation is calculated.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl): The credence given to a party's statement in a judicial dispute; the party whose statement is accepted as the basis for judgment.
التحالف (al-taḥāluf): Mutual oath: when both parties swear, the sale is rescinded and compensation is based on value.
بربح بعيب (bi-ribḥin bi-ʿayb): A sale where the seller states the cost and adds a profit, and then a defect is discovered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over the length and width of a sold item, whose word is accepted according to this passage?
A) The buyer's word
B) The seller's word
C) The word of an expert
D) The word of the witness

Key Takeaway
The say (al-qawl) in sales disputes is not always the buyer's; it depends on the nature of the disagreement: buyer says on price, seller says on identity and dimensions.

Murabaha Dispute: Profit Division and Defect
A specific murabaha case with two cloths and defect, ruling on whose word is accepted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is accepted when a buyer claims a defect in a murabaha lot and the seller disputes the profit division.

Core Idea
In a murabaha lot with a defect, the buyer's word is accepted with an oath denying a new increase.

This passage from Talkhīs al-Jāmiʿ presents a nuanced dispute in a murabaha transaction where two cloths are sold as one lot. After discovering a defect, the buyer and seller disagree on how to divide the profit. The ruling provides a clear legal principle: the buyer's claim is given weight when he denies a new increase that would alter the profit division. Understanding this ruling helps in resolving similar disputes in cost-plus sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Transaction Structure
Note that the buyer purchased two cloths as a single lot (ṣafqa). One cloth was his own, the other was given to him on commission. The seller claims each had a cost of 10, while the buyer claims the defective cloth had a lower value.
The concept of ṣafqa affects return options.
2
Determine the Point of Dispute
The buyer wants to return the defective cloth and receive a refund proportional to value (thirds of total price), while the seller insists on cost-based division (halves).
3
Apply the Ruling on Whose Word Is Accepted
The buyer's word is taken with an oath denying a new increase (mazīd ḥādith) in the defective cloth's value after sale.
The buyer swears that no such increase occurred.
4
Examine the Exception
If the buyer did not claim a defect, there is no benefit in disputing, so the ruling differs.
Without a defect, the buyer has no standing to contest the profit division.
5
Note the Evidence Rule
If both parties present evidence, the buyer's proof is preferred because he establishes a real intended increase (the defect).
No mutual oath (taḥāluf) is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي تلخيص الجامع من باب الاختلاف في المرابحة : اشترى ثوباً قيمته عشرة بعشرة ودفع إليه آخر ثوباً اشتراه بعشرة وقيمته عشرون ليبيع له مع ثوبه فقال لرجل هما قاما بعشرين فأبيعك عشرة فاشتراهما ثم وجد بثوب الأمر عيباً فقال شريتهما صفقة وانقسم الربح على القيمة أثلاثاً فأرده بثلثي الثمن فقال البائع ثمن كل ثوب عشرة فانقسم الربح على الثمنين فرد بنصفه فالقول للمشتري مع اليمين بجحده مزيد حادث بخلاف ما لم يدع عيباً لفقد الجدوى إلى أن قال : ولا تحالف وإن برهنا فالبينة للمشتري لإثباته زيادة حقيقة مقصودة وتمامه فيه.
And in Talkhīs al-Jāmiʿ from the chapter of dispute in murābaḥa: He bought a cloth worth ten for ten, and another person gave him a cloth he had bought for ten but worth twenty to sell together with his cloth. He said to a man: 'Both cost twenty, so I sell you for ten.' He bought both, then found a defect in the first cloth. He said: 'I bought them as one lot, and the profit is divided on the value in thirds, so I return it for two-thirds of the price.' The seller said: 'The price of each cloth is ten, so the profit is divided on the two prices, so he returns half.' The say is the buyer's with the oath denying a new increase, unlike if he did not claim a defect because there is no benefit. Up to he said: 'There is no mutual oath, and if they both provide evidence, the buyer's proof is accepted because he is establishing a real intended increase.' And its completion is in it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage from Talkhīs al-Jāmiʿ presents a detailed case in murābaḥa (cost-plus sale). The buyer purchased two cloths as one lot, one of which was his own and the other was given to him to sell on commission. After finding a defect in the first cloth, the buyer claimed the profit should be divided based on value (thirds), while the seller said it should be based on cost (halves). The ruling is that the buyer's word is accepted with an oath, because he is denying a new increase (mazīd ḥādith) in the defective cloth that would affect the profit calculation. This is different from a case without a defect, where there is no benefit in dispute. If both parties produce evidence, the buyer's evidence is preferred because he is proving a real increase. Note: 'mazīd ḥādith' refers to an alleged increase in value after the sale, which the buyer denies. The rule highlights that in defect cases, the buyer's claim about profit division is given weight due to the practical need to achieve justice. The passage also clarifies that mutual oath (taḥāluf) is not applied here; instead, evidence is considered.

Key Terms
المرابحة (al-murābaḥa): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit margin.
صفقة (ṣafqa): A single transaction involving multiple items sold together as one lot.
مزيد حادث (mazīd ḥādith): A new increase in value that occurred after the sale, claimed by the seller but denied by the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the murābaḥa case, what does the buyer swear an oath about?
A) That the defect existed at the time of sale
B) That he did not cause the defect
C) That there was no new increase (mazīd ḥādith) in the defective cloth
D) That the profit is divided on value

Key Takeaway
In a murabaha lot with a defect, the buyer's word is accepted with an oath denying a new increase, and if evidence is provided, the buyer's proof prevails.

Khiyar al-Ta'yin: The Say of the Seller and Buyer
Hanafi ruling on evidence in khiyar al-ta'yin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for who has the say in khiyar al-ta'yin disputes and apply it to scenarios where the buyer or seller denies a specific sale.

Core Idea
In khiyar al-ta'yin, the one who denies the specific sale has the say, regardless of possession.

In khiyar al-ta'yin, the buyer or seller has the right to select among multiple items. Disputes can arise about which item was sold. This lesson explains the Hanafi evidentiary rule: the say belongs to the one who denies the sale of a specific item, as stated in al-Zahiriyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Is the dispute about the buyer ratifying a sale of a specific item in the seller's hand, or the seller enforcing a sale of a specific item against the buyer's denial?
The two scenarios have opposite outcomes.
2
Apply the rule for buyer's ratification
If the buyer claims ratification and the seller denies the specific sale, the seller's word is taken because he is the denier (munkir).
Burden of proof on the claimant.
3
Apply the rule for seller's enforcement
If the seller has the option and wants to enforce a specific sale, but the buyer denies, the buyer's word is taken.
Again, the denier benefits from the presumption.
4
Contrast with the general principle
Normally, the say is for the possessor (al-qabid), but in khiyar al-ta'yin before selection, ownership is not complete, so the denier's rule prevails over possession.
This is an exception noted in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد بكونه مقبوضاً لأن المشتري بالخيار إذا أراد الإجازة في سلعة في يد البائع فقال البائع ما بعتكها قالوا : القول للبائع كما لو ادعى بيع عين وأنكر وإن كان الخيار للبائع فأراد الزام البيع في معين وأنكره المشتري فالقول للمشتري؛ كذا في الظهيرية من خيار التعيين. وشمل ما إذا ادعى المشتري بعد قبض المبيع أنه وجده ناقصاً فالقول له لأنه القابض. قال في الخلاصة من كتاب الصلح : رجل باع من آخر إبريسماً ووزنه عليه وقت البيع وحمله المشتري ثم رجع إليه بعد مدة وقال وجدته ناقصاً، فإن كان النقص يكون بين الوزنين فلا شيء له، وإن كان أكثر يمنع العيب كما في خيار الشرط. والحاصل أن خيار الشرط وخيار العيب ينبغي أن يتحدا في هذا الحكم اهـ. قال الشارح المؤلف في حواشيه على جامع الفصولين أقول : إن الأصل أن القول للقابض كما ذكره إلا في التعيين فإن القول للمالك ملكاً تاماً ففي العيب يثبت الملك التام لأن خيار العيب لا الملك ولا تمامه وإنما يمنع لزومه، وأما خيار الشرط فلأنه مانع يمنع تمام الحكم فكان على الأصل من أن القول للقابض وقد اشتبه ذلك على المؤلف فخبط ولم يفرق فليتأمل. وقد فرق في فتح القدير في آخر خيار الرؤية بفرق حسن وهو أن المشتري في خيار الشرط والرؤية ينفسخ العقد بفسخه بلا توقف على رضا الآخر بل على علمه على الخلاف، وإذا انفسخ يكون الاختلاف بعد ذلك اختلافاً في المقبوض فالقول فيه قول القابض بخلاف الفسخ بالعيب لا ينفرد المشتري بفسخه ولكنه يدعي ثبوت حق الفسخ في الذي أحضره والبائع ينكره .اهـ.
Qualified by its being possessed because the buyer has the option: if he wants to ratify the sale of a commodity in the seller's hand and the seller says 'I did not sell it to you,' they said: the say is the seller's, just as if he claimed a sale of an item and the other denied. And if the option is the seller's and he wants to enforce the sale of a specific item and the buyer denies it, then the say is the buyer's; as in al-Zahiriyya from the option of selection. And it includes if the buyer after taking possession of the sold item claims he found it deficient, then the say is his because he is the possessor. He said in al-Khulāṣa from the book of Ṣulḥ: A man sold to another silk and weighed it for him at the time of sale, and the buyer carried it, then after a while he returned and said 'I found it deficient.' If the deficiency is within the difference between the two weighings, then nothing is due; if it is more, it prevents the defect as in the option of condition. The conclusion is that the option of condition and the option of defect should be united in this ruling. The commentator author said in his supercommentary on Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn: I say that the principle is that the say is for the possessor as he mentioned, except in selection, then the say is for the owner with complete ownership. So in defect, complete ownership is established because the option of defect does not negate ownership nor its completion; rather, it prevents its binding. As for the option of condition, it is a preventive that prevents the completion of the ruling, so it remains on the principle that the say is for the possessor. This confused the author [of Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn] and he mixed things up and did not differentiate, so let him ponder. And in Fatḥ al-Qadīr at the end of the option of sight, a good distinction was made: that the buyer in the option of condition and sight cancels the contract by his cancellation without waiting for the other's consent, rather based on his knowledge, according to the difference. And when it is cancelled, the dispute after that becomes a dispute about the possessed item, so the say is the possessor's, unlike cancellation by defect: the buyer does not independently cancel, but rather claims the right of cancellation in the item he brought, and the seller denies it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule of al-qawl (who has the say) in two contexts: khiyar al-ta'yin (option of selection) and deficiency claims after possession. In khiyar al-ta'yin, if the buyer wants to ratify a sale of a specific item in the seller's hand and the seller denies making that sale, the seller's word is accepted (he is the denier). Conversely, if the seller has the option and wants to enforce a specific item, the buyer's word is accepted. This is from al-Zahiriyya. Then the passage extends to a case where the buyer claims a deficiency after possession: the buyer's word is accepted because he is the possessor. A similar ruling from al-Khulāṣa is cited about silk: if the deficiency is within the normal margin of weight difference, no claim is sustained; if more, it can prevent the defect claim, analogous to khiyar al-shart. The commentator then addresses a confusion in Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn regarding the consistent rule. He explains that in khiyar al-'ayb (defect option), the buyer's word is taken because he is possessor, while in khiyar al-shart, the same principle applies. However, in khiyar al-ta'yin, the owner (seller) has complete ownership until selection, so his word is taken. The distinction from Fatḥ al-Qadīr emphasizes that in khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya (sight option), the buyer can unilaterally cancel, making later disputes about the possessed item, so the possessor (buyer) has the say; in khiyar al-'ayb, the buyer cannot unilaterally cancel but must claim the right, so the seller's denial matters more.

Key Terms
خيار التعيين (khiyār al-taʿyīn): Option of selection: where the buyer or seller has the right to choose among multiple items within a specified time.
القابض (al-qābiḍ): The party in physical possession of the item.
المنكر (al-munkir): The party who denies a claim; in Hanafi evidentiary law, the denier's word is generally accepted with an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the buyer wants to ratify a sale in khiyar al-ta'yin and the seller denies, whose word is accepted?
A) The buyer's
B) The seller's
C) The witness's
D) The judge's

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ta'yin, the say is for the one who denies the specific sale, regardless of possession.

Deficiency Claims and the Principle of Al-Qawl lil-Qabid: Distinguishing Khiyar al-'Ayb and Khiyar al-Shart
Hanafi rulings on deficiency claims and distinctions among khiyar types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the say is for the possessor in deficiency claims and explain why khiyar al-'ayb differs from khiyar al-shart regarding unilateral cancellation.

Core Idea
After possession, the buyer's word on deficiency is taken; the key distinction is that khiyar al-shart allows unilateral cancellation, unlike khiyar al-'ayb.

After a buyer takes possession, if he claims a deficiency, his word is accepted because he is the possessor. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq also resolves a confusion about whether the same rule should apply to khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-'ayb. The distinction from Fath al-Qadir clarifies the role of unilateral cancellation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deficiency claim scenario
The buyer after possession claims the item is deficient (e.g., weight shortage). The say is his because he is the possessor (al-qabid).
This is the default rule.
2
Consider the silk example
If the deficiency is within the normal variance (e.g., between two weighings), no claim is allowed. If more, it is treated like khiyar al-shart.
Minor deficiencies are ignored.
3
Understand the confusion
The author of Jami' al-Fusulayn thought khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-'ayb should have the same rule. The commentator critiques this.
The confusion is about whether the possessor rule applies consistently.
4
Apply the distinction from Fath al-Qadir
In khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'ya, the buyer can unilaterally cancel the contract. After cancellation, the dispute is about the possessed item, so the possessor's word applies.
In khiyar al-'ayb, the buyer cannot unilaterally cancel; he claims a right to cancel, which the seller denies. Thus the dispute is about the right, not possession.
5
Conclude the correct rule
The say is for the possessor in deficiency claims. For khiyar al-shart, the same rule applies post-cancellation. For khiyar al-'ayb, the buyer's claim of a right to cancel is distinct.
The distinction preserves the logic of each option.

القابض (al-qābiḍ): The party in physical possession of the item.

خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ruʾya): Option of sight: the right to cancel if the item seen differs from description.

Key Takeaway

Returning One of Two Defective Items After Possession
Rulings on khiyar al-ayb when one of two items bought together has a defect after full possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning one of two items purchased together when a defect is found after possession, and identify the exceptions for functionally interdependent items.

Core Idea
After possession, only the defective item is returned—unless the items are functionally inseparable.

When you buy two items together and later discover a defect in one, can you return just that one? The Hanafi school has a detailed answer that depends on possession, timing, and the nature of the items. This lesson unpacks the general rule and its exceptions, helping you apply it correctly to real transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
After full possession of both items, if a defect appears in one, only the defective item is returned. This is called 'separation after completion' (tafrīq ba'd al-tamām).
Possession completes the sale for khiyar al-ayb.
2
Recognize the Exception
When items are functionally interdependent (e.g., a pair of socks, two leaves of a door, or yoked oxen), returning only the defective item is not allowed; both must be returned or kept.
The sound item is useless alone.
3
Distinguish Khiyar Types
This rule applies only to khiyar al-ayb (option of defect). Under khiyar al-shart or khiyar al-ru'ya, the sale is not completed until possession, so the rule differs.
Under those options, possession does not finalize the sale.
4
Consider Timing of Defect Discovery
If the defect is found before possession, and the buyer takes possession of the defective item, both items become binding. If the buyer takes possession of the sound item when both are defective, he may return both.
Acceptance of the defective item with knowledge binds the lot.
5
Apply to Examples
For yoked oxen: if one is defective, both must be returned. For independent items (e.g., two books), only the defective one is returned.
Interdependence is judged by common usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ردهما ولو قبضهما ثم وجد بأحدهما عيباً رد المعيب وحده لكونه تفريقاً بعد التمام لأن بالقبض تتم في خيار العيب وسيأتي أن مسألة زوجي الخف ومصراعي الباب مستثناة من هنا. وعلى هذا إذا اشترى ثورين فوجد بأحدهما عيباً بعد القبض، فإن كان ألف أحدهما الآخر بحيث لا يعمل بدونه لا يملك رد المعيب وحده. وقيد بخيار العيب لأنه ليس له رد أحدهما بخيار شرط أو رؤية قبل القبض أو بعده لأن الصفقة فيها لا تتم إلا بالقبض، قيد بتراخي ظهور العيب عن القبض لأنه لو وجد بأحدهما عيباً قبل القبض فإن قبض المعيب منهما لزماه، أما المعيب فلوجود الرضا به، وأما الآخر فلأنه لا عيب به. ولو قبض السليم منهما فلو كانا معيبين فقبض أحدهما له ردهما جميعاً لأنه لا يمكنه الزام البيع في المقبوض دون الصفقة
Return both, and if he took possession of them then found a defect in one, he returns the defective one alone, because it is a separation after completion, since possession completes in the option of defect. And it will come that the issue of a pair of socks and the two leaves of a door are excepted from here. And based on this, if he bought two oxen and found a defect in one after possession, then if one is attached to the other so that it does not work without it, he does not have the right to return the defective one alone. And it is qualified by the option of defect because he cannot return one of them under the option of condition or sight before or after possession, because the transaction is not completed except by possession. Qualified by the late appearance of the defect after possession because if he found a defect in one before possession, then if he takes possession of the defective one, both become binding: the defective one due to acceptance of it, and the other because it has no defect. And if he took possession of the sound one, then if both are defective and he took possession of one, he may return both, because he cannot enforce the sale on the possessed one without the lot.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on khiyar al-ayb when two items are purchased together in one transaction (ṣafqa). The general rule: after the buyer has taken possession of both items, if a defect is found in one, only the defective item can be returned, because the sale is considered completed for the sound one. This is 'separation after completion' (tafrīq ba'd al-tamām). However, there are exceptions: e.g., a pair of socks or two leaves of a door which are functionally dependent; if one is defective, both must be returned because the sound one is useless alone. Similarly, if two oxen are yoked together, only returning the defective one is not allowed if they are interdependent. The passage also distinguishes the rule based on khiyar type: under khiyar al-ayb, after possession, separation is allowed; under khiyar al-shart or khiyar al-ru'ya, the transaction is not complete until possession, so the same rule does not apply. The timing of the defect discovery matters: if the defect is found before possession, and the buyer takes possession of the defective item, then both items become binding because he accepted the defective one with knowledge, and the other is sound. If both are defective, and he takes possession of one, he may return both because he cannot be forced to accept only part of a defective lot.

Key Terms
صفقة (ṣafqa): A single sale transaction involving multiple items.
تفريق بعد التمام (tafrīq ba'd al-tamām): Separation after completion: returning part of a lot after the sale is fully executed.
زوجي الخف ومصراعي الباب (zawjay al-khuff wa miṣrāʿay al-bāb): A pair of socks and the two leaves of a door: examples of items that are functionally interdependent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone buys a pair of oxen that work together, and after possession finds a defect in one, what is the ruling?
A) He can return only the defective ox
B) He must return both oxen
C) He cannot return either
D) He can return the sound one instead

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi khiyar al-ayb, after full possession of two items bought together, only the defective item is returned unless the items are functionally interdependent, in which case both must be returned.

Dispute over Dimensions and Return of Defective Items
Rulings on dimension disputes and notes on return of defective items in multi-item purchases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer and seller dispute the dimensions of a folded cloth, and explain why returning a defective item to an agent is equivalent to returning to the principal in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
In dimension disputes, the seller's word is taken with oath; returning to agent equals returning to principal.

When a cloth is sold folded and the exact dimensions are disputed, the seller's statement is given precedence. This passage also draws an analogy from currency exchange (ṣarf) to show that returning a defective item to an agent has the same effect as returning it to the principal. Understanding these rulings clarifies the Hanafi approach to sales disputes and agent liabilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of dispute
Recognize that the dispute concerns the dimensions of a folded cloth (marwiyyan), where the buyer claims one measurement and the seller another.
The cloth's folded state makes inspection impossible at sale.
2
Determine who is the seller and what the seller claims
In the passage, the seller states a specific dimension or states that no dimension was mentioned. The buyer makes a counterclaim.
3
State the ruling on whose word is accepted
The Hanafi ruling is that the seller's word is accepted, but he must take an oath to support his claim.
Even if the seller never mentioned the cubit, his word stands according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
4
Understand the analogy from ṣarf to agent returns
In ṣarf (currency exchange), returning a defective dinar without a court order is valid. The passage extends this: returning a defective item to an agent is as valid as returning to the principal.
The difference between court order and consent is not applicable here; the key is the return's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه سبع في الظهيرية يوافق ما ذكره المؤلف ونصه ابن سماعة عن محمد: رجل باع من آخر ثوباً مروياً فقبضه أو لم يقبضه حتى اختلفا فقال البائع بعته على أنه ست في تسع وقال المشتري اشتريته على أنه ثمان فالقول قول البائع مع يمينه .اهـ. وقال في التتارخانية وفي نوادر هشام : إذا اشترى من آخر ثوباً وقال المشتري اشتريت منك بمائة على أنه ثمان أذرع في ثمان وهو سبع في سبع وقال البائع بعتك بمائة ولم أسم الذراع فالقول قول البائع في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد .اهـ. ومثله في الذخيرة. قوله : (وذكر لقبولها فائدة أخرى الخ) قال في النهر وأقول : قد علمت فيما مر أنه في الصرف لو رد عليه الدينار بغير قضاء كان له أن يرده على بائعه فسووا فيه بين القضاء والرضا، وعلى هذا فينبغي هنا أن يكون الرد على الوكيل رداً على الموكل والفرق ما مر فتدبر قوله : (فلو كانا معيبين) الذي في المنح
His statement: 'seven in al-Zahiriyya agrees with what the author mentioned, and its text: Ibn Samāʿa from Muḥammad: A man sold to another a folded cloth, and he took possession or did not take possession until they disputed. The seller said: 'I sold it to you on condition it is six in nine.' The buyer said: 'I bought it on condition it is eight.' Then the say is the seller's with his oath. And he said in al-Tātārkhāniyya and in Nawādir Hishām: If someone bought from another a cloth, and the buyer said: 'I bought from you for one hundred on condition it is eight cubits by eight, but it is seven by seven,' and the seller said: 'I sold to you for one hundred and did not mention the cubit,' then the say is the seller's in the opinion of Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad. And similar is in al-Dhakhīra. His statement: 'And he mentioned another benefit for its acceptance.' He said in al-Nahr: And I say: I have learned from what preceded that in ṣarf (currency exchange), if he returned the dinar without a court order, he had the right to return it to his seller. So they equated between court order and consent. Based on this, here the return to the agent should be a return to the principal, and the difference is as mentioned, so ponder. His statement: 'If both are defective,' which is in al-Munyah.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents additional rulings from various sources. First, from al-Zahiriyya via Ibn Samāʿa from Imam Muḥammad: if there is a dispute over the dimensions of a cloth that was bought folded, and the buyer claims different measurements than the seller, the seller's word is accepted with his oath. This is consistent with earlier rulings where the seller has the say in dimension disputes. Second, from al-Tātārkhāniyya and Nawādir Hishām: even if the seller did not mention the specific dimensions, the seller's word is accepted according to Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad. Then the passage discusses a note about the benefit of accepting evidence: in the context of ṣarf (currency exchange), if a person returns a defective dinar to his seller without a court order, it is considered valid. The same principle should apply here: returning a defective item to an agent is equivalent to returning it to the principal, so the return is effective. Finally, the passage references 'if both are defective' from al-Munyah, likely continuing the discussion about returning items when both are defective.

Key Terms
مروياً (marwiyyan): A cloth that is folded or pleated, making it unclear until opened.
الصرف (al-ṣarf): Currency exchange, specifically the exchange of gold and silver.
الوكيل (al-wakīl): An agent authorized to act on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in a dispute over the dimensions of a folded cloth, whose word is accepted?
A) The buyer's word
B) The seller's word
C) The word of an expert
D) The word of the weaver

Key Takeaway
In disputes over folded cloth dimensions, the seller's word with oath prevails; returning to agent is equivalent to returning to principal.

Buying Two Separate Items in One Bargain with Defect
Ruling on finding defect in one of two items bought together

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning a defective item from a multiple-item bargain and explain the principle of unity of the bargain.

Core Idea
A multiple-item bargain is one unit until all items are possessed — you cannot return part before completion.

This lesson examines a common scenario in sales: a buyer purchases two items together, takes possession of one, and then discovers a defect. The Hanafi school's ruling hinges on a key principle — the unity of the bargain (safqah). You will learn how this principle determines whether the buyer can return only the defective item or must return everything.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The buyer bought two items in one bargain, took possession of one, then found a defect. The Hanafi ruling: he must take both or return both.
2
Trace the talil (legal reasoning)
Why? Because the bargain is complete only upon possessing all items. Returning one splits the bargain before completion, which is like splitting the contract itself.
Possession resembles the contract in effect.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf held: if the defective item is the one possessed, only that one may be returned. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold the unified view.
Abu Yusuf treats the other as subsidiary.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The correct view (asahh) is the majority position: take both or return both.
This applies to any two or more items bought together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشتری عبدين صفقة فقبض أحدهما ووجد بإحدهما عيباً أخذهما أو ردهما لأن الصفقة تتم بقبضهما فيكون تفريقاً قبل التمام وهذا لأن القبض له شبه بالعقد فالتفرق فيه كالتفريق في العقد.. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المعيب المقبوض أو غيره، ويروي عن أبي يوسف أنه إذا وجد بالمقبوض عيباً برده خاصة كأنه جعل غير المعيب تبعاً له والأصح أنه يأخذهما أو يردهما لأن تمام الصفقة تتعلق بقبض المبيع وهو اسم للكل فصار كحبس المبيع لما تعلق زواله باستيفاء الثمن لا يزول دون قبض جميعه والعبدان مثال والمراد عبدان أو ثوبان أو نحوهما
And if he bought two slaves in one bargain and took possession of one and found a defect in one of them, he takes both or returns both because the bargain is completed by taking possession of both, so it would be splitting before completion. This is because taking possession has a resemblance to the contract, so separation in it is like separation in the contract. He [the author] stated it absolutely, so it includes whether the defective one is the one taken possession of or the other. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf that if he finds a defect in the one taken possession of, he may return that one alone, as if he considered the other as subsidiary. The correct view is that he takes both or returns both because the completion of the bargain is linked to taking possession of the sold item, which is a name for the whole, so it becomes like detaining the sold item because its removal is contingent upon receiving the full price; it is not removed without taking possession of the whole. The two slaves are an example; the meaning is two slaves or two garments or the like.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding a case where a buyer purchases two separate items (e.g., slaves or garments) in a single transaction, takes possession of one, and then discovers a defect in either item. The Hanafi position, as stated in the text, is that the buyer must either take both or return both. The reasoning is that the bargain is only complete upon taking possession of all items; returning only the defective one would split the bargain before it is fully concluded, which is prohibited because possession has a similar effect to the contract itself. The author notes that Abu Yusuf held a different view: if the defect is in the item already taken, only that one can be returned, treating the other as subsidiary. However, the correct (asahh) view according to the school is that both must be taken or both returned, because the completion of the sale hinges on taking possession of the entire sold item. The phrase 'sold item' applies to the whole, so partial return is not allowed until all possession is complete. This principle applies to any two or more items bought together, such as garments, as the text specifies.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A single bargain or transaction involving multiple items sold together for one price.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive taking possession of the sold item; a condition for completion of the bargain in the context of option of defect.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqa): Splitting the bargain by returning only part of a multiple-item sale before full possession is taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases two garments in one bargain, takes possession of one, and then finds a defect in the other garment (the one not yet possessed), what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He can return only the defective garment.
B) He can keep the possessed garment and return only the defective one.
C) He must either return both garments or keep both.
D) He can only return the defective one if he returns part of the price.

Key Takeaway
A multiple-item sale is a single legal unit until all items are taken possession of; you cannot return part of it before full completion.
