The Buyer's Option When Both Items Are Defective
Ruling on defective items after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both items in a single bargain are defective and the buyer has taken possession of one, and distinguish the consequences if the buyer disposes of the sound item.

Core Idea
You cannot split a bargain; return both or keep the defective one if you dispose of the sound item.

This lesson examines a scenario where both items purchased together are defective, and the buyer takes possession of one. The Hanafi school protects the unity of the sale: the buyer may return both items, but if he disposes of the sound one (by manumission or sale), the defective item becomes binding. This ruling prevents splitting the bargain on the seller.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Both items in a single sale are defective. The buyer takes physical possession of one item before discovering the defects.
Possession means actual delivery, not just ownership transfer.
2
State the default ruling
The buyer may return both items together. He cannot force the seller to accept the sale of only the possessed item.
Splitting the bargain is prohibited (la yumkinu tafriq al-safqah).
3
Explain the rationale
The sale is a single unit (safqah wahidah). Returning one part only would split it, harming the seller. The buyer also cannot waive his right over the unpossessed item because he never consented to that.
Consent (rida) applies to the whole bargain.
4
Analyze the effect of disposal
If the buyer, after possession, sells or manumits the sound (possessed) item, he has implicitly accepted the sale. The defective unpossessed item then becomes binding—he must keep it.
Disposal is an act of ownership that confirms the sale.
5
Contrast with pre-possession defect
If the defect is found before taking possession, the buyer can still return both if he wishes. The key difference is that possession triggers the option to return both; disposal after possession ends that option.
The ruling applies after qabd (possession).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانا معيبين فقبض أحدهما له ردهما جميعاً لأنه لا يمكنه الزام البيع في المقبوض دون الآخر لما فيه من تفريق الصفقة على البائع، ولا يمكن إسقاط حقه في غير المقبوض لأنه لم يرض به. ولو أعتق السليم أو باعه بعد قبضه لزمه الآخر كيلا تتفرق الصفقة على البائع لأن الصفقة لا تتم إلا بقبض المبيع؛ كذا في المحيط. وشمل إطلاقه ما إذا اشترى خاتم فضة فيه فص وقلع الفص لا يضرب بواحد منهما فوجد بأحدهما عيباً بعد القبض فله أن يقلع الفص ويرد المعيب منهما، ولو وجد بأحدهما عيباً قبل القبض ردهما، وكذا السيف المحلى والمنطقة المحلاة. ولو اشترى نخلاً فيه تمر فجز التمر ثم وجد بأحدهما عيباً لا يرد أحدهما بل يردهما لأنهما بمنزلة شيء واحد لأن التمر بعض النخل لأنه خرج منه بخلاف الفص لأنه ليس من الفضة؛ كذا في المحيط
And if both are defective and he took possession of one, he may return both because he cannot compel the sale in the possessed item without the other due to the splitting of the bargain upon the seller, and he cannot drop his right regarding the unpossessed because he did not consent to it. And if he manumitted the sound one or sold it after possession, the other becomes binding upon him so that the bargain is not split upon the seller, because the bargain is not completed except by taking possession of the sold item; thus in al-Muhit. And its absolute statement includes if he bought a silver ring with a gemstone, and the stone can be removed without damaging either, and he finds a defect in one after possession, he may remove the stone and return the defective one from the two; if he finds a defect in one before possession, he returns both. Similarly, a sword with ornamentation and a belt with ornamentation. And if he bought palm trees with dates, then cut the dates, and then finds a defect in one of them, he does not return one but returns both because they are like a single thing since the dates are part of the palm trees because they came from them, unlike the gemstone because it is not part of the silver; thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where both items purchased in one bargain are defective, and the buyer takes possession of one. The ruling is that the buyer may return both items. The reason is that forcing the seller to accept the sale of only the possessed item would split the bargain, which is not permissible. The buyer cannot forfeit his right regarding the unpossessed item because he never consented to it. If the buyer after taking possession of one item performs an act like manumitting or selling it, then the other item becomes binding on him (i.e., he must keep the defective one) to prevent splitting the bargain on the seller. The text then gives examples: a silver ring with a gemstone (fas) that is separable: if a defect appears after possession, the buyer can remove the stone (retaining it) and return only the defective part (the ring or stone). But if the defect appears before possession, he must return both. Similarly, for a sword with ornamental silver, or a belt. For palm trees with dates still on them, if the buyer cuts the dates and then finds a defect, the dates and trees are treated as one unit because the dates are considered part of the tree (having come from it), so he must return both; this contrasts with the gemstone, which is not part of the ring metal.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A bargain or transaction, often referring to a single sale involving multiple items.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or delivery of the sold item.
عتق (itq): Manumission (freeing a slave). Used in the text as an example of disposal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases two items together. After taking possession of one, he discovers both are defective. He then sells the sound item. What is the ruling?
A) He must return both items.
B) He must keep the defective item.
C) He can return only the defective item.
D) The sale is void.

Key Takeaway
When both purchased items are defective, the buyer can return both after taking possession of one; but if he disposes of the sound item, the defective becomes binding.

Separable vs Inseparable Components in Defective Sales
Examples illustrating separable vs inseparable components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi test of separability to determine whether a defective component can be returned alone or must be returned with the main item.

Core Idea
What forms a natural part stays together; what is separate can be returned alone.

The source text illustrates the principle through three examples: a gemstone (fas) in a ring, ornamentation on a sword and belt, and dates on palm trees. The ruling hinges on whether the component is considered part of the main item (inseparable) or not (separable). This distinction is crucial for the buyer's right to return only the defective part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three examples
The text mentions: (1) silver ring with a separable gemstone; (2) ornamented sword and belt (inseparable); (3) palm trees with dates (inseparable because dates come from the tree).
Separability is based on physical attachment and natural connection.
2
Extract the Hanafi principle
If a component is not part of the main item (like a gemstone that can be removed without damage), it can be returned separately. If it is part of the item (ornamentation attached, dates emanating from tree), it must be returned together.
The ruling after possession: if defect in one part, you may remove the separable part and return only that.
3
Apply to the gemstone case
If the ring has a gemstone that can be removed, and the buyer finds a defect in the ring (or stone) after possession, he may remove the stone and return only the defective part. If the defect is found before possession, he returns both.
The key is that the stone is not part of the silver metal.
4
Apply to the dates case
If dates are sold on the trees, they are considered part of the tree. If the buyer cuts the dates and then finds a defect in either, he must return both because they are one unit.
Dates emanate from the tree; they are not separable in the legal sense.
5
Contrast with inseparable ornamentation
A sword or belt with attached ornamentation is treated as one item. The buyer cannot return the ornamentation alone; the whole item must be returned.
Permanent attachment makes it part of the main item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if both are defective and he took possession of one, he may return both because he cannot compel the sale in the possessed item without the other due to the splitting of the bargain upon the seller, and he cannot drop his right regarding the unpossessed because he did not consent to it. Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where both items purchased in one bargain are defective, and the buyer takes possession of one. The ruling is that the buyer may return both items. The reason is that forcing the seller to accept the sale of only the possessed item would split the bargain, which is not permissible. The buyer cannot forfeit his right regarding the unpossessed item because he never consented to it. Key Terms
فص (fas): A gemstone set in a ring, considered a separate item from the metal ring itself in terms of fiqh rulings.
سيف محلى (sayf muhalla): A sword with ornamentation (usually silver inlay); the ornamentation is considered part of the sword for purposes of the sale.
نخل فيه تمر (nakhl fihi tamr): Palm trees with dates attached; the dates are considered part of the tree for the sale because they emanate from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a sword with ornamental silver inlay. After taking possession, he finds a defect in the silver. What is the ruling?
A) He may return only the silver part.
B) He must return the entire sword.
C) He can remove the silver and keep the sword.
D) He has no option because both are defective.

Key Takeaway
A component that is not part of the main item (like a separable gemstone) can be returned alone; components that are attached or emanate (like ornamentation or dates) must be returned with the whole.

Defect in Measured or Weighted Items: Unity of the Commodity
Ruling on partial defect in measured/weighed goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a defect in part of a measured/weighed commodity requires return of the whole, and distinguish the exceptions for multiple containers and non-fungible goods.

Core Idea
Measured/weighed goods are a single legal unit: a defect in part equals defect in all.

When you buy a sack of grain or a bar of gold by weight, the law treats it as one indivisible item. If you find a defect in a small portion, you cannot pick and choose—you must either keep the whole or return the whole. This lesson unpacks that rule, its basis in fungibility (mithliyyat), and the nuance when the goods are in separate containers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is discovering a defect in part of a measured (kayli) or weighed (wazni) commodity after purchase. The ruling: the buyer may either return all of it or keep all of it.
This is an option (mubah), not an obligation.
2
Understand the Principle: Unity of the Commodity
The reason given is 'liconnahu kashay'in wahid' (because it is like a single thing). In Hanafi law, fungible goods (mithliyyat) sold by measure or weight form a legal unit. A defect in any part renders the whole defective.
Contrast with qimiyyat (non-fungible goods) where after possession, only the defective part is returned.
3
Distinguish Before and After Possession
The author's absolute statement includes both before and after possession. However, Al-Hidayah clarifies that the rule of returning the whole applies after possession; before possession the buyer can reject the whole or part.
The Hidayah distinction is to separate mithli from qimi: after possession, mithli requires whole return; qimi allows partial return of the defective portion.
4
Consider Multiple Containers
If the commodity is in two separate containers, an opinion states each container is treated like an independent slave: only the defective container is returned. The absolute statement covers both one and two containers, but this alternative view limits it to one container.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the preferred view leans toward treating separate containers as separate units.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو وجد ببعض الكيلي أو الوزني عيباً رده كله أو أخذه) لكونه كالشيء الواحد. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان قبل القبض أو بعده، وما وقع في الهداية أن المراد بعد القبض فإنما هو ليقع الفرق بين القيميات والمثليات. وشمل ما إذا كان في من وعاء واحد أو وعاءين، وقيل إنه مخصوص بما إذا كان في وعاء واحد، أما إذا كان في وعاءين فهو بمنزلة العبدين حتى يرد الوعاء الذي وجد فيه العيب دون الآخر.
His statement: (And if he finds a defect in part of a measured or weighed item, he returns all of it or takes it) because it is like a single thing. He stated it absolutely, so it includes whether it is before or after possession. And what occurred in al-Hidayah that the intended meaning is after possession is only to establish a difference between fungible goods (mithliyyat) and non-fungible goods (qimiyyat). And it includes whether it is in one container or two containers. And it is said that it is specific to when it is in one container, but if it is in two containers, it is like the case of two slaves: he returns only the container in which the defect is found, not the other.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a defect is found in a portion of a commodity that is sold by measure (kayl) or weight (wazn). The Hanafi position is that such goods are considered as a single legal unit, so the buyer must either return the entire amount or keep the entire amount if he finds a defect in any part. The author, Ibn Nujaym, states that the ruling applies absolutely, whether the defect is discovered before or after possession. However, he notes that al-Hidayah specifies that the ruling is after possession, in order to differentiate between fungible goods (mithliyyat) and non-fungible goods (qimiyyat); for qimiyyat, after possession, only the defective part can be returned, but for mithliyyat, the whole must be returned. The passage also discusses the scenario where the item is in multiple containers. The preferred opinion (the one mentioned as 'qil') is that if the containers are separate, each container is treated like a separate slave (i.e., an independent item), so only the container with the defect is returned. But the absolute statement of the author includes the single-container case as well.

Key Terms
الكيلي (al-kayli): Goods sold by volume measure, such as grain.
الوزني (al-wazni): Goods sold by weight, such as gold, silver, or other commodities.
المثليات (al-mithliyyat): Fungible goods that have perfect substitutes, like identical grains or metals. Defect in part requires return of whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases 100 kg of rice (a mithli commodity) and after taking possession finds a defect in a small portion. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He may return only the defective portion.
B) He may either return all 100 kg or keep all 100 kg.
C) He can return the entire lot only if the defect existed before possession.
D) He must keep the rice but can claim compensation.

Key Takeaway
A defect in part of a measured or weighed commodity obligates the buyer to return the whole or keep the whole, unless the goods are in separate containers, in which case only the defective container is returned.

Defect in Homogeneous Goods: The Rule of Returning All or Keeping All
Ruling with proof and commentary on khiyar al-'ayb for mikyal/mawzun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for khiyar al-'ayb when a defect is found in part of measured or weighed goods, and distinguish the ruling before versus after possession based on whether the item is mithli or qimi.

Core Idea
For measured/weighed goods, a defect in part requires returning all or keeping all — they are one legal unit.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key principle in khiyar al-'ayb: homogeneous goods like grains or metals sold by measure or weight are treated as a single unit. If a defect is found in any part after possession, the buyer must either return the whole lot or keep it; he cannot return only the defective portion. The commentary clarifies that this rule is absolute but al-Hidayah restricts it to after possession to allow a distinction between fungibles and valuables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Recognize that the ruling applies to goods sold by measure (mikyal) or weight (mawzun). These are considered one unit in khiyar al-'ayb.
Examples: grains, oil, metals.
2
Read the matn statement
The matn says: 'If he finds a defect in part of a measured or weighed item, he returns all of it or takes all of it.' This is the core rule.
3
Understand the absolute application
The literal wording does not specify before or after possession. The commentator notes it is absolute, covering both scenarios.
Ibn al-Humam in al-Hidayah restricted it to after possession.
4
Apply the Hidayah restriction
Al-Hidayah states the rule applies only after possession. Before possession, there is no distinction between mithli and qimi; the buyer returns all or takes all regardless.
The restriction is to avoid confusion with the separate rule for qimi goods.
5
Distinguish mithli vs qimi after possession
After possession: for mithli (fungible), the buyer returns all or keeps all; for qimi (valuable), he returns only the defective part.
Example: two sacks of wheat (mithli) vs. a unique painting (qimi).

قبضهما، ومنها لو أعار أحدهما وأمر المستعير بقبضه لا يكون إذناً بقبض الآخر، ومنها لو استحق أحدهما بعد القبض رد المشتري الآخر إن شاء، ومنها لو عيب المشتري المأخوذ ثم هلك الآخر في يد البائع ولم يمنعه Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect: The other [item] due to the splitting of the transaction on the seller, and it is not possible to drop his right in what is not taken into possession because he did not consent to it. If he frees the sound [slave] or sells it after taking possession, the other becomes binding on him so that the transaction is not split on the seller, because the transaction is not completed except by taking possession of the sold item; as is in al-Muhit. Its absoluteness includes if he bought a silver ring with a gem and removed the gem, it is not considered one of them; he finds a defect in one of them after possession: he may remove the gem and return the defective one from them; if he finds a defect in one before possession, he returns both; likewise a decorated sword and a decorated belt. If he bought date palms with dates, then cut the dates, then finds a defect in one, he does not return one but returns both because they are like one thing, because the dates are part of the palm tree as they came out of it, unlike the gem because it is not part of the silver; as is in al-Muhit. His statement: (And if he finds a defect in part of a measured or weighed [item], he returns all of it or takes it) because it is like one thing. He made it absolute, so it includes whether before or after possession. What is in al-Hidayah that it is intended after possession is only to make a distinction between valuables and fungibles. It includes whether they are in one container or two; it is said it is specific to when they are in one container. As for when they are in two containers, it is like two slaves, so he returns the container in which the defect is found, not the other. The author did not mention the ruling for when the sold item is multiple, one cannot benefit from one except with the other, if he finds a defect in one of them: they said it is like a measured/weighed item; he has the option: if he wishes he takes both or returns both before and after possession, because they are like one thing, like a pair of shoes, two door leaves, and a pair of oxen one has bound the other. If he finds one narrower: if it deviates from what is customary for people’s shoes, he returns it, otherwise not. If it does not fit his foot: if he bought them for wearing, he returns, otherwise not, as in al-Muhit. Then know that what one cannot benefit from except with the other has rulings: among them the ruling of defect; among them: if he took possession of one without the seller's permission and the other perished with the seller, the buyer has the option regarding what he took by its portion; the seller's permission to take possession of one is permission to take possession of both; among them: if he lent one and ordered the borrower to take possession, it is not permission for the other; among them: if one is reclaimed after possession, the buyer returns the other if he wishes; among them: if the buyer damages the taken one, then the other perishes in the seller’s hand and the seller did not prevent it, it perishes on the buyer; if the seller prevented it, it perishes on the seller; among them: if the seller causes a defect in one by the buyer's order, he becomes a possessor of both; among them: if the buyer saw one and approved it, it is not approval of the other; among them: if one becomes defective, he does not return the other due to the defect and the option of sight. "Or if both are defective" His statement: He made it absolute, so it includes whether before or after possession. Al-Ramli said: I say in al-Nahr: This is restricted by two restrictions: first, that they are of one type; second, that it is after possession. It was restricted in al-Hidayah, and accordingly the situation differs between fungibles and valuables, because if before, he returns all or takes all, no difference between fungible or valuable. The difference in the ruling after possession: for a valuable, he returns the defective alone; for a fungible, he returns all or takes it. And it was presented in the commentary on his statement "and if he freed it for payment" that if it was food, and he ate part of it, he returns what is defective, returns all or takes it; and if part is reclaimed, he does not have the option to return the remainder, even if it is a garment he has the option, and riding and wearing, and he takes compensation for the deficiency; among them: if a man consumed one, he gives the other to him and is liable for the value of both if he wishes. All the issues are from al-Muhit. The conclusion is that the ruling of one is the ruling of the other except in matters of permission to take possession of one in lending does not constitute permission for the other, and seeing one is not seeing the other.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and explanation from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding khiyar al-'ayb when a defect is found in part of a multi-item sale that is considered a single unit. The author begins by stating the principle that if a buyer finds a defect in one of two items that are part of the same transaction, he cannot return only the defective one if that would split the transaction on the seller, unless the non-defective item is also taken into possession. Examples include a ring with a gem, a sword with its sheath, and dates on a palm. For measured (al-mikyal) or weighed (al-mawzun) goods, the matn states: 'If he finds a defect in part of a measured or weighed item, he returns all of it or takes all of it' because they are considered one unit. This is absolute, covering both before and after possession, but al-Hidayah restricts it to after possession to distinguish between fungibles (mithliyyat) and valuables (qimiyyat). Before possession, there is no distinction; after possession, for a valuable, the buyer returns only the defective part, while for a fungible, he returns all or takes all. The passage also discusses interdependent items (e.g., a pair of shoes, two door leaves, a pair of oxen) that cannot be used one without the other; these are treated like measured/weighed goods, so the buyer must return both or keep both. A list of sub-rulings follows, including cases of possession without permission, death of goods, reclaim, damage, and approval. The commentary by al-Ramli further clarifies the restriction from al-Nahr that the ruling applies only when the items are of one type and after possession. The whole discussion emphasizes the unity of the transaction and the prevention of splitting it unfairly on the seller.

Key Terms
مكيل (mikyal): Goods sold by measure, e.g., grains.
موزون (mawzun): Goods sold by weight, e.g., metals and foodstuffs.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that can be replaced by similar items.
قيمي (qimi): Valuable goods that are unique.
صفقة (safqah): The entire transaction; splitting it means dividing the sale into parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer finds a defect in part of a measured good after taking possession, what is his option?
A) He may return only the defective part.
B) He must return all of it or keep all of it.
C) He may choose to return the good and demand a refund of the entire price.
D) He must accept the good and claim compensation for the defect.

Key Takeaway
For measured or weighed goods of one type, a defect in part after possession requires the buyer to return the whole or keep the whole.

Interdependent Goods (Mutadhaif) in Khiyar al-Ayb and Sub-Rulings
Ruling and sub-rulings for interdependent goods in khiyar al-'ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify interdependent goods (mutadhaif) and apply the rule that a defect in one requires the buyer to return both or keep both, and recall the key sub-rulings related to possession, permission, and damage.

Core Idea
Interdependent goods are one unit: a defect in one forces the buyer to return both or keep both.

This lesson extends the principle of unity to items that cannot function without each other, such as a pair of shoes or door leaves. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq analogizes them to measured/weighed goods and lists numerous sub-rulings covering scenarios like possession without permission, death of goods, and reclaim. These sub-rulings illustrate the practical application of the principle that the transaction is single and indivisible.

Guided Path
1
Define interdependent goods
Identify items that cannot be used one without the other, e.g., a pair of shoes, two door leaves, a pair of oxen yoked together.
The criterion: no benefit without both.
2
State the ruling for defect
If a defect is found in one of a pair, the buyer has the option to return both or keep both, just like mikyal/mawzun.
This applies before and after possession.
3
Analyze sub-rulings: possession without permission
If the buyer takes possession of one item without the seller's permission, and the other perishes, he has an option regarding what he took.
The seller's permission to take one is permission for both.
4
Analyze sub-rulings: damage and reclaim
If the buyer damages one after possession, and the other perishes with the seller, liability depends on whether the seller prevented the buyer from taking it.
Also, if one is reclaimed, the buyer may return the other.
5
Note exceptions
Two exceptions are highlighted: lending one does not permit taking possession of the other, and seeing one does not count as seeing the other for khayar al-ru'yah.
These preserve the separate identity for certain purposes.

If he finds one narrower: if it deviates from what is customary for people’s shoes, he returns it, otherwise not. If it does not fit his foot: if he bought them for wearing, he returns, otherwise not, as in al-Muhit. Then know that what one cannot benefit from except with the other has rulings: among them the ruling of defect; among them: if he took possession of one without the seller's permission and the other perished with the seller, the buyer has the option regarding what he took by its portion; the seller's permission to take possession of one is permission to take possession of both; among them: if he lent one and ordered the borrower to take possession, it is not permission for the other; among them: if one is reclaimed after possession, the buyer returns the other if he wishes; among them: if the buyer damages the taken one, then the other perishes in the seller’s hand and the seller did not prevent it, it perishes on the buyer; if the seller prevented it, it perishes on the seller; among them: if the seller causes a defect in one by the buyer's order, he becomes a possessor of both; among them: if the buyer saw one and approved it, it is not approval of the other; among them: if one becomes defective, he does not return the other due to the defect and the option of sight. "Or if both are defective" His statement: He made it absolute, so it includes whether before or after possession. Al-Ramli said: I say in al-Nahr: This is restricted by two restrictions: first, that they are of one type; second, that it is after possession. It was restricted in al-Hidayah, and accordingly the situation differs between fungibles and valuables, because if before, he returns all or takes all, no difference between fungible or valuable. The difference in the ruling after possession: for a valuable, he returns the defective alone; for a fungible, he returns all or takes it. And it was presented in the commentary on his statement "and if he freed it for payment" that if it was food, and he ate part of it, he returns what is defective, returns all or takes it; and if part is reclaimed, he does not have the option to return the remainder, even if it is a garment he has the option, and riding and wearing, and he takes compensation for the deficiency; among them: if a man consumed one, he gives the other to him and is liable for the value of both if he wishes. All the issues are from al-Muhit. The conclusion is that the ruling of one is the ruling of the other except in matters of permission to take possession of one in lending does not constitute permission for the other, and seeing one is not seeing the other.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and explanation from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding khiyar al-'ayb when a defect is found in part of a multi-item sale that is considered a single unit. The author begins by stating the principle that if a buyer finds a defect in one of two items that are part of the same transaction, he cannot return only the defective one if that would split the transaction on the seller, unless the non-defective item is also taken into possession. Examples include a ring with a gem, a sword with its sheath, and dates on a palm. For measured (al-mikyal) or weighed (al-mawzun) goods, the matn states: 'If he finds a defect in part of a measured or weighed item, he returns all of it or takes all of it' because they are considered one unit. This is absolute, covering both before and after possession, but al-Hidayah restricts it to after possession to distinguish between fungibles (mithliyyat) and valuables (qimiyyat). Before possession, there is no distinction; after possession, for a valuable, the buyer returns only the defective part, while for a fungible, he returns all or takes all. The passage also discusses interdependent items (e.g., a pair of shoes, two door leaves, a pair of oxen) that cannot be used one without the other; these are treated like measured/weighed goods, so the buyer must return both or keep both. A list of sub-rulings follows, including cases of possession without permission, death of goods, reclaim, damage, and approval. The commentary by al-Ramli further clarifies the restriction from al-Nahr that the ruling applies only when the items are of one type and after possession. The whole discussion emphasizes the unity of the transaction and the prevention of splitting it unfairly on the seller.

Key Terms
متضايف (mutadhaif): Interdependent goods that cannot be used one without the other.
العارية (al-ariyah): Lending; a contract of loan for use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a pair of shoes and after delivery finds one shoe is defective. What is his option under Hanafi law?
A) He may return only the defective shoe.
B) He must return both shoes or keep both.
C) He can keep the good shoe and claim a refund for the defective one.
D) He has no option because the shoes are not measured/weighed.

Key Takeaway
Interdependent goods (mutadhaif) share the same ruling as measured/weighed goods: a defect in one requires the buyer to return both or keep both, with two exceptions for permission and sight.

Ruling on Partial Reclaim (Istihqaq) of a Multi-Component Sale
Ruling that partial reclaim does not allow return of remaining item unless it is a valuable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fungible (mithli) and valuable (qimi) goods in the context of partial reclaim and apply the Hanafi ruling on khiyar (option) for each case.

Core Idea
After possession, partial reclaim gives no option for fungibles, but an option for valuables.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the Hanafi ruling when a part of a sold item is legally reclaimed after the buyer has taken possession. The key distinction is whether the item is fungible (mithli) or valuable (qimi), and whether the reclaim occurs before or after possession. Understanding this ruling prevents disputes when property rights are challenged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a partial reclaim (istihqaq) of a sold item. The ruling is that the buyer may have an option to return the remainder or not, depending on the type of good and timing.
This is not an obligation but a right (khiyar).
2
Distinguish by timing
If reclaim occurs before the buyer takes possession, he may return the entire remainder regardless of the good type, because the transaction is not yet complete. If after possession, the ruling depends on the type of good.
Before possession, the transaction can be voided entirely.
3
Distinguish by type of good
For a fungible (mithli), partial reclaim after possession does not give the buyer the option to return the remainder, because splitting does not harm a fungible. For a valuable (qimi), the buyer has the option because splitting is a defect.
Examples of mithli: wheat, oil, dates. Examples of qimi: a specific garment, a slave, a house.
4
Apply to the house and building case
If a house with its building is sold and the building is reclaimed after possession, the buyer may keep the land and return the building with compensation from the seller, according to the relied-upon fatwa.
This accounts for the defect of splitting a house from its building.
5
Note the practical consequence
The buyer must know when he can return the remainder and when he must seek compensation from the seller for the reclaimed portion. This affects how disputes are resolved in courts.
For fungibles, the buyer only has recourse for the value of the reclaimed portion, not return of the remainder.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (And if part of it is reclaimed, he is not given the option to return the remainder, even if it is a garment, he has the option) because splitting does not harm a fungible (mithli), and reclaim does not prevent completion of the transaction because its completion is by the consent of the contracting parties, not by the consent of the owner. He made it absolute, and it is restricted to after possession. As for before, he may return the remainder due to splitting the transaction before completion. By 'garment' he intended a valuable (qimi), because splitting it is a defect, and it existed at the time of sale when the reclaim appeared, unlike measured/weighed goods. So it includes a slave and a house, as in al-Nihayah. Land should be like a house. The summary: if part of the sold item is reclaimed, if before possession, he has option in all; if after, he has option for a valuable but not for a fungible. Likewise, if one is reclaimed, the presence of the other is not required, whether by judgment or consent. And it is mentioned in the chapter of reclaim: He bought and built, then half is reclaimed, and the buyer returns the remainder to the seller; he may recourse to his seller for its price and half the value of the building because he is deceived in half. If the specific half is reclaimed, if the building was in that half specifically, he recourses also for the value of the building; if the building was in the half that was not reclaimed, he keeps the building and does not recourse for anything of the building's value. He bought a house, then its plot was reclaimed and the building was demolished; the buyer said 'I built it, so I recourse to my seller' and the seller said 'I sold it built'; the statement is of the seller. He bought half as an undivided share; half of that is reclaimed before division: the sold item is half of the remaining; if after division: the sold item is half of the remaining which is a quarter. Then he said: He bought a house with its building, then the building was reclaimed before possession; they said: the buyer has the option: if he wishes he may take the land by its portion of the price, or leave. If after possession, he takes the land, returns the remainder, and takes compensation for the deficiency in what he ate; and upon this is the fatwa. He only did not mention it due to disagreement, so ponder. His statement: (And its summary: if part is reclaimed, etc.) al-Inayah said: Reflect on the author's words; you find the ruling of defect and reclaim is before possession in all cases, meaning in what is measured, weighed, or others. As for defect, it is apparent; as for reclaim, because his statement: if that is before possession, he may return the remainder due to splitting the transaction before completion. You find its ruling after possession likewise except in measured/weighed goods, because he mentioned in the case of two slaves: if one is reclaimed, he does not have the right to return the other; and he said in measured/weighed goods: he returns all or takes all, intending after possession. Then he said: and if part is reclaimed, he has no option to return the remainder. His statement: (He bought a house with its building, then the building was reclaimed, etc.) Al-Ramli said: I say in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If part of the sold item is reclaimed before possession, the sale becomes void to the extent...

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when part of the sold item is legally reclaimed (istihqaq) by a third party. The matn states: 'If part of it is reclaimed, he is not given the option to return the remainder, even if it is a garment, he has the option.' The commentary explains that for a mithli (fungible), partial reclaim does not harm because it can be replaced by like; the transaction is considered complete by the consent of the contracting parties, not the owner. However, this applies only after possession. Before possession, the buyer may return the remainder because the transaction is not yet complete. The 'garment' refers to a qimi (valuable), where splitting is a defect. The summary: if reclaim occurs before possession, the buyer has option on the whole; after possession, he has option only for a valuable, not a fungible. The passage then provides details from Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Muhit about scenarios involving pairs, building construction, and houses. The commentary by al-Ramli further clarifies that if part is reclaimed before possession, the sale is void proportionally. The relied-upon fatwa (عليه الفتوى) is that for a house where the building is reclaimed after possession, the buyer takes the land and returns the rest with compensation.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Legal reclaim of property by a rightful owner, voiding the sale of that portion.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible good that can be replaced by similar units (e.g., wheat, oil).
قيمي (qimi): Valuable good that is unique (e.g., a specific garment, slave, house).
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the single transaction into parts, which is generally prohibited if it harms one party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a measured quantity of wheat, takes possession, and then half of the wheat is legally reclaimed. What is the Hanafi ruling regarding the remaining half?
A) The buyer must return the remaining half to the seller.
B) The buyer may keep the remaining half without any recourse.
C) The buyer has no option to return the remaining half because wheat is a fungible (mithli).
D) The buyer may choose to return the remaining half or keep it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, partial reclaim (istihqaq) after possession grants the buyer an option to return the remainder only for valuable (qimi) goods, not for fungibles (mithli), because splitting a fungible does not harm the buyer.

Actions That Indicate Acceptance of Defect
Explaining which actions constitute acceptance of defect and bar return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which buyer actions after knowledge of a defect constitute acceptance (rida) and forfeit the right of return, and apply the necessity exception to riding.

Core Idea
Using an item after knowing its defect bars return—unless the use was necessary.

When a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item, they have the right to return it (khiyar al-'ayb). However, if they perform any action that clearly indicates they intend to keep the item despite the defect, that right is lost. This lesson clarifies which actions—wearing, riding, medical treatment—are considered acceptance and which special circumstances preserve the right of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
The base rule: wearing, riding, and medical treatment are acceptance of the defect. But not all riding is acceptance—some necessary uses are exceptions.
The text also implies that any action indicating intention to retain the item has the same effect.
2
Distinguish necessary vs. non-necessary actions
Riding for watering, returning, or buying fodder is not acceptance if the buyer had no alternative (e.g., difficulty, single load). If an alternative exists, it is acceptance.
Medical treatment is acceptance only for the specific defect treated; treating a different defect does not bar return.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Actions that show the buyer's desire to keep the item (retention in ownership) forfeit the option. The proof is the buyer's conduct after knowledge.
The text analogizes: selling or offering for sale also indicates acceptance, except for counterfeit dirhams (which were never owned).
4
Consider intra-Hanafi disagreement
There is a difference: Imam Sarakhsi holds that second employment (second use) is acceptance; others say even first use can be acceptance if forced by the slave's dislike.
The mufta bihi follows the necessity exception from Al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللبس والركوب والمداواة رضا بالعيب لا الركوب للسقي أو للرد أو لشراء العلف قوله : ( واللبس والركوب والمداواة رضا بالعيب) لأنه دليل الاستبقاء في ملكه أطلق الركوب وهو مقيد بما إذا ركبها في سيصرح به وكذا المداواة إنما تكون رضا بعيب داواه، أما إذا داوى المبيع من عيب قد برئ منه البائع ويه عيب آخر فإنه لا يمتنع رده كما في الولوالجية. وفي خزانة الفقه : اختلفا قال البائع ركبتها لحاجتك وقال المشتري لا ردها عليك فالقول للمشتري. وقيد بخيار العيب لأن هذه الأشياء لا تسقط خيار الشرط لأن الخيار هناك للاختبار وأنه بالاستعمال فلا يكون مسقطاً. وقيد بهذه الأشياء لأن الاستخدام بعد العلم بالعيب لا يكون رضا استحساناً لأن الناس يتوسعون فيه وهو للاختبار ؛ هكذا أطلقه في المبسوط. ونقل عن السرخسي في البزازية أن الصحيح أن الاستخدام رضا بالعيب في المرة الثانية إلا إذا كان في نوع آخر. وفي الصغرى الاستخدام مرة واحدة لا يكون رضا إلا إذا كان على كره من العبد اه قوله : (لا الركوب للسقي أو للرد أو لشراء العلف أي لا يكون الركوب لهذه الأشياء رضا بالعيب. أطلقه وهو كذلك في الرد، وأما في السقي وشراء العلف فلا بد أن يكون لا بد له منه لصعوبتها أو لعجزه أو لكون العلف في عدل واحد، أما إذا كان له بد منه فهو رضا كما في الهداية. وفي جامع الفصولين : ادعى عيباً في حمار فركبه ليرده فجز عن البينة فركبه جائياً فله الرد .اهـ. وفي البزازية : لو ركب لينظر إلى سيرها أو لبس لينظر إلى قدها فهو رضا. وفي فتح القدير : وجد بها عيباً في السفر فحملها فهو عذر. وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى باللبس وأخويه لغير حاجة إلى أن كل تصرف يدل على الرضا بالعيب بعد العلم به يمنع الرد والأرش فمن ذلك البيع والعرض عليه وكتبناه في الفوائد إلا في الدراهم إذا وجدها البائع زيوفاً فعرضها على البيع فإنه لا يمنع الرد على المشتري لأن ردها لكونها خلاف حقه لأن حقه في الجياد فلم تدخل الزيوف في ملكه بخلاف المبيع العين فإنه ملكه فالعرض رضا
And wearing, riding, and medical treatment are acceptance of the defect; not riding for watering, or for returning, or for buying fodder. His statement: (And wearing, riding, and medical treatment are acceptance of the defect) because it is evidence of retaining it in his ownership. He gave riding absolutely but it is restricted to when he rode it, as will be stated. Similarly, medical treatment is acceptance of the defect he treated. If he treated the sold item for a defect from which the seller was free, and there is another defect, it does not prevent its return, as in al-Walwaljiyah. In Khizanat al-Fiqh: they differed: the seller said 'you rode it for your need', the buyer said 'I return it to you'; the word is the buyer's. It is restricted to the option of defect because these things do not nullify the option of condition, since the option there is for testing, and use does not nullify it. It is restricted to these things because employing after knowledge of the defect is not acceptance by istihsan because people are lenient with it and it is for testing. Thus it was given absolutely in al-Mabsut. It is reported from al-Sarakhsi in al-Bazzaziyah that the correct view is that employing is acceptance of the defect the second time unless it is in another type. In al-Sughra, employing once is not acceptance unless it is by the slave's dislike. His statement: (not riding for watering, or for returning, or for buying fodder) means riding for these things is not acceptance of the defect. He gave it absolutely, and it is so for returning. As for watering and buying fodder, it must be that he had no alternative due to difficulty or inability, or because the fodder was in one load. If he had an alternative, it is acceptance, as in al-Hidayah. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: he claimed a defect in a donkey and rode it to return it, then he found a proof, so he rode it coming; he has the right to return. In al-Bazzaziyah: if he rode to see its gait or wore to see its length, it is acceptance. In Fath al-Qadir: he found a defect in it while traveling and mounted it; it is an excuse. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by wearing and its two counterparts for non-necessity that every action indicating acceptance of the defect after knowledge prevents return and compensation. So from that is selling and offering for sale, and we wrote it in al-Fawa'id, except with dirhams if the seller finds them counterfeit and offers them for sale, it does not prevent the buyer's return because returning them is because they are contrary to his right, since his right is in good ones, so the counterfeit did not enter his ownership, unlike the sold item which he owns, so offering is acceptance.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage clarifies what actions by the buyer, after learning of a defect, constitute acceptance (rida) and thus forfeit the right to return the item or claim compensation for the defect. The base matn lists three actions: wearing a garment, riding an animal, and medical treatment of the animal. The commentator explains that these actions indicate the buyer's intention to keep the item in his ownership. However, riding is restricted to cases where the buyer rode the animal for his own need, not for necessities like watering, returning, or buying fodder. The reasoning is that such necessary uses do not imply acceptance. Medical treatment is similarly restricted: if the buyer treats a specific defect from which the seller was free, it is acceptance; but if he treats a different defect, it does not bar his option of return. The passage also discusses disputes: if the buyer rides and the seller claims it was for personal need while the buyer claims it was for return, the buyer's word is taken (with oath). The overall principle is that any action clearly indicating the buyer's desire to keep the item (like selling it or offering it for sale) after knowledge of the defect forfeits his rights, except in the case of counterfeit dirhams where offering them for sale does not constitute acceptance because the buyer never owns them in the first place (they were not lawfully his). This lesson is critical for understanding khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and the conditions that nullify it.

Key Terms
رضا بالعيب (rida bil-'ayb): The buyer's acceptance of a defect after knowledge, which bars return or claim for compensation
استخدام (istikhdam): Using the sold item (especially a slave) after knowledge of defect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered acceptance of the defect?
A) Wearing a garment after discovering a tear
B) Riding a horse to the market after discovering a limp
C) Treating a horse's saddle sore that the seller was responsible for
D) Riding a horse to return it to the seller when there is no other means

Key Takeaway
Any action clearly indicating the buyer's desire to keep the item after knowledge of defect forfeits the right of return, but necessary actions like riding for return are excepted.

Actions That Constitute Acceptance of Defect: The General Rule and Its Necessary Exceptions
Expands on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding acts of acceptance and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions after discovering a defect constitute acceptance under Hanafi fiqh and state the exceptional cases where necessary actions do not bar return.

Core Idea
Necessity overrides acceptance: riding to water or return does not forfeit the option of defect.

When a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item, certain actions after that discovery may be taken as acceptance of the defect, waiving the right to return or seek compensation. However, not all uses indicate acceptance; the Hanafi school carves out exceptions for actions compelled by necessity, such as riding the animal to water or to return it. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks these rules and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
The passage states that wearing, riding, and treating a defect are prima facie evidence of acceptance because they indicate the buyer's intent to retain the item. Understand that this is the starting presumption.
These actions are considered 'acts of ownership' that signal satisfaction with the purchase.
2
Distinguish khiyar al-'ayb from khiyar al-shart
Note that these rulings apply specifically to the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), not the option of condition (khiyar al-shart). In khiyar al-shart, using the item for testing is the very purpose and does not nullify the option.
This distinction is crucial: in defect option, testing is not the purpose; in condition option, it is.
3
Analyze the exceptions for necessity
The passage clarifies that riding for watering, buying fodder, or returning the item is not acceptance if the buyer had no alternative. Recognize the condition: necessity excuses the act from being a voluntary expression of acceptance.
If an alternative exists, the act is again acceptance. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference).
4
Evaluate the internal Hanafi dispute
Some later scholars (e.g., in al-Bazzaziyya) hold that any use the second time is acceptance regardless of purpose. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, as per al-Hidaya, is the necessity exception. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) in judicial practice.
When buyer and seller dispute the purpose, the buyer's word is taken (Khizanat al-Fiqh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللبس والركوب والمداواة رضا بالعيب لأنه دليل الاستبقاء في ملكه أطلق الركوب وهو مقيد بما إذا ركبها في سيصرح به وكذا المداواة إنما تكون رضا بعيب داواه، أما إذا داوى المبيع من عيب قد برئ منه البائع وفيه عيب آخر فإنه لا يمتنع رده كما في الولوالجية. وفي خزانة الفقه: اختلفا قال البائع ركبتها لحاجتك وقال المشتري لا ردها عليك فالقول للمشتري. وقيد بخيار العيب لأن هذه الأشياء لا تسقط خيار الشرط لأن الخيار هناك للاختبار وأنه بالاستعمال فلا يكون مسقطاً. وقيد بهذه الأشياء لأن الاستخدام بعد العلم بالعيب لا يكون رضا استحساناً لأن الناس يتوسعون فيه وهو للاختبار؛ هكذا أطلقه في المبسوط. ونقل عن السرخسي في البزازية أن الصحيح أن الاستخدام رضا بالعيب في المرة الثانية إلا إذا كان في نوع آخر. وفي الصغرى الاستخدام مرة واحدة لا يكون رضا إلا إذا كان على كره من العبد. (قوله: لا الركوب للسقي أو للرد أو لشراء العلف) أي لا يكون الركوب لهذه الأشياء رضا بالعيب. أطلقه وهو كذلك في الرد، وأما في السقي وشراء العلف فلا بد أن يكون لا بد له منه لصعوبتها أو لعجزه أو لكون العلف في عدل واحد، أما إذا كان له بد منه فهو رضا كما في الهداية. وفي جامع الفصولين: ادعى عيباً في حمار فركبه ليرده فجز عن البينة فركبه جائياً فله الرد. وفي البزازية: لو ركب لينظر إلى سيرها أو لبس لينظر إلى قدها فهو رضا. وفي فتح القدير: وجد بها عيباً في السفر فحملها فهو عذر.
And wearing, riding, and treating [the defect] are acceptance of the defect because it is evidence of keeping it in his ownership. Riding is unrestricted but is restricted to when he rides it as will be clarified. Likewise, treating is only acceptance of the defect for which he treated it. But if he treated the sold item for a defect from which the seller had been absolved and it has another defect, then its return is not prevented, as in al-Walwalijiyya. And in Khizanat al-Fiqh: They differed: The seller said, 'You rode it for your need,' and the buyer said, 'No, to return it to you'; the word is the buyer's. And it is restricted to the option of defect because these actions do not nullify the option of condition, since the option there is for testing and using it does not nullify it. And it is restricted to these actions because using after knowledge of the defect is not acceptance by istihsan, since people have flexibility in it and it is for testing. Thus it is absolute in al-Mabsut. And it is narrated from al-Sarakhsi in al-Bazzaziyya that the correct view is that using is acceptance of the defect the second time unless it is in a different type. And in al-Sughra: using once is not acceptance unless it is against the slave's will. (His statement: 'not riding for watering or for return or to buy fodder') i.e., riding for these purposes is not acceptance of the defect. He stated it absolutely, and it is thus for return. But for watering and buying fodder, it must be necessary due to difficulty or inability or because the fodder is in a single load. If there is an alternative, then it is acceptance, as in al-Hidaya. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He claimed a defect in a donkey and rode it to return it, and the witness was absent, so he rode it coming back, so he has the right to return. And in al-Bazzaziyya: If he rode to see its gait or wore to see its fit, it is acceptance. And in Fath al-Qadir: He found a defect in it during travel, so he carried it; that is an excuse.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding actions that indicate acceptance (rida) of a defect after discovery, thereby nullifying the buyer's option to return (khiyar al-'ayb) or claim compensation (arsh). The author explains that wearing, riding, and treating the defect are prima facie evidence of the buyer's intent to retain the item. However, there are exceptions: riding for the purpose of returning the item, watering it, or buying fodder does not constitute acceptance, as these are necessary actions that the buyer would take despite the defect. The passage also records internal Hanafi disagreements: in Khizanat al-Fiqh, when the seller claims the buyer rode for his own need and the buyer claims it was for return, the word is the buyer's. Al-Sarakhsi in al-Bazzaziyya distinguishes between first and second uses. The text emphasizes that these rulings pertain specifically to khiyar al-'ayb, not khiyar al-shart, where testing use is itself the purpose. The overall principle is that any deliberate act indicating enjoyment or disposal of the property after knowledge of the defect generally bars return, except when the act is necessitated by circumstances beyond the buyer's control.

Key Terms
رضا بالعيب (rida bi al-'ayb): Acceptance of the defect, a legal concept wherein the buyer's actions after discovering a defect indicate he is willing to keep the item as is, thereby forfeiting his right to return or seek compensation.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of defect; the buyer's right to return a purchased item if a pre-existing defect is discovered, unless he has accepted the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following actions after discovering a defect is NOT considered acceptance (rida) and thus does NOT bar the buyer from returning the item?
A) Wearing the garment to see if it fits.
B) Riding the animal to water it when there is no other water source.
C) Treating a wound on the animal with medicine.
D) Selling the item to a third party.

Key Takeaway
Necessary actions like riding to return or water do not constitute acceptance of defect; the buyer retains the right to return.

Actions That Nullify the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-'Ayb)
Listing actions that constitute acceptance and void the option of defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which actions prevent the right of return for a defect and explain why they indicate acceptance (rida).

Core Idea
Any action showing ownership consent after knowing the defect voids the right of return.

When a buyer discovers a defect after purchase, certain actions automatically signal acceptance and cancel the option to return. This lesson identifies those actions, such as offering the item for sale, gifting, or paying the remaining price, and explains the Hanafi reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage is a ruling statement listing actions that prevent return. The underlying principle is that any act demonstrating acceptance (rida) after knowledge of the defect nullifies khiyar al-'ayb.
2
Categorize the actions listed
List the actions explicitly mentioned as preventing return: offering for sale, sale itself (in full or part), gift, emancipation, paying remaining price, becoming a scribe, ratification (ijazah), demanding rent, pawning, and collecting produce of the estate.
These are all acts of ownership or benefit.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Note the distinction between offering for sale (prevents return) and seeking cancellation (istiqalah) or merely showing for inspection (does not prevent). The former implies the buyer treats the item as his own to sell, while the latter does not.
4
Apply the ruling to a new case
Consider a buyer who after discovering a defect, uses the item for his own need (e.g., riding the animal for a personal errand). According to the passage, this is acceptance unless done out of necessity.
Riding for return, watering, or buying fodder is not acceptance.

كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب
فله بعيبه، ولا فرق بين أن يكون البائع في المسألتين قال له اعرضها على البيع فإن لم تشتر منك ردها علي أو لا . وقيدنا بالبيع لأنه لو اشترى ثوبا فعرضه على الخياط لينظره أيكفيه أم لا لم يبطل حقه في رده بعيب، وكذا لو عرضها على المقومين لتقوم كما في جامع الفصولين. وفي البزازية : لو قال له البائع بعد الإطلاع أتبيعها قال نعم لزم ولا يتمكن من الرد. قال الشيخ الإمام وينبغي أن يقول بدل قوله نعم لا لأنه «نعم» عرض على البيع «ولا» تقرير لمكنته . وفيها : الاستقالة بعد الإطلاع لا تمنع الرد بخلاف العرض ومن ذلك الإجازة والعرض عليها والمطالبة بالغلة والرهن والكتابة وهذا إذا كان بعد العلم بالعيب، فإن أجره ثم علم به نقضها للعذر ويرده بخلاف الرهن لأنه لا يرده إلا بعد الفكاك كذا في جامع الفصولين. ومنه إرسال ولد البقرة عليها ليرتضع منها أو حلبه لبن الشاة أو شرب اللبن. وهل يرجع بالنقصان؟ قولان وليس منه أكل ثمر الشجر وغلة القن والدار وإرضاع الأمة ولد المشتري وإتلاف كسب المبيع بعد علمه وضرب العبد إن لم يؤثر الضرب فيه، فإن أثر فلا رد ولا رجوع. وليس منه جز صوف الغنم إن نقصه، فإن لم ينقصه فله الرد ، وكذا قطف الثمار إن لم ينقص. واستشكله في جامع الفصولين بأنه ينبغي أن لا يرد لأنها زيادة منفصلة متولدة وهي تمنع الرد ولم أر فيها خلافاً، ولكن يظهر من هذا أن فيها روايتين. ومنه كما في البزازية الوطء بكراً كانت أو ثيباً نقصها أو لا، فلا رد ولا رجوع . وكذا لو قبلها بشهوة أو لمسها لكن يرجع بالنقص إلا أن يقبلها البائع، وإن وطئها الزوج إن ثيباً ردها، وإن بكراً لا . وسكنى الدار أي ابتداؤها لا الدوام، ومنه سقي الأرض وزراعتها وكسح الكرم والبيع كلاً أو بعضاً بعد الإطلاع مانع من الرد، والرجوع وكذا الهبة والإعتاق مطلقاً؛ كذا في البزازية. وفيها : دفع باقي الثمن بعد العلم بالعيب رضاً. وفي الواقعات : الهبة رضا وإن لم يسلم العين إلى الموهوب له لأنه أقوى من العرض اهـ. وفيها : لو عرض نصف الطعام على البيع لزمه النصف ويرد النصف كالبيع وجمع غلات الضيعة رضا، وكذا تركها لأنها تضييع وفي فتح القدير : هنا أن خيار العيب على التراخي عندنا فلا تنساق فلا يكون دليل الرضا إلا إذا ركبها في حاجة نفسه . وقيل : تأويله إذا لم يكن له بد من الركوب إن كان العلف في عدل واحد ولا تنساق ولا تنقاد وقيل الركوب للرد لا يكون رضا كيفما كان قوله: (وليس منه جز صوف الغنم ظاهره أنه عطف على قوله وليس منه أكل ثمر الشجر الخ» أي مما يمنع الرد فيفيد أن جز الصوف أن نقصه ليس مما يمنع الرد أيضاً مع أنه مما الرد بدليل قوله «فإن لم ينقصه فله الرد تأمل قوله : ( فلا ردود ولا رجوع) هذا مخالف لما قدمه في شرح يمنع
Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect. He has [the right to return] due to its defect, and there is no difference whether the seller in the two cases said to him: 'Show it for sale, and if you do not buy from you, return it to me' or not. And we restricted 'for sale' because if he bought a garment and showed it to the tailor to see if it suffices him or not, his right to return due to a defect is not nullified. Likewise if he showed it to appraisers for valuation, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If the seller after inspection said 'Will you sell it?' and he said 'Yes', it is binding and he cannot return. The Shaykh al-Imam said: He should say instead of 'Yes' the word 'No' because 'Yes' is an offer for sale, and 'No' is a confirmation of his option. Also in it: Seeking to cancel (istiqalah) after inspection does not prevent return, unlike offering for sale. And from that: ratification (ijazah), offering for sale, demanding rent, pawning, and becoming a scribe. This is when after knowledge of the defect. If he rented it then knew of it, he revokes it due to the excuse and returns it, unlike pawning because he cannot return it except after redemption, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And from that: sending the calf of the cow onto it to suckle, or milking the sheep's milk, or drinking the milk. And does he claim compensation for the deficiency? Two opinions. And from that is NOT: eating the fruit of the tree, the produce of the slave and the house, suckling the child of the buyer by the slave-woman, destroying the earnings of the sold item after knowledge, and striking the slave if the strike does not affect him; if it affects him then no return and no recourse. And from that is NOT: shearing the sheep's wool if it reduces it; if it does not reduce it, then he may return. Likewise picking fruits if it does not reduce. And this was questioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn that he should not return because it is a separate generated increase which prevents return, and I have not seen any disagreement about it, but it appears from this that there are two narrations. And from that, as in al-Bazzaziyyah: intercourse, whether virgin or non-virgin, whether it reduces her value or not, so no return and no recourse. Likewise if he kissed her with desire or touched her, but he claims for the deficiency unless the seller kissed her. And if the husband had intercourse, if non-virgin he returns her, if virgin no. And residence in the house: i.e., beginning it, not continuation. And from that: watering the land, cultivating it, sweeping the vineyard, and selling all or part after inspection prevents return and recourse. Likewise gift and emancipation absolutely, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And in it: paying the remaining price after knowledge of the defect is acceptance. And in al-Waqi'at: gift is acceptance even if the item is not handed over to the donee because it is stronger than offering for sale. And in it: if he offered half the food for sale, half is binding and he returns half, like a sale; and collecting the produce of the estate is acceptance; likewise leaving it because it is waste. In Fath al-Qadir: here, the option of defect is at leisure according to us, so it is not considered; thus it is not evidence of acceptance unless he rides it for his own need. And it is said: its interpretation is when he has no alternative to riding, if the fodder is in one container and it does not lead or obey. And it is said: riding for the purpose of return is not acceptance in any case because it is a cause for return; for other purposes it is acceptance except out of necessity. And in al-Mawahib: riding for return, watering, or buying fodder is not acceptance absolutely in the most apparent view. He said: (And from that is NOT shearing the sheep's wool) — it appears it is connected to his statement 'And from that is NOT eating the fruit of the tree' etc., meaning that it prevents return, so it indicates that shearing wool if it reduces it is also something that prevents return? But it is something that allows return according to his statement 'if it does not reduce it, then he may return.' Consider his statement: (so no return and no recourse) — this contradicts what he previously mentioned in the commentary that prevents [return].
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, enumerating actions that either prevent or do not prevent the buyer's right to return a defective item after discovering the defect. The core principle is that any action indicating acceptance (rida) nullifies the option of return, while actions that are merely preparatory or done out of necessity do not. The text distinguishes between explicit offers for sale (which prevent return) and mere inspection or estimation (which do not). It also covers nuanced cases like seeking cancellation (istiqalah), renting, pawning, or using the item (riding, milking, etc.). The final part discusses a textual issue regarding shearing wool and clarifies that the correct position is that shearing that reduces value does prevent return, contrary to an apparent contradiction.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return a purchased item due to a defect that existed at the time of sale but was unknown to the buyer.
رضا (rida): Acceptance or consent that nullifies the right of return, implied by certain actions like using the item or offering it for sale after knowledge of the defect.
استقالة (istiqalah): Request to cancel the sale (mutual rescission), which does not prevent return because it is not an act of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions after knowledge of a defect does NOT prevent the right of return according to the Hanafi school?
A) Offering the item for sale
B) Gifting the item to another
C) Demanding a reduction in price instead of return
D) Paying the remaining balance

Key Takeaway
Offering the item for sale, gifting, emancipating, and similar ownership acts after knowing the defect nullify the right of return; inspection and seeking cancellation do not.

Actions That Preserve the Option and the Shearing Case
Listing actions that do not nullify the option, with a focus on the shearing wool controversy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish actions that preserve the right of return from those that void it, and explain the disagreement about shearing wool.

Core Idea
Necessity and non-ownership acts preserve the option; shearing wool that reduces value is a debated exception.

Not every use of a defective item cancels the option. Actions like istiqalah, riding for necessity, or non-destructive inspection keep the right alive. This lesson also examines the tricky case of shearing wool, which reveals an internal Hanafi divergence.

Guided Path
1
List actions that do NOT prevent return
From the passage: istiqalah, showing to tailor/appraiser, renting then revoking, using for necessity (e.g., riding for return/watering/buying fodder), eating fruit, produce of slave/house, suckling buyer's child, destroying earnings, striking without effect, shearing wool if no reduction.
These are either preparatory, non-ownership, or necessary.
2
Understand the shearing debate
The text says shearing that reduces value is 'not from that which prevents return' (i.e., does not prevent?) but then says if no reduction, return allowed. The commentator notes a contradiction and mentions two narrations.
The core issue: is shearing an act of ownership or a necessary use?
3
Apply the necessity principle
Riding for return, watering, buying fodder: these are not acts of ownership but necessary. They do not imply acceptance.
Contrast with riding for personal need, which does imply acceptance.
4
Examine the exception regarding striking
Striking a slave without effect does not prevent return; with effect, it does. This shows that actions which change the item may affect the option.
This parallels the shearing case: reducing value matters.

وزراعتها وكسح الكرم والبيع كلاً أو بعضاً بعد الإطلاع مانع من الرد، والرجوع وكذا الهبة والإعتاق مطلقاً؛ كذا في البزازية. وفيها : دفع باقي الثمن بعد العلم بالعيب رضاً. وفي الواقعات : الهبة رضا وإن لم يسلم العين إلى الموهوب له لأنه أقوى من العرض اهـ. وفيها : لو عرض نصف الطعام على البيع لزمه النصف ويرد النصف كالبيع وجمع غلات الضيعة رضا، وكذا تركها لأنها تضييع وفي فتح القدير : هنا أن خيار العيب على التراخي عندنا فلا تنساق فلا يكون دليل الرضا إلا إذا ركبها في حاجة نفسه . وقيل : تأويله إذا لم يكن له بد من الركوب إن كان العلف في عدل واحد ولا تنساق ولا تنقاد وقيل الركوب للرد لا يكون رضا كيفما كان لأنه سبب للرد ولغيره يكون رضا إلا عن ضرورة .اهـ. وفي المواهب : الركوب للرد أو للسقي أو لشراء العلف لا يكون رضا مطلقاً في الأظهر اهـ.
قوله: (وليس منه جز صوف الغنم ظاهره أنه عطف على قوله وليس منه أكل ثمر الشجر الخ» أي مما يمنع الرد فيفيد أن جز الصوف أن نقصه ليس مما يمنع الرد أيضاً مع أنه مما الرد بدليل قوله «فإن لم ينقصه فله الرد تأمل قوله : ( فلا ردود ولا رجوع) هذا مخالف لما قدمه في شرح يمنع
Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect. He has [the right to return] due to its defect, and there is no difference whether the seller in the two cases said to him: 'Show it for sale, and if you do not buy from you, return it to me' or not. And we restricted 'for sale' because if he bought a garment and showed it to the tailor to see if it suffices him or not, his right to return due to a defect is not nullified. Likewise if he showed it to appraisers for valuation, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If the seller after inspection said 'Will you sell it?' and he said 'Yes', it is binding and he cannot return. The Shaykh al-Imam said: He should say instead of 'Yes' the word 'No' because 'Yes' is an offer for sale, and 'No' is a confirmation of his option. Also in it: Seeking to cancel (istiqalah) after inspection does not prevent return, unlike offering for sale. And from that: ratification (ijazah), offering for sale, demanding rent, pawning, and becoming a scribe. This is when after knowledge of the defect. If he rented it then knew of it, he revokes it due to the excuse and returns it, unlike pawning because he cannot return it except after redemption, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And from that: sending the calf of the cow onto it to suckle, or milking the sheep's milk, or drinking the milk. And does he claim compensation for the deficiency? Two opinions. And from that is NOT: eating the fruit of the tree, the produce of the slave and the house, suckling the child of the buyer by the slave-woman, destroying the earnings of the sold item after knowledge, and striking the slave if the strike does not affect him; if it affects him then no return and no recourse. And from that is NOT: shearing the sheep's wool if it reduces it; if it does not reduce it, then he may return. Likewise picking fruits if it does not reduce. And this was questioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn that he should not return because it is a separate generated increase which prevents return, and I have not seen any disagreement about it, but it appears from this that there are two narrations. And from that, as in al-Bazzaziyyah: intercourse, whether virgin or non-virgin, whether it reduces her value or not, so no return and no recourse. Likewise if he kissed her with desire or touched her, but he claims for the deficiency unless the seller kissed her. And if the husband had intercourse, if non-virgin he returns her, if virgin no. And residence in the house: i.e., beginning it, not continuation. And from that: watering the land, cultivating it, sweeping the vineyard, and selling all or part after inspection prevents return and recourse. Likewise gift and emancipation absolutely, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And in it: paying the remaining price after knowledge of the defect is acceptance. And in al-Waqi'at: gift is acceptance even if the item is not handed over to the donee because it is stronger than offering for sale. And in it: if he offered half the food for sale, half is binding and he returns half, like a sale; and collecting the produce of the estate is acceptance; likewise leaving it because it is waste. In Fath al-Qadir: here, the option of defect is at leisure according to us, so it is not considered; thus it is not evidence of acceptance unless he rides it for his own need. And it is said: its interpretation is when he has no alternative to riding, if the fodder is in one container and it does not lead or obey. And it is said: riding for the purpose of return is not acceptance in any case because it is a cause for return; for other purposes it is acceptance except out of necessity. And in al-Mawahib: riding for return, watering, or buying fodder is not acceptance absolutely in the most apparent view. He said: (And from that is NOT shearing the sheep's wool) — it appears it is connected to his statement 'And from that is NOT eating the fruit of the tree' etc., meaning that it prevents return, so it indicates that shearing wool if it reduces it is also something that prevents return? But it is something that allows return according to his statement 'if it does not reduce it, then he may return.' Consider his statement: (so no return and no recourse) — this contradicts what he previously mentioned in the commentary that prevents [return].
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, enumerating actions that either prevent or do not prevent the buyer's right to return a defective item after discovering the defect. The core principle is that any action indicating acceptance (rida) nullifies the option of return, while actions that are merely preparatory or done out of necessity do not. The text distinguishes between explicit offers for sale (which prevent return) and mere inspection or estimation (which do not). It also covers nuanced cases like seeking cancellation (istiqalah), renting, pawning, or using the item (riding, milking, etc.). The final part discusses a textual issue regarding shearing wool and clarifies that the correct position is that shearing that reduces value does prevent return, contrary to an apparent contradiction.

Key Terms
زيادة منفصلة متولدة (ziyadah munfasilah mutawallidah): A separate generated increase, like wool or milk, that is produced by the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions does NOT prevent the right of return?
A) Shearing the sheep's wool if it reduces its value (according to one narration)
B) Intercourse with a slave-woman (virgin or not)
C) Seeking cancellation (istiqalah) from the seller
D) Paying the remaining price after knowledge of the defect

Key Takeaway
Non-ownership actions and necessary uses preserve the option; shearing wool that reduces value is a debated exception with two Hanafi narrations.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
