The Core Dispute: Prior Cause of Loss – Istihqaq vs. ‘Ayb
Analyzing the intra-madhhab dispute on prior cause of loss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the case of a purchased item with a prior cause of loss, and explain the analogical reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
A cause at the seller voids the sale like defalcation; a defect does not – the key is whether the cause is prior and productive.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: a slave with a prior cause of loss (e.g., theft) at the seller is purchased, and the buyer subsequently cuts the hand. Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes to istihqaq (defalcation), allowing full return; the Sahibayn analogize to a defect, allowing only a claim for deficiency. Understanding these analogies is essential for determining the correct remedy in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
State the facts: a slave is bought with a prior cause of hand-cutting (theft) at the seller, unknown to the buyer at sale or receipt. The buyer then cuts the hand.
The prior cause exists at the seller's hand.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer may return the slave and recover the full price. He argues that the prior cause makes the transaction voidable, analogous to defalcation (istihqaq).
Cause prior → obligation prior → existence attributed to cause.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the buyer recovers only the difference in value between a thief and a non-thief. They consider the slave defective, not void, so the contract is valid but compensation for defect is due.
Cause does not negate value; it is a defect.
4
Compare the analogies
Imam uses qiyas to istihqaq: the cause existed at seller, so the transaction is voided from the start. The Sahibayn use qiyas to 'ayb: the cause coexists with the commodity, making it defective but not void.
The key is whether the cause is prior and productive or simultaneous.
5
Note the thamarah (practical consequence)
Under Imam's view, the buyer returns the slave and gets full price. Under Sahibayn, the buyer keeps the slave and claims only the deficiency.
This difference applies to all similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع رده واسترد الثمن) يعني لو اشترى عبداً قد سرق عند البائع ولم يعلم به وقت الشراء ولا وقت القبض فقطعت يده عند المشتري له أن يرده ويأخذ ما دفعه عند الإمام. وقالا : يرجع بما بين قيمته سارقاً إلى غير سارق. وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قتل بسبب كان عند البائع. والحاصل أنه بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده وبمنزلة العيب عندهما لهما أن الموجود في يد البائع . سبب القطع والقتل وأنه لا ينافي المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه متعيب فيرجع بنقصانه عند تعذر رده وصار كما إذا اشترى حاملاً فماتت في يده بالولادة فإنه يرجع بفضل ما بين قيمتها حاملاً إلى غير حامل . وله أن سبب الوجوب في يد البائع والوجوب يفضي إلى الوجود فيكون الوجود مضافاً إلى السبب السابق وصار كما إذا قتل المغصوب أو قطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت في يد الغاصب ومسألة الحامل ممنوعة قيد بكونه بسبب عند البائع فقط لأنه لو سرق عندهما فقطع بهما يرجع بالنقصان كما ذكرنا، وعنده لا . يرده بدون رضا البائع للعيب الحادث بربع الثمن، وإن قبله البائع فبثلاثة الأرباع لأن اليد من الآدمي نصفه وقد تلفت بالجنايتين أحدهما الرجوع فيتنصف، فلو تداولته الأيدي ثم قطع في يد الأخير رجع الباعة بعضهم على بعض عنده كما في الاستحقاق وعندهما يرجع الأخير على بائعه ولا يرجع بائعه على بائعه لأنه بمنزلة العيب. ولم يقيد المصنف بعدم علم المشتري لسرقته عند البائع، وقيده به في الجامع الصغير وهو مفيد على قولهما لأن العلم بالعيب رضا به ولا يفيد على قوله في الصحيح لأن العلم بالاستحقاق لا يمنع الرجوع ؛ كذا في الهداية. ثم اعلم أنه لا أثر في الاستحقاق بعلم المشتري أنه ملك المستحق إلا فيما لو كانت جارية فأولدها عالماً بأنها ملك الغير، فإن الولد رقيق لعدم الغرور كما في فصله من جامع الفصولين. وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه ليس بمخير بين إمساكه والرجوع بنصف الثمن وليس كذلك، بل هو مخير فله إمساكه وأخذ نصف الثمن لأنه بمنزلة الاستحقاق لا العيب - كما ذكره الشارح - حتى لو مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه. رجع بنصف الثمن عنده كالاستحقاق ولو أعتقه المشتري ثم قتل أو قطعت يده به فإنه لا يرجع عنده بشيء لفوات المالية به، وعندهما يرجع بالنقصان. وإلى هنا ظهر أن الاختلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في ستة مسائل : الأولى له رده عنده لا عندهما. الثانية في قوله : (وكذا لو قبلها يرجع بشهوة) قال في البزازية قال التمرتاشي : قول السرخسي رحمه الله تعالى التقبيل بشهوة يمنع الرد» محمول على ما بعد العلم بالعيب ..اهـ. وفيها قبل هذا وطء الثيب يمنع الرد بالعيب والرجوع بالنقصان، وكذا التقبيل والمس بشهوة لأنه دليل الرضا، وسواء كان قبل العلم بالعيب أو بعده .
He said: (And if the received item is cut due to a cause at the seller, he returns it and recovers the price): meaning if he bought a slave who had stolen at the seller and he did not know at the time of sale or reception, and then the hand was cut at the buyer, according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] he may return him and take back what he paid. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He recovers the difference between his value as a thief and his value as a non-thief. And on this dispute, if he was killed due to a cause at the seller. And the summary: It is like defalcation (istihqaq) according to him, and like a defect according to them. For them, the cause of cutting and killing existed in the seller's hand, and it does not negate value, so the contract is valid but it is defective, so he recovers the deficiency when return is impossible, like if he bought a pregnant animal and she died in his hand due to childbirth, he recovers the difference between her value pregnant and non-pregnant. And for him (Imam), the cause of obligation existed in the seller's hand, and obligation leads to existence, so existence is attributed to the prior cause, like if the usurped property was killed or cut after return due to a offence that existed while in the usurper's hand. And the case of the pregnant animal is disputed. It is restricted to a cause at the seller only, because if it was stolen while with both, and then it was cut while with both, he recovers by deficiency as we mentioned, while according to him he does not. He returns it without the seller's consent due to the newly arisen defect for one-fourth of the price, and if the seller accepts, then for three-fourths because the hand of a human is half of it, and it was lost due to two offences, one of which is the return, so it halves. So if hands alternated and then it was cut in the last hand, according to him the sellers claim from each other as in istihqaq, and according to them the last claimant against his seller, and his seller does not claim from his seller because it is like a defect. And the author did not restrict to the buyer's ignorance of the theft at the seller, but he did restrict it in al-Jami' al-Saghir, which is beneficial according to them because knowledge of the defect is acceptance, but it is not beneficial according to him because knowledge of istihqaq does not prevent recourse, as in al-Hidayah. Then know that knowledge of the buyer that it belongs to the rightful owner has no effect in istihqaq except if it was a slave-woman and he got her pregnant knowing she belonged to another, then the child is a slave due to lack of deception, as in its section from Jami' al-Fusulayn. And apparently the author's statement is that he is not given a choice between keeping it and claiming half the price, but that is not so; rather he is given a choice, so he may keep it and take half the price because it is like istihqaq, not defect—as the commentator mentioned—so even if it died after the cutting naturally, he recovers half the price according to him as in istihqaq. And if the buyer emancipated it and then it was killed or its hand cut, according to him he recovers nothing due to loss of value, and according to them he recovers deficiency. And up to here it appears that the disagreement between Imam and his two companions is in six issues: First: He can return it according to him, not according to them. Second: In his statement: (And likewise if he kissed her he recovers due to desire) said in al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Tamartashi said: The statement of al-Sarakhsi that 'kissing with desire prevents return' is interpreted as after knowledge of the defect. And in it before this: Intercourse with a non-virgin prevents return due to defect and recourse for deficiency, likewise kissing and touching with desire because it is evidence of acceptance, whether before or after knowledge of the defect.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an intra-madhhab dispute (intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding a purchased slave who had a prior cause of loss (like theft or murder) at the seller, and then the buyer causes a physical defect (like cutting a hand). Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes this to defalcation (istihqaq): since the cause existed before the sale, the buyer can return the slave and reclaim the full price. The Sahibayn analogize it to a defect: the sale is valid but the slave became defective, so they only allow recovery of the difference in value. The commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Raiq refines the positions, discussing various applications and consequences, including cases of multiple hands, knowledge of the defect, and emancipation. It concludes by enumerating six points of difference between the Imam and the Sahibayn, and also clarifies the ruling on kissing and touching in the context of option of defect.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Defalcation: a situation where the sold item is found to belong to someone else, making the sale void. Used here analogically for prior causes.
عيب ('ayb): A defect in the item that reduces its value, which does not nullify the sale but allows compensation.
سبب (sabab): A legal cause that leads to a ruling; here, the theft or murder cause already present at the seller's hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the buyer's remedy when a purchased slave had a prior cause of hand-cutting at the seller and the buyer cuts the hand?
A) The buyer may return the slave and recover the full price.
B) The buyer may only claim the difference in value.
C) The buyer must keep the slave and cannot claim anything.
D) The sale is void and the slave must be returned without payment.

Key Takeaway
The prior cause of loss at the seller, when it leads to a subsequent loss by the buyer, is treated by Imam Abu Hanifa as a voidable element (like istihqaq), allowing full return, whereas the Sahibayn treat it as a defect (like 'ayb), only allowing deficiency.

Applications and Six Points of Difference
Exploring practical applications and the six differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the intra-Hanafi dispute on prior cause of loss to scenarios involving multiple hands, buyer knowledge, emancipation, and enumerate the six points of difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The analogy to istihqaq or defect determines outcomes in multiple hands, knowledge, and subsequent acts – six differences flow from this root.

This lesson builds on the core dispute to explore its practical applications. The text details cases of multiple changes of hands, buyer knowledge of the prior cause, emancipation, and more. It culminates in an enumeration of six points of difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, showing how the root analogy branches into specific rulings.

Guided Path
1
Review the core dispute
Recall that Imam Abu Hanifa treats the prior cause as istihqaq (voidable from root), while the Sahibayn treat it as a defect (valid but compensated).
This root determines all subsequent applications.
2
Apply to multiple hands
If the item passes through several sellers and is cut in the last hand, under Imam, each seller can claim from the previous seller (like istihqaq). Under Sahibayn, only the last buyer claims from his seller, and so on, but no chain of recourse because it's like a defect.
The difference in recourse chains.
3
Apply to buyer knowledge
If the buyer knows of the prior theft, under Sahibayn, knowledge of a defect implies acceptance and prevents recourse. Under Imam, knowledge of istihqaq does not prevent recourse because the transaction is voidable regardless.
Knowledge has different effects.
4
Apply to emancipation
If the buyer emancipates the slave and then the hand is cut, under Imam, the buyer loses the right to return (value lost), and gets nothing. Under Sahibayn, he still claims deficiency.
Emancipation destroys the subject matter for return.
5
Enumerate the six points of difference
The text mentions at least six specific cases where the positions differ, including the core case, multiple hands, knowledge, emancipation, etc. The first two are explicitly stated in the passage.
The six points are a summary of the thamarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع رده واسترد الثمن) يعني لو اشترى عبداً قد سرق عند البائع ولم يعلم به وقت الشراء ولا وقت القبض فقطعت يده عند المشتري له أن يرده ويأخذ ما دفعه عند الإمام. وقالا : يرجع بما بين قيمته سارقاً إلى غير سارق. وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قتل بسبب كان عند البائع. والحاصل أنه بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده وبمنزلة العيب عندهما لهما أن الموجود في يد البائع . سبب القطع والقتل وأنه لا ينافي المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه متعيب فيرجع بنقصانه عند تعذر رده وصار كما إذا اشترى حاملاً فماتت في يده بالولادة فإنه يرجع بفضل ما بين قيمتها حاملاً إلى غير حامل . وله أن سبب الوجوب في يد البائع والوجوب يفضي إلى الوجود فيكون الوجود مضافاً إلى السبب السابق وصار كما إذا قتل المغصوب أو قطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت في يد الغاصب ومسألة الحامل ممنوعة قيد بكونه بسبب عند البائع فقط لأنه لو سرق عندهما فقطع بهما يرجع بالنقصان كما ذكرنا، وعنده لا . يرده بدون رضا البائع للعيب الحادث بربع الثمن، وإن قبله البائع فبثلاثة الأرباع لأن اليد من الآدمي نصفه وقد تلفت بالجنايتين أحدهما الرجوع فيتنصف، فلو تداولته الأيدي ثم قطع في يد الأخير رجع الباعة بعضهم على بعض عنده كما في الاستحقاق وعندهما يرجع الأخير على بائعه ولا يرجع بائعه على بائعه لأنه بمنزلة العيب. ولم يقيد المصنف بعدم علم المشتري لسرقته عند البائع، وقيده به في الجامع الصغير وهو مفيد على قولهما لأن العلم بالعيب رضا به ولا يفيد على قوله في الصحيح لأن العلم بالاستحقاق لا يمنع الرجوع ؛ كذا في الهداية. ثم اعلم أنه لا أثر في الاستحقاق بعلم المشتري أنه ملك المستحق إلا فيما لو كانت جارية فأولدها عالماً بأنها ملك الغير، فإن الولد رقيق لعدم الغرور كما في فصله من جامع الفصولين. وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه ليس بمخير بين إمساكه والرجوع بنصف الثمن وليس كذلك، بل هو مخير فله إمساكه وأخذ نصف الثمن لأنه بمنزلة الاستحقاق لا العيب - كما ذكره الشارح - حتى لو مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه. رجع بنصف الثمن عنده كالاستحقاق ولو أعتقه المشتري ثم قتل أو قطعت يده به فإنه لا يرجع عنده بشيء لفوات المالية به، وعندهما يرجع بالنقصان. وإلى هنا ظهر أن الاختلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في ستة مسائل : الأولى له رده عنده لا عندهما. الثانية في قوله : (وكذا لو قبلها يرجع بشهوة) قال في البزازية قال التمرتاشي : قول السرخسي رحمه الله تعالى التقبيل بشهوة يمنع الرد» محمول على ما بعد العلم بالعيب ..اهـ. وفيها قبل هذا وطء الثيب يمنع الرد بالعيب والرجوع بالنقصان، وكذا التقبيل والمس بشهوة لأنه دليل الرضا، وسواء كان قبل العلم بالعيب أو بعده .
He said: (And if the received item is cut due to a cause at the seller, he returns it and recovers the price): meaning if he bought a slave who had stolen at the seller and he did not know at the time of sale or reception, and then the hand was cut at the buyer, according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] he may return him and take back what he paid. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He recovers the difference between his value as a thief and his value as a non-thief. And on this dispute, if he was killed due to a cause at the seller. And the summary: It is like defalcation (istihqaq) according to him, and like a defect according to them. For them, the cause of cutting and killing existed in the seller's hand, and it does not negate value, so the contract is valid but it is defective, so he recovers the deficiency when return is impossible, like if he bought a pregnant animal and she died in his hand due to childbirth, he recovers the difference between her value pregnant and non-pregnant. And for him (Imam), the cause of obligation existed in the seller's hand, and obligation leads to existence, so existence is attributed to the prior cause, like if the usurped property was killed or cut after return due to a offence that existed while in the usurper's hand. And the case of the pregnant animal is disputed. It is restricted to a cause at the seller only, because if it was stolen while with both, and then it was cut while with both, he recovers by deficiency as we mentioned, while according to him he does not. He returns it without the seller's consent due to the newly arisen defect for one-fourth of the price, and if the seller accepts, then for three-fourths because the hand of a human is half of it, and it was lost due to two offences, one of which is the return, so it halves. So if hands alternated and then it was cut in the last hand, according to him the sellers claim from each other as in istihqaq, and according to them the last claimant against his seller, and his seller does not claim from his seller because it is like a defect. And the author did not restrict to the buyer's ignorance of the theft at the seller, but he did restrict it in al-Jami' al-Saghir, which is beneficial according to them because knowledge of the defect is acceptance, but it is not beneficial according to him because knowledge of istihqaq does not prevent recourse, as in al-Hidayah. Then know that knowledge of the buyer that it belongs to the rightful owner has no effect in istihqaq except if it was a slave-woman and he got her pregnant knowing she belonged to another, then the child is a slave due to lack of deception, as in its section from Jami' al-Fusulayn. And apparently the author's statement is that he is not given a choice between keeping it and claiming half the price, but that is not so; rather he is given a choice, so he may keep it and take half the price because it is like istihqaq, not defect—as the commentator mentioned—so even if it died after the cutting naturally, he recovers half the price according to him as in istihqaq. And if the buyer emancipated it and then it was killed or its hand cut, according to him he recovers nothing due to loss of value, and according to them he recovers deficiency. And up to here it appears that the disagreement between Imam and his two companions is in six issues: First: He can return it according to him, not according to them. Second: In his statement: (And likewise if he kissed her he recovers due to desire) said in al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Tamartashi said: The statement of al-Sarakhsi that 'kissing with desire prevents return' is interpreted as after knowledge of the defect. And in it before this: Intercourse with a non-virgin prevents return due to defect and recourse for deficiency, likewise kissing and touching with desire because it is evidence of acceptance, whether before or after knowledge of the defect.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an intra-madhhab dispute (intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding a purchased slave who had a prior cause of loss (like theft or murder) at the seller, and then the buyer causes a physical defect (like cutting a hand). Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes this to defalcation (istihqaq): since the cause existed before the sale, the buyer can return the slave and reclaim the full price. The Sahibayn analogize it to a defect: the sale is valid but the slave became defective, so they only allow recovery of the difference in value. The commentary (sharh) from al-Bahr al-Raiq refines the positions, discussing various applications and consequences, including cases of multiple hands, knowledge of the defect, and emancipation. It concludes by enumerating six points of difference between the Imam and the Sahibayn, and also clarifies the ruling on kissing and touching in the context of option of defect.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): Defalcation: situation where sold item belongs to another, voiding sale.
عيب ('ayb): Defect in item, allowing compensation but not voiding.
سبب (sabab): Prior cause (e.g., theft) at seller.
تداول الأيدي (tadawul al-aydi): Passing through multiple hands (successive sales).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only the last buyer can claim from his immediate seller.
B) Each seller can claim from the previous seller in the chain.
C) No recourse is available; each bears the loss.
D) The first seller is liable to all.

Key Takeaway
The analogy to istihqaq or defect systematically determines outcomes in multiple hands, knowledge, emancipation, and other scenarios, yielding six specific points of difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Returning a Slave with a Hidden Defect: The Six-Point Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the legal characterization of a defect with a pre-existing cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six differences between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the return of a slave whose hand was cut due to a prior theft at the seller's, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf in each scenario.

Core Idea
When cause precedes possession, one Imam sees it as istihqaq (full return), the other two as a mere defect (deficiency only).

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq confronts an intra-Hanafi debate with far-reaching practical consequences. A slave who stole at the seller's is bought; after purchase, his hand is cut as penalty. Should the buyer get his full money back, or only the loss in value? The answer splits the school: Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes to istihqaq, while his two companions treat it as a defect. Let's trace their arguments and the six concrete differences that emerge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and competing analogies
Recognize the fact pattern: a slave who was a thief at the seller's is sold, then after purchase his hand is cut as a penalty. The dispute centers on whether this is treated like istihqaq (a claim of ownership that existed pre-sale) or like a defect ('ayb) that arose post-sale.
Abu Hanifa: istihqaq; Sahibayn: 'ayb.
2
Trace Abu Hanifa's reasoning
According to Abu Hanifa, the cause of the cutting (the theft) existed with the seller. Obligation leads to existence, so the cutting is attributed to that prior cause. Therefore, it is like a case of istihqaq where the buyer can return the slave and recover the full price, or keep it and get half the price (since the hand is half the body).
Qiyas to istihqaq: the existent cutting is caused by a pre-existing sabab.
3
Trace the Sahibayn's reasoning
The two companions argue that the contract is valid; the cutting is a defect that occurs after possession. The slave is still valuable but defective, so the buyer only recovers the difference in value between a thieving slave and a non-thieving slave (the deficiency), and cannot return the slave.
They analogize to the case of a pregnant slave who dies in childbirth: compensation is for the loss in value, not full price.
4
List the six points of difference
The text enumerates six scenarios where the two positions yield different outcomes: (1) right to return, (2) method of compensation, (3) death after cutting, (4) freeing the slave, (5) chain of sellers, (6) knowledge of the defect. Identify each and the ruling under each view.
These six points capture the entire thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Examine the practical thamarah al-khilaf
For each of the six points, determine the practical consequence. For example, under Abu Hanifa the buyer has more options and can get full compensation; under the Sahibayn the buyer's remedy is limited to the deficiency. This affects the buyer's willingness to purchase and the seller's liability.
Also note the effect of knowledge: according to Sahibayn, knowledge bars the claim; according to Abu Hanifa, it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع رده واسترد الثمن) يعني لو اشترى عبداً قد سرق عند البائع ولم يعلم به وقت الشراء ولا وقت القبض فقطعت يده عند المشتري له أن يرده ويأخذ ما دفعه عند الإمام. وقالا : يرجع بما بين قيمته سارقاً إلى غير سارق. وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا قتل بسبب كان عند البائع. والحاصل أنه بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده وبمنزلة العيب عندهما لهما أن الموجود في يد البائع . سبب القطع والقتل وأنه لا ينافي المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه متعيب فيرجع بنقصانه عند تعذر رده وصار كما إذا اشترى حاملاً فماتت في يده بالولادة فإنه يرجع بفضل ما بين قيمتها حاملاً إلى غير حامل . وله أن سبب الوجوب في يد البائع والوجوب يفضي إلى الوجود فيكون الوجود مضافاً إلى السبب السابق وصار كما إذا قتل المغصوب أو قطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت في يد الغاصب ومسألة الحامل ممنوعة قيد بكونه بسبب عند البائع فقط لأنه لو سرق عندهما فقطع بهما يرجع بالنقصان كما ذكرنا، وعنده لا . يرده بدون رضا البائع للعيب الحادث بربع الثمن، وإن قبله البائع فبثلاثة الأرباع لأن اليد من الآدمي نصفه وقد تلفت بالجنايتين أحدهما الرجوع فيتنصف، فلو تداولته الأيدي ثم قطع في يد الأخير رجع الباعة بعضهم على بعض عنده كما في الاستحقاق وعندهما يرجع الأخير على بائعه ولا يرجع بائعه على بائعه لأنه بمنزلة العيب. ولم يقيد المصنف بعدم علم المشتري لسرقته عند البائع، وقيده به في الجامع الصغير وهو مفيد على قولهما لأن العلم بالعيب رضا به ولا يفيد على قوله في الصحيح لأن العلم بالاستحقاق لا يمنع الرجوع ؛ كذا في الهداية. ثم اعلم أنه لا أثر في الاستحقاق بعلم المشتري أنه ملك المستحق إلا فيما لو كانت جارية فأولدها عالماً بأنها ملك الغير، فإن الولد رقيق لعدم الغرور كما في فصله من جامع الفصولين. وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه ليس بمخير بين إمساكه والرجوع بنصف الثمن وليس كذلك، بل هو مخير فله إمساكه وأخذ نصف الثمن لأنه بمنزلة الاستحقاق لا العيب - كما ذكره الشارح - حتى لو مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه. رجع بنصف الثمن عنده كالاستحقاق ولو أعتقه المشتري ثم قتل أو قطعت يده به فإنه لا يرجع عنده بشيء لفوات المالية به، وعندهما يرجع بالنقصان. وإلى هنا ظهر أن الاختلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه في ستة مسائل : الأولى له رده عنده لا عندهما. الثانية في كيفية الرجوع فعنده بالكل إن رده وبالنصف إن أمسكه وعندهما بالنقصان . الثالثة إذا مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه فعنده بالنصف ولا عندهما . الرابعة لو أعتقه فلا رجوع عنده خلافاً لهما الخامسة في رجوع الباعة. السادسة العلم به لهما.
And if the possessed (slave) is cut due to a cause (sabab) with the seller, he may return it and recover the price) meaning if he bought a slave who had stolen at the seller's and did not know of it at the time of purchase nor at the time of possession, and then his hand was cut at the buyer's – he may return him and take what he paid according to Imam Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He recovers the difference between his value as a thief and as a non-thief. And on this difference is the case if he is killed due to a cause that was with the seller. The conclusion is that according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is like a claim of ownership (istihqaq) and according to them it is like a defect. For them, the cause of cutting and killing existed in the seller's hand, and it does not negate monetary value, so the contract is valid but it is defective, so he recovers the deficiency when return is impossible. This is like if he bought a pregnant slave and she died in his hand due to childbirth – he recovers the difference between her value pregnant and non-pregnant. And according to him, the cause of obligation was in the seller's hand, and obligation leads to existence, so the existence is attributed to the prior cause, like if a usurped (object) is killed or cut after return due to an injury that occurred in the usurper's hand. But the case of the pregnant slave is disputed. It is conditioned that it is due to a cause at the seller only, because if he (the slave) stole at the seller's and then cut in both (places) he recovers the deficiency as we mentioned, but according to him he does not. He (the buyer) can return it without the seller's consent for the newly occurring defect for one quarter of the price, and if the seller accepts, then for three quarters, because the hand of a human is half of him, and it has been lost by two injuries – one of them is return, so it is halved. If hands (of buyers) succeeded each other and then cut in the hand of the last, the sellers can claim from each other according to him (Abu Hanifa) as in istihqaq, but according to them the last buyer claims from his seller and his seller does not claim from his seller because it is like a defect. And the author did not condition the buyer's ignorance of his (the slave's) theft at the seller's, but it was conditioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and it is beneficial according to them because knowledge of a defect implies consent, but it is not beneficial according to him in the correct view because knowledge of istihqaq does not bar return; as in al-Hidayah. The apparent wording of the author is that he is not given a choice between keeping it and recovering half the price, but it is not so; rather he is given a choice, so he may keep it and take half the price because it is like istihqaq, not a defect – as the commentator mentioned – even if he dies after the cutting a natural death. He recovers half the price according to him (Abu Hanifa) like istihqaq. And if the buyer sets him free and then he is killed or his hand cut due to it, then according to him he recovers nothing due to loss of monetary value, but according to them he recovers the deficiency. And up to here it appears that the difference between the Imam and his two companions is in six matters: First: He (Abu Hanifa) allows his return, not them. Second: In the manner of recourse: according to him, full price if returned, half if kept; according to them, the deficiency. Third: If he dies after the cutting a natural death: according to him, half; not according to them. Fourth: If he sets him free: no recourse according to him, contrary to them. Fifth: In the recourse of sellers. Sixth: Knowledge (of the defect) bars the option according to them, contrary to him.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a slave who had a hidden defect (thievery) at the seller's, and the buyer cuts his hand (as a penalty for theft) after purchase. The core legal question: can the buyer return the slave and recover the full price, or only the reduction in value? Abu Hanifa treats this as a case of istihqaq (the slave being subject to a claim that existed prior to sale), analogizing it to a slave who is found to be owned by someone else (mustahaqq). In istihqaq, the buyer can return the slave and recover all the price because the contract is void ab initio regarding the defect that existed at the seller. Therefore, if the cause of cutting (the theft) existed with the seller, the buyer can return the slave and take back all the price. The two companions consider it a case of 'ayb (defect) that arose after the contract but before delivery? Actually the defect (the hand being cut) occurs after the buyer's possession, but the cause (theft) was at the seller's. They view it as a defect in the slave that existed at the time of sale (since the slave was a thief, which is a defect in his value) but the cutting actually removes a part, which is a new defect. They argue that the contract is valid, but the slave is defective, so the buyer can only recover the difference in value between a thieving slave and a non-thieving slave (since the hand is lost due to the theft, the value is reduced). The dispute extends to various scenarios: if the slave is killed due to a cause at the seller's, if the slave is a pregnant female that dies in childbirth, and if there are multiple buyers. The author then lists six points of difference: (1) Right to return: Abu Hanifa allows return, the two do not (they only allow compensation for defect). (2) How compensation works: According to Abu Hanifa, if the buyer returns the slave, he gets full price; if he keeps it, he gets half the price (because the hand is half the body). According to the two, he gets the difference in value. (3) If the slave dies naturally after the cutting: Abu Hanifa gives half the price, the two give nothing. (4) If the buyer frees the slave: Abu Hanifa gives no recourse (because the slave is no longer in his possession), the two give the deficiency. (5) In multiple sales: Abu Hanifa allows each buyer to claim back from his seller in a chain; the two only allow the last buyer to claim from his immediate seller. (6) Knowledge of the defect: According to the two, if the buyer knew the slave was a thief, he cannot claim because knowledge implies consent; according to Abu Hanifa, knowledge does not bar return because it is like istihqaq, where knowledge does not prevent the right to return. The passage emphasizes the practical differences between the two positions and provides the reasoning behind each view.

Key Terms
قطع المقبوض بسبب عند البائع (qata'a al-maqbood bi-sabab 'inda al-ba'i'): The cutting of the possession (slave) due to a cause that existed with the seller, such as the slave being a thief and then the hand is severed as a penalty.
استحقاق (istihqaq): A claim of ownership that existed prior to the sale, rendering the sale void ab initio regarding that claim, allowing full return of the price.
عيب ('ayb): A defect in the merchandise that reduces its value, which gives the buyer an option to return or seek compensation for the deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a buyer purchases a slave who was a thief at the seller's, and the buyer's hand is cut (due to the theft), what are the buyer's options?
A) He can only return the slave and get the full price.
B) He can only keep the slave and get half the price.
C) He can either return the slave and get the full price, or keep the slave and get half the price.
D) He can only get the difference in value between a thieving and non-thieving slave.

Key Takeaway
An intra-Hanafi dispute on how to compensate a buyer when a pre-existing cause leads to a subsequent loss yields six concrete differences, with Imam Abu Hanifa allowing full return and the Sahibayn limiting to deficiency.

Ruling When the Cutting Occurs at the Seller's Before Sale
Explaining that if the cutting was at the seller's, the buyer still gets deficiency, and additional cases of sick or adulterous slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that when a slave dies at the buyer's due to a cause that existed at the seller's, the buyer recovers only the deficiency, and explain the underlying principle of 'increase of pains and their succession' (ziyadat al-alam wa taradufiha).

Core Idea
When a slave dies from a pre-existing defect at the buyer's, the buyer recovers deficiency not full price – liability hinges on where the immediate cause of death occurs.

We saw earlier that if a seller cuts off a slave's limb and then sells him, and the slave dies at the buyer's, the buyer recovers the deficiency. Now the author expands the ruling: even if the cutting or illness was at the seller's, the buyer still only recovers deficiency because the actual death occurred at the buyer's due to an increase of pain. This lesson walks through the additional cases of a sick slave, an adulterous slave, and the distinct case of loss of virginity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common scenarios
The passage discusses three scenarios: (a) slave cut by seller then dies at buyer's, (b) slave sick at seller's then dies at buyer's, (c) slave who committed adultery at seller's flogged and dies at buyer's. Identify the shared element: the cause (cut, illness, adultery) existed at seller's; death occurred at buyer's due to additional factors.
2
Extract the legal principle
The author uses the concept of 'ziyadat al-alam wa taradufiha' (increase and succession of pains). This means that when the death at the buyer's is the result of a progression or combination of factors (not directly caused by the seller's act alone), the seller is not liable for the full price.
This principle is a form of causal apportionment in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the principle to each case
For the sick slave: illness was at seller's, but death from illness at buyer's involves increased pain. For the adulterous slave: adultery necessitates flogging, but flogging occurred at buyer's and caused death; seller not liable for flogging. In both, buyer only gets deficiency.
4
Distinguish the virginity case
If a seller marries off a virgin then sells her, and buyer loses virginity due to intercourse, buyer cannot claim deficiency because virginity is not a property right acquired by sale. This shows a different principle: some defects are not compensable in sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بكونه قطع عند المشتري لأنه لو قطع عند البائع ثم باعه فمات عند المشتري به فإنه يرجع بالنقصان عنده أيضاً وبالقطع لأنه لو اشترى مريضاً فمات منه عند المشتري أو عبداً زنى عند البائع فجلد عند المشتري فمات به رجع بالنقصان عنده أيضاً لأن المريض والمقطوع عند البائع إنما ماتا بزيادة الآلام وترادفها عند المشتري وهي لم توجد عند البائع، وزنا العبد يوجب الجلد والموت غيره فلا يؤاخذ البائع بما لم يكن عنده . وكذا لو زوج البكر ثم باعها وقبضها المشتري ولم يعلم بالنكاح ثم وطئها الزوج لا يرجع بنقصان البكارة وإن كان زوالها بسبب كان عند البائع لأن البكارة لا تستحق بالبيع؛ كذا في فتح القدير . وكتبنا في شرح المنار من بحث الأداء والقضاء أنه لو بيع عند المشتري بدين كان عند البائع أمته فإنه يرجع بالثمن فالمسائل الموردة عليه خمس
And it is conditioned that the cutting is at the buyer's, because if the cutting was at the seller's and then he sold him, and he (the slave) died at the buyer's due to it, then he (the buyer) recovers the deficiency according to him (Abu Hanifa) as well, and by the cutting because if he bought a sick person who died from it at the buyer's, or a slave who committed adultery at the seller's and then was flogged at the buyer's and died from it, he recovers the deficiency according to him as well, because the sick and the one cut at the seller's only died from an increase of pains and their succession at the buyer's which did not exist at the seller's, and the slave's adultery necessitates flogging, and death is other than it, so the seller cannot be held liable for what was not with him. Similarly, if he (the seller) married off a virgin and then sold her, and the buyer took possession without knowing of the marriage, and then the husband had intercourse with her, he does not recover the deficiency of virginity even if its removal was due to a cause at the seller's, because virginity is not a right acquired by sale; as in Fath al-Qadir. And we wrote in Sharh al-Minar from the discussion of performance and recompense that if she is sold at the buyer's (while being indebted) due to a debt that was at the seller's, he recovers the price. So the issues presented to him are five.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous ruling by considering cases where the cutting or cause of death occurred at the seller's before the sale, and then the slave dies at the buyer's. Even according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer only recovers the deficiency (naqsan) in such cases, because the actual death occurred at the buyer's due to additional factors (such as increased pain). The example of a sick slave: if the slave was sick at the seller's and then dies at the buyer's, the buyer gets the deficiency because the death resulted from the progression of the illness at the buyer's. Similarly, if a slave committed adultery at the seller's and is flogged at the buyer's and dies from the flogging, the buyer gets the deficiency because the flogging is a separate cause from the adultery; the seller is not liable for the death since the flogging occurred at the buyer's. Another example: if a seller marries off a virgin daughter (or female slave?) and then sells her, and the buyer does not know of the marriage, and then the husband has intercourse causing loss of virginity, the buyer cannot claim compensation for loss of virginity because virginity is not a property right acquired by sale. These examples show that the key factor is where the actual harm (death, loss) occurs, and whether the cause at the seller's directly leads to the harm or if there is an intervening cause at the buyer's. The passage ends with a hint that the author has presented five issues (masa'il) on this topic, possibly to be enumerated later.

Key Terms
زيادة الآلام وترادفها (ziyadat al-alam wa taradufiha): The increase of pains and their succession – referring to the progression of illness or injury that leads to death at the buyer's, differentiating from a direct cause at the seller's.
نقصان البكارة (nuqsan al-bikarah): The reduction in value due to loss of virginity in a female slave (or woman sold); the buyer cannot claim for this because it is not a property right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchased a slave who was sick at the seller's, and the slave died at the buyer's due to the illness, what does the buyer recover according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The full price because the cause of death was at the seller's.
B) The deficiency (difference in value) because the death occurred at the buyer's.
C) Nothing, because the buyer took the risk.
D) Half the price because the illness was a defect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a defect from the seller's side causes loss at the buyer's due to progression or intervening causes, the buyer recovers only the deficiency, not the full price.

Scope of Seller's Acquittal from Defects: Present vs. Future Defects
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of bara'ah from defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on whether a seller's blanket acquittal (bara'ah min kull 'ayb) covers defects arising before delivery, and apply the respective rulings to a given sale scenario.

Core Idea
A seller's 'free from all defects' covers existing defects; whether it covers pre-delivery defects is disputed among Hanafi imams.

When a seller says 'I am free from every defect in this item,' the buyer cannot later return the item for a defect that existed at the time of sale. But what about a defect that appears after the contract but before the buyer takes delivery? The Hanafi school is divided on this point. This lesson examines the arguments of Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar, and the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and ruling
The seller's statement 'I am free from every defect' is a valid acquittal (ibra') that waives the buyer's right to return due to defects. The general rule is that the acquittal covers defects that exist at the time of the statement.
The validity is based on the principle that ignorance in relinquishment (jahalah fi al-isqat) is tolerated.
2
Frame the dispute
The issue is whether this acquittal also covers defects that arise after the contract but before the buyer takes delivery (qabl al-qabd). Two positions exist within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the proof of Muhammad and Zufar
Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar argue that acquittal only applies to what is fixed (thabit) at the time of the statement. Future defects are unknown and cannot be waived because ibra' operates on known rights.
They also note that if the seller explicitly says 'and what occurs,' some narrations consider that invalid by consensus.
4
Examine the proof of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf argues that the purpose of the acquittal is to bind the contract by removing the buyer's right to claim unsoundness. This purpose is achieved only if both existing and future pre-delivery defects are included. He considers the contract itself as the cause ('illah) for the acquittal.
This view is reported in al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi, and some sources attribute it also to Abu Hanifa.
5
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf, if a defect arises before delivery, the buyer cannot return the item because the acquittal covers it. Under Muhammad and Zufar, the buyer retains the right to return for that new defect.
The text does not provide a clear tarjih (preference) between the two views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو) برئ من كل عيب به صح وإن يسم الكل ولا يرد بعيب لأن الجهالة في الإسقاط لا تفضي إلى المنازعة وإن كان في ضمنه التمليك لعدم الحاجة إلى التسليم فلا تكون مفسدة، ويدخل تحت الإبراء الموجود والحادث قبل القبض في قول الثاني وذكره مع الإمام في المبسوط وشرح الطحاوي. وفي الخانية أن ظاهر مذهبهما. وقال محمد: لا يدخل فيه الحادث. وهو قول زفر لأن البراءة تتناول الثابت. ولأبي يوسف أن الغرض إلزام العقد بإسقاط حقه عن صفة السلامة وذلك بالبراءة من الموجود والحادث. وأجمعوا أنه لو أبرأه من كل عيب به لا يدخل الحادث ولا يرد علينا عدم صحة أبرأت أحد كما لجهالة من له الحق كقوله لرجل علي ،كذا، ولو قال أبرأتك من كل عيب به وما يحدث لم يصح إجماعاً فاستشكل قول أبي يوسف لأنه مع التنصيص لا يصح فكيف يصححه ويدخله بلا تنصيص ؟ ولكن هذا على رواية الأسبيجابي، وأما على رواية المبسوط فيصح الاشتراط باعتبار أنه يقيم السبب وهو العقد مكان العيب الموجب للرد. وفي قوله : ( ومسألة الحامل ممنوعة) أي على قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله بل يرجع على قوله «بكل الثمن قاله القاضيان أبو زيد وفخر الدين قاضيخان وتمامه في فتح القدير قوله : (ولكن هذا على رواية الاسبيجابي الخ) جواب عن الإشكال بمنع الإجماع. قال في فتح القدير : أجيب بمنع أنه إجماع بأن في الذخيرة إذا باع بشرط البراءة من كل عيب وما يحدث بعد البيع قبل القبض يصح عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد. وذكر في المبسوط في موضع آخر لا رواية عن أبي يوسف فيما إذا نص على البراءة من حادث كل عيب ثم عنده باعتبار صحيح يقيم السبب وهو العقد مقام العيب الموجب للرد، ولئن سلمنا فالفرق أن الحادث يدخل تبعاً لتقرير غرضهما وكم من شيء لا يثبت قال : وقيل ذلك أنه
His statement: (And if) he is free from every defect in it, it is valid even if he does not name all, and he cannot return for a defect. Because ignorance in relinquishing (a right) does not lead to dispute, even if it includes transfer of ownership, due to lack of need for delivery, so it is not harmful. And the existing defect and the one occurring before delivery are included under the acquittal in the statement of the second (Abu Yusuf), and he mentioned it with the Imam in al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi. In al-Khaniyyah, that is the apparent position of the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?). And Muhammad said: The occurring (defect) is not included. And it is the statement of Zufar because acquittal encompasses what is fixed. And according to Abu Yusuf, the purpose is to bind the contract by dropping his right regarding the quality of soundness, and that is through acquittal from both existing and occurring. And they all agreed that if he acquits him from every defect in it, the occurring (defect) is not included. And we are not refuted by the invalidity of 'I have acquitted someone' due to ignorance of the person entitled, like saying 'on such-and-such a man is owed to me such-and-such'. And if he says 'I acquit you from every defect in it and what occurs' it is invalid by consensus, so the statement of Abu Yusuf is problematic because when expressly stated it is invalid, so how can he validate it without express statement? But this is according to the narration of al-Asbijabi. According to the narration of al-Mabsut, the condition is valid considering that he establishes the cause (the contract) in place of the defect that gives the right of return. And in his statement: (And the case of the pregnant slave is disputed) meaning according to Abu Hanifa may Allah have mercy on him, rather he recovers according to his statement with all the price, said by the two qadis Abu Zaid and Fakhr al-Din Qadikhan, and its completion is in Fath al-Qadir. His statement: (But this is according to the narration of al-Asbijabi) is an answer to the objection by denying that it is consensus. He said in Fath al-Qadir: It was answered by denying that it is consensus, because in al-Dhakhirah, if he sells with the condition of acquittal from every defect and what occurs after the sale before delivery, it is valid according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And it was mentioned in al-Mabsut in another place: no narration from Abu Yusuf when he explicitly mentions acquittal from each occurring defect; then according to him, by valid consideration, he establishes the cause (the contract) in place of the defect that gives the right of return. And if we accept that, the difference is that the occurring (defect) is included as a subsidiary for establishing their objective, and how many things are not established? He said: And it is said that this is...
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity and scope of a seller's statement 'I am free from every defect' (bara'ah min kull 'ayb) in a sale contract. The main issue is whether such a blanket acquittal covers not only defects existing at the time of contract but also defects that arise before the buyer takes delivery (qabl al-qabd). The Hanafi school has internal disagreement. According to Abu Yusuf (the 'second'), the acquittal covers both existing and future defects (occurring before delivery), because the purpose is to bind the contract by waiving the buyer's right to claim defects. Muhammad al-Shaybani and Zufar disagree, saying acquittal only covers defects existing at the time of the statement (thabit), as the word 'ayb refers to what is present. The text references al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi supporting Abu Yusuf. There is also a discussion that if the seller explicitly says 'and what occurs' (wa ma yahduth), some narrations say it is invalid by consensus because it involves uncertainty (jahalah). Abu Yusuf's position is justified by considering the contract itself as the cause for the acquittal, analogous to the defect that gives the right of return. The passage also includes a digression about the pregnant slave case (mentioned in the first lesson), clarifying that according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer can return for full price (bikull al-thaman) as ruled by Qadi Abu Zaid and Qadi Khan. The overall commentary is dense and references multiple authorities, showing the nuanced development of the Hanafi position on bara'ah.

Key Terms
برئ من كل عيب (bari'a min kull 'ayb): The seller's statement that he is free from (i.e., not liable for) any defect in the sold item, effectively waiving the buyer's right to return due to defects.
إبراء (ibra'): A legal acquittal or waiver of a right. Here, it is the seller's waiver of the buyer's right to claim defects.
جهالة في الإسقاط (jahalah fi al-isqat): Ignorance in the subject of relinquishment – meaning that if the buyer does not know exactly which defects are waived, it is still valid because the purpose is to avoid disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a seller says 'I am free from every defect in this slave', does it cover a defect that appears before the buyer takes delivery?
A) Yes, because the purpose is to bind the contract and waive the right to claim unsoundness.
B) No, because acquittal only applies to existing defects.
C) Yes, but only if the seller explicitly mentions future defects.
D) No, because the buyer can always return for any defect.

Key Takeaway
The scope of a blanket acquittal from defects is limited to existing defects according to Muhammad and Zufar, but includes future defects before delivery according to Abu Yusuf, with no definitive tarjih in the text.

Imam vs Sahibayn on Reduction for Defects
States method of return for defects and cases of death after maiming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on claiming reduction for pre-existing defects and apply them to scenarios involving retention and death of the item.

Core Idea
The amount of reduction for a defect differs between Imam and Sahibayn: half vs actual, and in death after maiming: half vs nothing.

When a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect in a purchased item, the remedy depends on whether the buyer returns or retains it. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on the amount of reduction. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rules for various scenarios, including when the item dies after the defect, and conditions the defect occurred at the buyer's. Understanding this dispute is essential for determining the mufta bihi position today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the general rule
Recognize that 'عنده' refers to Imam Abu Hanifa and 'عندهما' to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The general rule: if the buyer returns the defective item, Imam gives full price; if he keeps it, Imam gives half the reduction; Sahibayn give actual reduction in both cases.
The reduction (النقصان) is the difference between the sound price and the defective price.
2
Analyze the death-after-maiming case
If the slave dies after being maimed (cut limb) at the buyer's, Imam gives half the reduction, Sahibayn give nothing. This is because Imam sees the pre-existing defect as a partial cause; Sahibayn see death as a new event severing liability.
The maiming must occur at the buyer's; if at seller's, the ruling changes.
3
Examine the emancipation case
If the seller emancipates the slave after sale, the buyer cannot return, but according to Imam, the option to return is not prevented; according to Sahibayn, it is prevented.
This is a separate dispute not detailed here.
4
Understand the condition of maiming location
The text conditions that the maiming be at the buyer's. If maimed at seller's, then sold, and the slave dies from it at buyer's, Imam still allows reduction because the cause begins with seller. The reasoning: the pains intensified at the buyer's.
Same applies to a sick slave or a slave who committed zina - seller not liable for death if cause is new.
5
Apply the virginity case
If a virgin slave was married off by seller before sale, and husband consummates after buyer takes possession, buyer cannot claim reduction for loss of virginity because virginity is not a guaranteed right in the sale (بكارة لا تستحق بالبيع).
This illustrates the principle that not every pre-existing condition is compensable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية الرجوع فعنده بالكل إن رده وبالنصف إن أمسكه وعندهما بالنقصان . الثالثة إذا مات بعد القطع حتف أنفه فعنده بالنصف ولا عندهما . الرابعة لو أعتقه فلا رجوع رجوع لا يمنع الخيار عنده خلافاً عنده خلافاً لهما الخامسة في رجوع الباعة. السادسة العلم به لهما. وقيد بكونه قطع عند المشتري لأنه لو قطع عند البائع ثم باعه فمات عند المشتري به فإنه يرجع بالنقصان عنده أيضاً وبالقطع لأنه لو اشترى مريضاً فمات منه عند المشتري أو عبداً زنى عند البائع فجلد عند المشتري فمات به رجع بالنقصان عنده أيضاً لأن المريض والمقطوع عند البائع إنما ماتا بزيادة الآلام وترادفها عند المشتري وهي لم توجد عند البائع، وزنا العبد يوجب الجلد والموت غيره فلا يؤاخذ البائع بما لم يكن عنده . وكذا لو زوج البكر ثم باعها وقبضها المشتري ولم يعلم بالنكاح ثم وطئها الزوج لا يرجع بنقصان البكارة وإن كان زوالها بسبب كان عند البائع لأن البكارة لا تستحق بالبيع؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
The method of return: according to him (Imam), if he returns it, he gets the full price; if he keeps it, he gets half; according to them (the two), he gets the reduction. Third: if it dies after being maimed a natural death, according to him he gets half, according to them nothing. Fourth: if he emancipates it, there is no return; return does not prevent the option according to him, contrary to them. Fifth: regarding the return of sellers. Sixth: knowledge of it according to them. And it is conditioned that the maiming be at the buyer's because if it was maimed at the seller's, then sold, and it died at the buyer's due to that, then he claims reduction according to him also, and by the maiming. Because if he bought a sick slave and he died from it at the buyer's, or a slave who committed zina at the seller's and was flogged at the buyer's and died due to it, he claims reduction according to him also, because the sick and maimed at the seller's died due to the increase of pains and their repetition at the buyer's, which did not exist at the seller's; and the zina of the slave necessitates flogging, and death is other than it, so the seller is not held liable for what was not with him. Likewise, if he married off a virgin, then sold her, and the buyer took possession without knowledge of the marriage, then the husband consummated with her, he does not claim reduction for the loss of virginity, even though its removal was due to a cause that existed at the seller's, because virginity is not entitled by sale; thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the method of return (رجوع) when a defect is discovered in a purchased item. The pronoun 'عنده' refers to Imam Abu Hanifa, and 'عندهما' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). The basic ruling: If the buyer chooses to return the defective item, Imam gives him the full price; if he keeps it, Imam gives him half the reduction. The two give the actual reduction in both cases. Then several specific cases: Third: If the animal dies after being maimed (e.g., a cut limb) at the buyer's, according to Imam he gets half the reduction, according to the two he gets nothing because the cause of death is new. Fourth: If the seller emancipates the slave after sale, the buyer cannot return, but the option (to return) is not prevented according to Imam, contrary to the two. Then a condition: the maiming must occur at the buyer's; if it occurred at the seller's, then the buyer can still claim reduction according to Imam because the defect existed with the seller. The reasoning is that the increased pain and its recurrence happened at the buyer's, not at the seller's. Similarly for a sick slave or a slave who committed zina: the seller is not liable for the death because the cause of death is new. Finally, the case of a virgin slave who was married off before sale: the buyer cannot claim reduction for loss of virginity because it is not a compensable defect in the sale.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The buyer's right to return a defective item or claim a reduction in price.
النقصان (al-nuqsan): The reduction in price corresponding to the defect; also called 'arsh.
القطع (al-qat'): A maiming or cutting of a limb, considered a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased item and chooses to keep it, what does he receive?
A) Full price refund
B) Half the reduction
C) The actual reduction
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The method of reduction for defects in Hanafi law is governed by a key intra-madhhab dispute: Imam gives half reduction upon retention, while the Sahibayn give actual reduction; in case of death after maiming, Imam gives half, Sahibayn give nothing.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Scope of Bara'a in Sales
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of bara'a clause, with sub-issues on specific expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether a general exemption from defects covers future defects, explain their reasoning, and identify the relied-upon ruling for fatwa.

Core Idea
A bara'a clause is valid, but whether it covers future defects divides the Hanafi school.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical intra-Hanafi dispute: when a seller declares 'I am free from every defect in this item,' does the exemption include defects that arise after the contract is signed but before delivery? The answer determines whether the buyer can later claim a reduction or return. Let's follow the arguments of the early masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling on bara'a
The passage first confirms that a general bara'a clause is valid—the buyer cannot return the item due to hidden defects because the waiver is effective despite ignorance of specific defects.
This is the foundation: the clause is sahih (valid).
2
State the conflict on future defects
The core dispute: does the phrase 'every defect in it' cover defects that arise after the contract but before possession? Abu Yusuf says yes; Muhammad and Zufar say no.
The phrase is the same, but interpretation differs.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's argument
Abu Yusuf: the purpose of the clause is to bind the contract by dropping the buyer's right to soundness. That right extends to future soundness until delivery, so the exemption must cover future defects.
He sees the clause as protecting the contract's finality.
4
Follow Muhammad's argument
Muhammad: bara'a applies only to what is established at the time of the contract. Future defects are unknown and cannot be waived in advance. Moreover, explicit exemption from future defects is invalid by some riwayas, so implicit inclusion is even weaker.
His approach is literal: 'present defects only.'
5
Resolve the dispute for fatwa
The later masters (e.g., al-Kasani) give tarjih to Muhammad's view. The mufta bihi is that the clause covers only existing defects. The buyer may claim reduction for a defect that appears after the contract but before delivery.
This is the operative fatwa today.

His saying: (And if) he is free from every defect in it, it is valid even if it is known that it has all [defects], and it is not returned due to a defect, because ignorance in the matter of dropping [rights] does not lead to dispute, even if it includes the meaning of transfer because there is no need for delivery, so it is not corrupting. And it includes under the discharge the existing and the occurring before possession according to the statement of the second (Abu Yusuf), and he mentioned it with the Imam in al-Mabsut and Sharh al-Tahawi. And in al-Khaniyya that is the apparent position of both. And Muhammad said: the occurring does not enter it. And that is the statement of Zufar, because the discharge applies to what is established. And Abu Yusuf's [argument] is that the purpose is to bind the contract by dropping his right to the attribute of soundness, and that is by discharging from the existing and the occurring. And they agreed that if he discharged him from every defect in it, the occurring does not enter. And it does not rebut us the invalidity of 'I discharged you just as ignorance of the one entitled to right, like his saying to a man 'I have such and such upon you', and if he said 'I discharged you from every defect in it and what occurs' it is invalid by consensus, so the statement of Abu Yusuf is problematic because with explicit mention it is invalid, so how can he validate it and include it without explicit mention? But this is on the narration of al-Aspijabi. As for the narration of al-Mabsut, the condition is valid on the basis that it establishes the cause, which is the contract, in place of the defect that necessitates return. And... His saying: (And the issue of the pregnant woman is prevented) i.e., according to the statement of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, but rather he returns on his statement with the full price, said by the two qadis Abu Zayd and Fakhr al-Din Qadikhan, and its completion in Fath al-Qadir. His saying: (But this is on the narration of al-Aspijabi etc.) is a response to the objection by denying consensus. He said in Fath al-Qadir: It is answered by denying that it is consensus, because in al-Dhakhira if he sells with the condition of exemption from every defect and what occurs after the sale before possession, it is valid according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad. And it is mentioned in al-Mabsut in another place that there is no narration from Abu Yusuf regarding explicit exemption from occurring every defect, then according to him on a sound consideration, he establishes the cause, which is the contract, in place of the defect that necessitates return. And if we concede, the difference is that the occurring enters incidentally to realize their purpose, and how many things are not established... He said: And it is said that... Book of sale, chapter of option of defect. al-Bada'i: If he sells on condition that he is free from every defect that occurs after the sale, the sale with this condition is invalid according to us because exemption does not allow addition, and if it is a dropping, it contains the meaning of transfer, and therefore it does not accept rejection, so it does not allow addition explicitly like suspension, so it is a corrupt condition that invalidates the sale. End. And if they differ about a defect whether it occurred after the contract or existed with him, there is no effect of this according to Abu Yusuf, and according to Muhammad the statement is of the seller with his oath on his knowledge that it is occurring. This is if absolute; but if he discharged him restricted to a defect that existed at the seller's, then they differ as we mentioned, the statement is of the buyer; thus in al-Bada'i. And if he conditioned it for a single defect like a cut, and then at the buyer's a defect or death occurred, and he discovered another and wanted to claim reduction, Abu Yusuf gives the seller the option of specification, and Muhammad gives it to the buyer. And its place is when he did not specify it at the sale, but rather discharged him from a cut in it or a defect. And if he discharged him from every evil (gha'ila), it includes theft, absconding, and fornication. And if he discharged him from every disease (da'), it is according to what is inside in custom, and other than that is called illness. And Abu Yusuf said: it covers all. And if he accepted the cloth with its defects, he is free from rips and includes patches and darning. And if he discharged him from every black tooth, it includes red and green, and from every ulcer it includes bloody ulcers; thus in al-Miraj. And the effect (scar) from which he was discharged does not include branding (kay) as in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Muhit: I discharged you from every specific defect, then he is one-eyed, he is not discharged because it is a non-existence not a defect, and likewise if he said 'my hand' and it is cut, he is not discharged contrary to a cut finger, and contrary to if he was discharged from every defect in it; thus in al-Waqi'at. And if he said 'I am free from every defect except absconding', he is free from his absconding. And if he said 'except absconding' (without adding to the slave), he has the right to return due to absconding because he did not attribute absconding to the slave nor describe him with it, so it was not an admission of existing absconding at present, because this speech can mean exemption from an existing absconding of the slave and can mean exemption from an absconding that will occur in the future, so he is not considered to be admitting to his being an absconder at present due to doubt, so the right of return is not established by doubt. End. And if he said 'You are free from every right I have against you' includes defects, that is the chosen, not the responsibility (darak). And in al-Sughra: If the first buyer discharges his seller from the defect after the second has discovered it, it is valid and he does not return it to his seller if he returns it to him. And in al-Khaniyya: If he sells a slave girl and says 'I am free from every defect in her', he is free from every defect in her. And if he says 'I am free from her', he is not free from any defect. And if he says 'I discharge you from every defect' and does not say 'in her', this is a discharge from every defect. End. And in it: He sold a thing on the condition that he is free from every defect, it is not an admission of the defect. And if he conditions exemption from one or two defects, that is an admission of that defect; its explanation: if he sells two slaves on condition that he is free from every defect in this specific slave, and handed them to the buyer, then one is disputed, and the buyer found the purpose... and it is established incidentally. End. What is in al-Fath. His saying: (And in al-Bada'i if he sells on condition that he is free etc.) He said in al-Nahr: It is based on Muhammad's statement as in the sharh, and according to Abu Yusuf it is valid because the purpose is to effect a sale in a way that the seller does not become entitled to soundness from defects. End. And it is far-fetched, rather the apparent meaning of his saying 'according to us' follows what is in the sharh. Free.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq dealing with bara'a (exemption from defects) in sales. It first states the main ruling: if the seller says 'I am free from every defect in this item' (or similar), the sale is valid and the buyer cannot return it due to a hidden defect, even if the item has many defects. This is because the buyer has agreed to drop his right to claim the item's soundness. The ignorance about specific defects does not lead to dispute because it is a waiver of rights. The passage then dives into a major intra-Hanafi dispute: does a general exemption (bara'a) cover defects that arise after the contract but before delivery? According to Abu Yusuf (al-thani), it does cover both existing and future defects (up to delivery). According to Muhammad, it only covers existing defects; future defects are not included. Zufar agrees with Muhammad. The reasoning: Abu Yusuf says the purpose is to bind the contract by dropping the buyer's right to soundness, so the exemption must cover both. Muhammad says bara'a only applies to what is established at the time. There is a claim of consensus that explicit exemption from future defects is invalid, but this is disputed based on different riwayas (narrations) from Abu Yusuf. According to al-Aspijabi, even explicit stipulation of future defects is invalid, so Abu Yusuf's view is inconsistent. According to al-Mabsut, the stipulation is valid because the contract itself replaces the defect as the cause for return. The passage then discusses sub-issues: if the exemption is restricted to a specific defect, or if the parties later dispute whether a defect existed earlier or occurred later. It also details the meanings of various terms like 'gha'ila' (evil inclinations: theft, absconding, fornication), 'da'' (disease: covers internal ailments), and specific expressions like 'every black tooth' includes red and green (variations of color). The rule is that exemption covers only what is specifically included in the expression, and any ambiguity is resolved based on custom (urf) or the apparent meaning. For example, 'I am free from every specific defect' does not cover a missing eye because blindness is a nonexistence, not a defect. The passage concludes with practical applications, such as the first buyer's discharge affecting the second buyer, and the distinction between exemption from all defects (not an admission) and exemption from one or two defects (which is an admission of that defect).

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'a): Exemption of the seller from liability for defects; a waiver of the buyer's right to claim the item's soundness.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): The act of discharging or exempting; used synonymously with bara'a in this context.
العيب الحادث (al-'ayb al-hadith): A defect that arises after the contract is made, before the buyer takes possession.
الغائلة (al-gha'ila): An evil characteristic typically referring to theft, absconding, or fornication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Hanafi Imam held that a general bara'a clause (e.g., 'I am free from every defect in this item') covers defects that arise after the contract but before delivery?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Zufar

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a general exemption from defects covers only defects existing at the time of the contract, not those arising before delivery.

Sale with Condition of Freedom from Future Defects: The Ruling on Ibra Attached to a Future Event
Ruling with proof on a sale condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale conditioned on freedom from future defects, explain the legal reasoning (ibra cannot be attached to the future), and identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Ibra cannot be deferred; a condition of future exemption is a corrupt condition that makes the sale fasid.

In this passage from al-Bada'i' cited in al-Bahr al-Raiq, a key sale condition is examined: a seller stipulates that he is free from any defect that may arise after the sale. The Hanafi school considers this condition corrupt (fasid) because ibra (absolution) cannot be attached to a future time. But why does this reasoning apply, and what happens to the sale? Let's break it down step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the act
The seller conditions that he is 'free from every defect that occurs after the sale.' Identify this as a condition of exemption (ibra) applied to future defects.
The condition is not about existing or discovered defects, but about defects that may arise later.
2
State the ruling
The passage rules that the sale with this condition is fasid (corrupt) in the Hanafi school. Note: fasid means the sale is not valid as structured but can be rectified by removing the condition.
Contrast with batil (void), which cannot be rectified.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The reason given: 'because ibra cannot be attached to the future.' Ibra is a relinquishment of a right, but in Hanafi fiqh it partakes of the nature of tamlik (transfer of ownership), so it cannot be deferred or made contingent (like ta'liq).
The analogy: ibra is like a contract that does not accept ta'liq, so a condition based on a future ibra is corrupt.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds the sale valid because the intention is to sell without warranty of soundness, not a deferred ibra. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the sale is fasid, following Imam Muhammad and the majority.
This difference affects how a mufti advises in practice.
5
Apply the ruling: concrete outcome
If the condition is included, the sale is not automatically void; the condition is void, but the sale can be valid if the condition is dropped (or the buyer waives the right). The buyer can also return the goods due to the corrupt condition.
This is the practical thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البدائع : لو باع على أنه برئ من كل عيب يحدث بعد البيع فالبيع بهذا الشرط فاسد عندنا لأن الإبراء لا يحتمل الإضافة، وإن كان اسقاطاً ففيه معنى التمليك ولهذا لا يقبل الرد فلا يحتمل الإضافة نصاً كالتعليق فكان شرطاً فاسداً فأفسد البيع .اهـ.
In al-Bada'i': If someone sells on the condition that he is free from every defect that occurs after the sale, then the sale with this condition is corrupt (fasid) in our school, because absolution (ibra) cannot be attached to the future. And even if it is a relinquishment (isqat), it contains the meaning of ownership (tamlik), and for this reason it does not accept return, so it cannot be attached to the future textually (nassan) like suspending (ta'liq), so it is a corrupt condition that corrupts the sale.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Bada'i' cited in al-Bahr al-Raiq. It states that if a seller stipulates that he is exempt from any defect that arises after the sale, the sale is fasid (corrupt) in the Hanafi school. The reason is that ibra (absolution or release) cannot be deferred or attached to a future event. Although ibra is a form of isqat (relinquishment of a right), it has an element of tamlik (transfer of ownership) because by absolving the buyer of the right to claim, the seller is effectively giving that right away. Since ibra cannot be made contingent (ta'liq) or added to the future, stipulating it as a condition renders the condition fasid, and a corrupt condition corrupts the sale. This is the view of Imam Muhammad and the relied-upon position. According to Abu Yusuf, such a sale would be valid because the purpose is to sell without warranty of soundness.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra): Absolution or release from a right or claim; in this context, the seller absolving himself from liability for future defects.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A condition that is corrupt but does not necessarily render the entire contract batil; the condition is void but the contract may be valid if the condition is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling with a condition of being free from future defects considered fasid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because it violates the prohibition of gharar
B) Because ibra cannot be attached to the future
C) Because the seller must guarantee all defects
D) Because the buyer must have full knowledge of all defects

Key Takeaway
Ibra cannot be deferred; a condition of future exemption corrupts the sale, making it fasid, but the condition alone can be removed to validate the contract.
