Burden of Proof in Disputes Over Defect Timing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on burden of proof when seller and buyer differ on timing of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on the dispute over whether a defect existed before or after sale, and apply the distinction regarding restricted absolution.

Core Idea
In disputes over defect timing, the seller is believed with an oath unless he specifically absolved himself of that defect.

When a buyer discovers a defect after purchase, the right to return depends on whether the defect existed before the sale or arose after. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whose claim is accepted. This lesson unravels that dispute and the important exception when the seller has given a restricted release.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The buyer and seller disagree on the timing of the defect: buyer claims it existed at sale, seller claims it occurred after.
This dispute only arises when the defect is discovered after possession and the contract is silent.
2
State Abu Yusuf's ruling
Abu Yusuf holds that such a dispute has 'no effect' – likely meaning the buyer can return without needing to prove timing, or that the dispute does not prevent return.
The text is ambiguous; later jurists interpret it as giving the buyer an advantage.
3
State Muhammad's ruling
Muhammad holds that the seller's statement is taken with an oath, i.e., the seller can avoid return by swearing the defect occurred after delivery.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Apply the exception for restricted absolution
If the seller had specifically absolved himself from a particular defect (e.g., 'I am not responsible for this defect'), then the buyer's statement is taken.
A general absolution does not change the rule; only a restricted one shifts the burden to the buyer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Muhammad's view, as stated in al-Bada'i' and al-Hidayah.
This ruling balances the seller's right to defend and the buyer's right to return for genuine defects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلفا في عيب أنه حادث بعد العقد أو كان عنده لا أثر لهذا عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد القول للبائع مع يمينه على العلم بأنه حادث، هذا إذا أطلق أما إذا أبرأه مقيداً بعيب كان عند البائع ثم اختلفا على نحو ما ذكرنا فالقول للمشتري ؛ كذا في البدائع
And if they dispute about a defect: whether it occurred after the contract or it was with him (before the sale), there is no effect for this with Abu Yusuf. And according to Muhammad, the statement is of the seller with his oath upon his knowledge that it occurred (after). This is when it is absolute. But if he absolved him restricted to a defect that was with the seller, then they disputed in the manner we mentioned, then the statement is of the buyer; thus in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute from al-Bada'i'. The issue is: when a buyer discovers a defect and the seller claims the defect occurred after the sale while the buyer claims it existed at the time of sale, who has the burden of proof? According to Abu Yusuf, such a dispute has no effect, meaning the buyer can return the item without oath? Or perhaps no effect meaning the buyer cannot return? The text says 'no effect' which is ambiguous. According to Muhammad, the seller's statement is taken with his oath that the defect arose after the sale. This is when the sale was absolute (without any condition of release). However, if the seller had specifically absolved himself of a particular defect that was known to exist at the seller's end, and then they dispute, the buyer's statement is taken because the seller's absolution implies acknowledgment. This is a nuanced ruling distinguishing between general and specific release.

Key Terms
القول للبائع (al-qawl lil-ba'i'): The statement of the seller is given credence, meaning his claim is accepted with an oath.
إبراء مقيد (ibra' muqayyad): A restricted absolution where the seller specifically absolves himself from a particular defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, when the seller and buyer dispute about whether a defect existed at the time of sale or occurred after, whose statement is taken?
A) The buyer's statement with an oath
B) The seller's statement with an oath
C) Both are equal and the item is returned
D) The dispute has no effect

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over defect timing, the Hanafi school follows Imam Muhammad: the seller's statement with an oath is taken, unless the seller has specifically absolved himself of that defect.

Khiyar al-Ta'yin: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Whose Choice
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-ta'yin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in khiyar al-ta'yin and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of their disagreement.

Core Idea
When buyer causes new defect after discovering old one, who chooses which defect to remedy? Abu Yusuf says seller; Muhammad says buyer.

This passage from al-Bada'i' presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. The scenario involves a sale where the seller exempts himself from a specific defect (e.g., a scar). After the buyer causes a new defect (or the item is destroyed), he discovers a different old defect and wants to claim compensation for the deficiency. The question is: who has the option (khiyar al-ta'yin) to decide which defect to pursue? Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad hold opposing views, and the practical impact is significant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A seller stipulates exemption from one specific defect (e.g., a scar). Later, the buyer causes a new defect (e.g., a cut) or the item dies. Then the buyer discovers a different old defect that existed before the sale. He wants to recover the deficiency caused by that old defect.
The seller's exemption is from a specific defect, not all defects.
2
State the two positions
Abu Yusuf gives the choice (khiyar al-ta'yin) to the seller: the seller can choose whether to compensate for the new defect or the old exempted one. Imam Muhammad gives the choice to the buyer: the buyer can choose which defect to base his claim on.
Both positions are authoritative within the Hanafi school.
3
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the seller has the choice, he will select the defect that results in less compensation. If the buyer has the choice, he will select the defect that gives him more compensation.
This directly affects the amount of compensation.
4
Recognize the limitation
This dispute applies only when the seller did not specify the defect at the time of sale but instead gave a general exemption, e.g., absolved from 'a scar or a defect in it'.
If the defect was specified, the rule may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شرطها من عيب واحد كشجة فحدث عند المشتري عيب أو موت فاطلع على آخر فأراد الرجوع بالنقصان جعل أبو يوسف الخيار للبائع في التعيين، وجعله محمد رحمه الله تعالى للمشتري، ومحله ما إذا لم يعينها عند البيع بل أبرأه من شجة به أو عيب
And if he stipulated from one specific defect like a scar (shajjah), then a defect or death occurs with the buyer, and he discovers another (old defect) and wants to recourse by the (difference in) deficiency, Abu Yusuf gave the choice to the seller in designation, and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) gave it to the buyer. Its place is when he did not specify it at the time of sale but rather absolved him from a scar in it or a defect.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute from al-Bada'i'. The scenario: the seller stipulates exemption from one specific defect (e.g., a scar). Later, the buyer causes a new defect (e.g., a cut) or the item dies, and then discovers that a different old defect existed. The buyer wants to claim compensation for the deficiency caused by that old defect. According to Abu Yusuf, the seller has the option to choose which defect—the one he was exempted from or the new one—to compensate for the deficiency. According to Muhammad, the buyer has that choice. This applies when the seller did not specify the defect at the time of sale but rather gave a general exemption from 'a scar or a defect' in it.

Key Terms
خيار التعيين (khiyar al-ta'yin): Option of designation: the right to choose which specific defect to base the claim on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a buyer causes a new defect after discovering an old one, and the seller had exempted himself from a specific defect, who does Imam Muhammad give the choice of designation to?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) The judge
D) No one, the sale is void

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-ta'yin, Abu Yusuf gives seller the choice of designation; Muhammad gives buyer the choice; the practical effect is who controls compensation.

The Scope of 'Kull Gha'ila' and 'Kull Da'' in Release Clauses
Defining the scope of 'gha'ila' and 'da'' in release clauses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific defects covered by 'kull gha'ila' and 'kull da'' and explain Abu Yusuf's differing position.

Core Idea
A general release from 'gha'ila' only covers theft, absconding, and fornication; 'da'' covers only internal diseases.

When a seller uses a general phrase of exemption, its meaning is not unlimited. Classical Hanafi texts define specific scopes for terms like 'gha'ila' and 'da' based on custom and linguistic usage. This lesson unpacks those definitions and notes an intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key phrases
The text presents 'kull gha'ila' and 'kull da'' as examples of general release clauses. Note that 'gha'ila' is a technical term for hidden risks, while 'da'' means disease.
2
Recall the scope of 'gha'ila'
The passage states that 'gha'ila' covers only three specific things: theft (sariqa), absconding (ibaq), and fornication (fujur). These are the risks typically associated with slaves.
3
Recall the scope of 'da''
The text says that 'da'' (disease) is restricted to what is inside the body (internal) by custom; external conditions are called 'marad' (illness) and are not included in 'da''.
This distinction is based on customary usage.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that 'kull da'' includes everything (internal and external). This is a valid difference within the Hanafi school, but the relied-upon view is the majority restriction to internal diseases.
The majority view restricts 'da'' based on urf (custom).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحله ما إذا لم يعينها عند البيع بل أبرأه من شجة به أو عيب، ولو أبرأه من كل غائلة فهي في السرقة والإباق والفجور، ولو أبرأه من كل داء فهو على ما في الباطن في العادة وما سواه يسمى مرضاً. وقال أبو يوسف : يتناول الكل. ولو قبل الثوب بعيوبه يبرأ من الخروق وتدخل الرقع والرفو، ولو أبرأه من كل سن سوداء تدخل الحمراء والخضراء ومن كل قرح تدخل القروح الدامية؛ كذا في المعراج . والأثر الذي برئ منه ولا يدخل الكي كما في الخانية. وفي المحيط : أبرأتك من كل عيب بعينه فإذا هو أعور لا يبرأ لأنه عدمها لا عيب وكذا لو قال بيده فإذا هي مقطوعة لا يبرأ بخلاف قطع الأصبع، وبخلاف ما إذا برئ من كل عيب به ؛ كذا في الواقعات. ولو قال أنا بريء من كل عيب إلا إباقة بريء من إباقه، ولو قال إلا الإباق فله الرد بالإباق لأنه لم يضف الإباق إلى العبد ولا وصفه به فلم يكن اعترافاً بوجود الإباق للحال لأن هذا الكلام كما يحتمل التبرئ عن إباق موجود من العبد يحتمل التبري عن إباق سيحدث في المستقبل فلا يكون مقراً بكونه أبقاً للحال بالشك فلا يثبت حق الرد بالشك .اهـ. ولو قال أنت بريء من كل حق لي قبلك دخل العيب هو المختار دون الدرك. وفي الصغرى : المشتري الأول إذا أبرأ بائعه عن العيب بعدما اطلع الثاني عليه صح ولا يرده على بائعه إذا رد عليه. وفي الخانية : إذا باع جارية وقال أنا بريء من كل عيب بها فهو بريء من كل عيب بها، ولو قال أنا منها لا يبرأ عن شيء من العيوب، ولو قال أبرأتك عن كل عيب ولم يقل بها فهذه براءة عن كل عيب اهـ. وفيها : باع شيئاً على أنه بريء من كل عيب لا يكون إقراراً بالعيب، ولو شرط البراءة عن عيب واحد أو عيبين كان ذلك إقراراً بذلك العيب؛ بيانه إذا باع عبدين على أنه بريء من كل عيب بهذا العبد بعينه وسلمهما إلى المشتري فاستحق أحدهما ووجد المشتري مقصوداً و ويثبت تبعاً اهـ ما في الفتح.
And its place is when he did not specify it at the sale but rather absolved him from a scar in it or a defect. And if he absolved him from every 'gha'ila' (hidden risk), it refers to theft, absconding, and fornication. And if he absolved him from every 'da' (disease), it is regarding what is in the interior customarily, and other than that is called 'marad' (illness). Abu Yusuf said: it includes everything. And if he accepted the cloth with its defects, he is free from tears, and patches and darning are included. And if he absolved him from every black tooth, red and green are included; and from every ulcer, bleeding ulcers are included; thus in al-Mi'raj. And the 'athar' (scar) from which he is absolved, and cauterization (kai) is not included, as in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Muhit: 'I absolved you from every specific defect' then if he is one-eyed, he is not absolved because it is a lack of an eye, not a defect; and similarly if he said 'his hand' and it is cut off, he is not absolved, unlike a cut finger; and unlike if he was absolved from every defect in him; thus in al-Waqi'at. And if he said 'I am free from every defect except absconding (ibaq)', he is free from his absconding. And if he said 'except absconding', he has the right to return due to absconding because he did not attribute absconding to the slave nor describe him with it, so it was not an acknowledgment of the existence of absconding at present; because this statement, as it can bear the meaning of acquittal from existing absconding of the slave, it can also bear acittal from absconding that will occur in the future, so he is not confessing that he was an absconder at present due to doubt, so the right of return is not established by doubt. And if he said 'you are free from every right I have against you', the defect is included, this is the chosen view, not the 'darak' (liability for claim). And in al-Sughra: The first buyer, if he absolved his seller from the defect after the second buyer has become aware of it, it is valid, and he does not return it to his seller when he returns it to him. And in al-Khaniyya: If he sold a slave-girl and said 'I am free from every defect in her', he is free from every defect in her. And if he said 'I am free from her', he is not free from anything regarding defects. And if he said 'I absolved you from every defect' and did not say 'in her', this is an absolution from every defect. And in it: He sold something on the basis that he is free from every defect, this is not an acknowledgment of the defect. But if he stipulated exemption from one or two defects, that is an acknowledgment of that defect. Explanation: If he sold two slaves for one price on condition that he is free from every defect in this specific slave, and he handed them over to the buyer, and one is reclaimed, and the buyer found... (text truncated) and it is established as a consequence, thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles multiple rulings on how to interpret various phrases used in exemptions from defects. It covers: 1) The phrase 'kull gha'ila' covers only theft, absconding, and fornication; 'kull da'' covers internal diseases; Abu Yusuf expands to everything. 2) If a cloth is accepted with its defects, the seller is free from tears but patches and darning are considered defects that are included? Actually, the text says patches and darning are included (i.e., as defects the seller is not liable for). 3) Specific colors: black teeth include red and green; ulcers include bleeding ones. 4) 'Athar' (scar) is included but cauterization (kai) is not. 5) If one says 'from every specific defect' and the item is one-eyed, the seller is not free because loss of an eye is not a defect but a lack; similarly a cut hand is not a defect but a severed finger is. 6) Exception wording: 'except ibaq' (absconding) - if the exception is indefinite ('illa ibaq'), it does not acknowledge present absconding because it could refer to future; so the buyer cannot return for that. 7) 'You are free from every right I have' includes defect but not darak (liability for eviction). 8) The first buyer's absolution after second buyer knows is valid. 9) Wording 'I am free from every defect in her' vs 'I am free from her' - the latter gives no exemption. 10) General exemption from all defects is not an acknowledgment of any specific defect, but exemption from one or two defects is acknowledgment of those defects. 11) A case of two slaves sold together with a specific exemption for one, and one is reclaimed, then the buyer finds a defect in the other. This is a detailed area of Hanafi sale law where wording determines liability.

Key Terms
غائلة (gha'ila): Hidden risk; specifically theft, absconding, and fornication in the context of slaves.
داء (da'): Disease; interpreted as internal illness as opposed to external condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a seller says 'I absolved you from every da' (disease)' in a slave, what is included?
A) Only internal diseases customarily
B) All diseases internal and external
C) Only external defects
D) Nothing specific

Key Takeaway
The wording of release clauses must be interpreted according to established legal meanings; 'gha'ila' and 'da'' have specific scopes.

Scars, Cauterization, and Missing Limbs: Defining Defects vs. Lacks
Distinguishing defects (ayb) from lacks (adam) and defining athar vs kai

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a defect ('ayb) and a lack ('adam) in the context of release clauses, and apply the rulings on scars and cauterization.

Core Idea
Scars are included in a release from defects; cauterization is not. Missing an eye or a hand is not a defect but a lack, so no release applies.

Not every undesirable condition in a sold item is considered a 'defect' for legal purposes. This lesson examines the fine distinctions between a scar (athar), a medical procedure (kai), and the absence of a limb, showing how wording impacts liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions covered: athar and kai
The text states that 'athar' (scar) is included in a release from defects, but 'kai' (cauterization) is not. Understand that a scar is a defect, while cauterization is a treatment, not a defect.
2
Distinguish between 'ayb and 'adam in missing body parts
If the seller says 'from every specific defect' and the item is one-eyed or has a cut hand, the seller is not absolved because the absence of an eye or hand is a lack, not a defect. However, a cut finger is considered a defect, so the seller would be absolved if the release includes it.
The key is whether the condition is a defect that affects value or completion, or a total absence.
3
Note the exception: 'from every defect in him'
If the release is phrased as 'from every defect in him', even a lack like a missing eye would be included because it is a defect in the body.
The wording 'in him' expands the scope.
4
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical
Test yourself: A slave seller says 'I am free from every specific defect'. The slave is missing a finger. Is the seller liable? Yes, because a missing finger is a defect. If the slave is missing a hand, no release.

Source Text

Exception Wording in Release Clauses: The Case of 'Illa Ibaq'
Interpreting an indefinite exception in a release clause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an exception phrased indefinitely ('illa ibaq') does not establish present absconding or give the right to return.

Core Idea
A release clause with an indefinite exception ('except absconding') does not acknowledge present absconding; the buyer cannot return the item based on doubt.

When a seller says 'I am free from every defect except absconding (ibaq),' does that mean the slave is currently an absconder? The Hanafi answer distinguishes between attributing the defect to the slave and simply excepting it from the release. This lesson clarifies how such wording impacts the buyer's right to return.

Guided Path
1
Read the two exception wordings
The text contrasts two phrasings: (1) 'I am free from every defect except absconding' (with 'illa ibaq) and (2) 'I am free from every defect except absconding' (with 'illa ibaq) - actually the same phrasing but the interpretation differs based on whether the exception is attached to the slave.
Actually the two are: 'ana bari' min kull 'ayb illa ibaqatahu' (attributing ibaq to the slave) vs 'illa ibaq' (without attribution).
2
Understand the significance of attribution
If the seller says 'illa ibaqatahu' (except his absconding), that is an acknowledgment that the slave has absconded, so the buyer can return him. If he says simply 'illa ibaq' (except absconding), it does not specify whose absconding, so it could refer to future absconding; thus it is not an acknowledgment and the buyer cannot return based on doubt.
The key is whether the exception is connected (mudaf) to the slave.
3
Apply the principle of doubt (shakk)
The right to return is not established by doubt. Since the phrase 'illa ibaq' can bear two meanings (present or future absconding), the buyer cannot prove present absconding, so the seller is not liable.
This is a general Hanafi evidentiary principle: certainty is required for claims.

Source Text

ومحله ما إذا لم يعينها عند البيع بل أبرأه من شجة به أو عيب، ولو أبرأه من كل غائلة فهي في السرقة والإباق والفجور، ولو أبرأه من كل داء فهو على ما في الباطن في العادة وما سواه يسمى مرضاً. وقال أبو يوسف : يتناول الكل. ولو قبل الثوب بعيوبه يبرأ من الخروق وتدخل الرقع والرفو، ولو أبرأه من كل سن سوداء تدخل الحمراء والخضراء ومن كل قرح تدخل القروح الدامية؛ كذا في المعراج . والأثر الذي برئ منه ولا يدخل الكي كما في الخانية. وفي المحيط : أبرأتك من كل عيب بعينه فإذا هو أعور لا يبرأ لأنه عدمها لا عيب وكذا لو قال بيده فإذا هي مقطوعة لا يبرأ بخلاف قطع الأصبع، وبخلاف ما إذا برئ من كل عيب به ؛ كذا في الواقعات. ولو قال أنا بريء من كل عيب إلا إباقة بريء من إباقه، ولو قال إلا الإباق فله الرد بالإباق لأنه لم يضف الإباق إلى العبد ولا وصفه به فلم يكن اعترافاً بوجود الإباق للحال لأن هذا الكلام كما يحتمل التبرئ عن إباق موجود من العبد يحتمل التبري عن إباق سيحدث في المستقبل فلا يكون مقراً بكونه أبقاً للحال بالشك فلا يثبت حق الرد بالشك .اهـ. ولو قال أنت بريء من كل حق لي قبلك دخل العيب هو المختار دون الدرك. وفي الصغرى : المشتري الأول إذا أبرأ بائعه عن العيب بعدما اطلع الثاني عليه صح ولا يرده على بائعه إذا رد عليه. وفي الخانية : إذا باع جارية وقال أنا بريء من كل عيب بها فهو بريء من كل عيب بها، ولو قال أنا منها لا يبرأ عن شيء من العيوب، ولو قال أبرأتك عن كل عيب ولم يقل بها فهذه براءة عن كل عيب اهـ. وفيها : باع شيئاً على أنه بريء من كل عيب لا يكون إقراراً بالعيب، ولو شرط البراءة عن عيب واحد أو عيبين كان ذلك إقراراً بذلك العيب؛ بيانه إذا باع عبدين على أنه بريء من كل عيب بهذا العبد بعينه وسلمهما إلى المشتري فاستحق أحدهما ووجد المشتري مقصوداً و ويثبت تبعاً اهـ ما في الفتح.
And its place is when he did not specify it at the sale but rather absolved him from a scar in it or a defect. And if he absolved him from every 'gha'ila' (hidden risk), it refers to theft, absconding, and fornication. And if he absolved him from every 'da' (disease), it is regarding what is in the interior customarily, and other than that is called 'marad' (illness). Abu Yusuf said: it includes everything. And if he accepted the cloth with its defects, he is free from tears, and patches and darning are included. And if he absolved him from every black tooth, red and green are included; and from every ulcer, bleeding ulcers are included; thus in al-Mi'raj. And the 'athar' (scar) from which he is absolved, and cauterization (kai) is not included, as in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Muhit: 'I absolved you from every specific defect' then if he is one-eyed, he is not absolved because it is a lack of an eye, not a defect; and similarly if he said 'his hand' and it is cut off, he is not absolved, unlike a cut finger; and unlike if he was absolved from every defect in him; thus in al-Waqi'at. And if he said 'I am free from every defect except absconding (ibaq)', he is free from his absconding. And if he said 'except absconding', he has the right to return due to absconding because he did not attribute absconding to the slave nor describe him with it, so it was not an acknowledgment of the existence of absconding at present; because this statement, as it can bear the meaning of acquittal from existing absconding of the slave, it can also bear acittal from absconding that will occur in the future, so he is not confessing that he was an absconder at present due to doubt, so the right of return is not established by doubt. And if he said 'you are free from every right I have against you', the defect is included, this is the chosen view, not the 'darak' (liability for claim). And in al-Sughra: The first buyer, if he absolved his seller from the defect after the second buyer has become aware of it, it is valid, and he does not return it to his seller when he returns it to him. And in al-Khaniyya: If he sold a slave-girl and said 'I am free from every defect in her', he is free from every defect in her. And if he said 'I am free from her', he is not free from anything regarding defects. And if he said 'I absolved you from every defect' and did not say 'in her', this is an absolution from every defect. And in it: He sold something on the basis that he is free from every defect, this is not an acknowledgment of the defect. But if he stipulated exemption from one or two defects, that is an acknowledgment of that defect. Explanation: If he sold two slaves for one price on condition that he is free from every defect in this specific slave, and he handed them over to the buyer, and one is reclaimed, and the buyer found... Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles multiple rulings on how to interpret various phrases used in exemptions from defects. It covers: 1) The phrase 'kull gha'ila' covers only theft, absconding, and fornication; 'kull da'' covers internal diseases; Abu Yusuf expands to everything. 2) If a cloth is accepted with its defects, the seller is free from tears but patches and darning are considered defects that are included? Actually, the text says patches and darning are included (i.e., as defects the seller is not liable for). 3) Specific colors: black teeth include red and green; ulcers include bleeding ones. 4) 'Athar' (scar) is included but cauterization (kai) is not. 5) If one says 'from every specific defect' and the item is one-eyed, the seller is not free because loss of an eye is not a defect but a lack; similarly a cut hand is not a defect but a severed finger is. 6) Exception wording: 'except ibaq' (absconding) - if the exception is indefinite ('illa ibaq'), it does not acknowledge present absconding because it could refer to future; so the buyer cannot return for that. 7) 'You are free from every right I have' includes defect but not darak (liability for eviction). 8) The first buyer's absolution after second buyer knows is valid. 9) Wording 'I am free from every defect in her' vs 'I am free from her' - the latter gives no exemption. 10) General exemption from all defects is not an acknowledgment of any specific defect, but exemption from one or two defects is acknowledgment of those defects. 11) A case of two slaves sold together with a specific exemption for one, and one is reclaimed, then the buyer finds a defect in the other. This is a detailed area of Hanafi sale law where wording determines liability.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaq): Absconding; running away by a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the seller says 'I am free from every defect except absconding' (illa ibaq) without mentioning the slave, what is the effect?
A) The seller acknowledges the absconding and the buyer can return.
B) The seller does not acknowledge present absconding and the buyer cannot return.
C) The slave must be returned anyway.
D) The contract is void.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite exception ('except absconding') is not an acknowledgment of a present defect; the buyer cannot return based on doubt.

General vs Specific Exemptions: Acknowledgment and Liability in Release Clauses
Multiple rulings on general vs specific exemptions and wording effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general exemption (not an acknowledgment) and specific exemption (acknowledgment) and apply the rulings on wording variations like 'in her' vs 'from her' and scope of 'every right I have'.

Core Idea
A general exemption from all defects is not an acknowledgment of any specific defect; a specific exemption from one or two defects is an acknowledgment of those defects.

The wording of a release clause determines whether it merely protects the seller from future claims or constitutes an admission of a specific existing defect. This lesson covers multiple sub-rulings: 'in her' vs 'from her', 'every right I have', and the effect of a sale of multiple items with a specific exemption for one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the baseline: general exemption is not an acknowledgment
If a seller says 'I am free from every defect' without specifying, this is not an admission that any defect exists. It is a protective clause.
2
Specific exemption: acknowledgment
If the seller exempts himself from one or two specific defects (e.g., 'from this scar and that blindness'), that is an acknowledgment that those defects exist, and the buyer can return if they are present.
3
Wording difference: 'in her' vs 'from her'
The text from al-Khaniyya says: if the seller says 'I am free from every defect in her', it is effective for all defects. If he says 'I am free from her' (without mentioning defects), it does not exempt him from any defect.
The word 'biha' (in her) is crucial; without it, the release does not apply to defects.
4
Scope of 'every right I have'
If the seller says 'you are free from every right I have against you', this includes defects (the buyer can claim defect) but does not include darak (liability for eviction). The chosen view is that defects are included.
Darak is a separate right, not considered a defect claim.
5
Application to multiple items sold together
If two slaves are sold for one price and the seller exempts himself from every defect in one specific slave, and that slave is later reclaimed from the buyer, the buyer still has the right to return the other slave if it has a defect because the exemption only applied to the specific slave.
The exemption is tied to the specific slave, not to the transaction.

درك (darak): Liability for recovery of the sold item if it is found to be owned by someone else; not included in general release from rights.

Dividing the Price When a Sold Slave Is Reclaimed and Another Has a Defect
Calculation of price distribution when one slave is reclaimed and the other has a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the share of the price a buyer can recover when one of two slaves sold in a single contract is reclaimed and the other has a pre-existing defect, using the Hanafi valuation method.

Core Idea
The price is apportioned by fair value, not equally: the reclaimed slave valued sound, the other valued with its defect.

When two items are sold for a lump sum and one is later proven to belong to a third party (reclaimed), the buyer can recover the portion of the price attributable to that item. But what if the other item has a defect? The Hanafi jurists provide a specific method: value the reclaimed item as if sound, and the other item with its known defect. Then the price is split proportionally. This lesson unpacks that calculation from al-Khaniyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contract
The seller sells two slaves for a single price, with a condition that he is free from one specific defect in one of the slaves.
2
Identify the events after sale
One slave is reclaimed (a third party proves ownership). The buyer then finds an additional defect in the other slave (the one with the original exemption condition).
3
State the rule for price division
The price is divided according to fair market values: the reclaimed slave is valued as sound (without any defect), and the other slave is valued with its one known defect.
4
Calculate the buyer's recourse
The buyer's recourse is the share of the price corresponding to the reclaimed slave. That share = (Value of reclaimed as sound) / (Value of reclaimed as sound + Value of other with defect) × Total price.
5
Note what remains
The buyer does not recover for the new defect in the other slave through this calculation; that may be a separate claim under khiyar al-ayb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو باع عبدين بثمن واحد على أنه بريء من عيب واحد بهذا ثم استحق أحدهما فوجد بالذي برئ عن عيب واحد عيباً فإنه يقسم الثمن عليهما على قيمة المستحق صحيحاً وعلى قيمة الآخر وبه عيب واحد فإذا عرفت حصة المستحق رجع المشتري على البائع بذلك. اهـ ما في الخانية.
And if he sold two slaves for one price on condition that he is free from one specific defect in this one, then one of them is reclaimed, and he (the buyer) finds in the one about which he was exempted from one defect a defect (i.e., an additional defect), then the price is divided between them: based on the value of the reclaimed (slave) as sound, and based on the value of the other with its one defect. Then when the share of the reclaimed is known, the buyer recourse against the seller for that (share). Thus in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a specific calculation from al-Khaniyya. The scenario: two slaves are sold for a single price, with a condition that the seller is exempt from one specific defect in one of them. Later, one of the slaves is reclaimed (e.g., because a third party proves ownership). The buyer then discovers an additional defect in the other slave (the one with the original exemption). The ruling: the price is divided between the two slaves proportionally to their values. The reclaimed slave is valued as sound (without any defect). The other slave is valued with its one defect (the known one). Then the share of the reclaimed slave in the price is calculated as (value of reclaimed as sound) / (value of reclaimed as sound + value of other with defect) * total price. The buyer then recovers that share from the seller because the slave was reclaimed. The buyer does not recover for the new defect in the other slave because the seller was already exempted from one defect in that slave; the new defect is separate? Actually the buyer found in that slave a defect, but the seller was only exempted from one defect. The buyer may have a claim for the new defect. The text ends abruptly, but the point is the division of price.

Key Terms
حصة المستحق (hissat al-mustahaqq): The share of the price allocated to the slave that was reclaimed (proved to be owned by another).
قيمة المستحق صحيحاً (qimat al-mustahaqq sahiihan): The fair market value of the reclaimed slave assuming it is free from defects.
الثمن الواحد (al-thaman al-wahid): A single lump-sum price for multiple items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When two slaves are sold for one price and one is reclaimed, how is the price divided to find the buyer's recourse?
A) Equally between the two slaves
B) Based on their purchase price ratio
C) Based on their value: the reclaimed as sound, the other with its known defect
D) The buyer recovers the entire price

Key Takeaway
When two items are sold for one price and one is reclaimed, the buyer recovers a proportion of the price based on the relative fair values of the items, not an equal split.

Compromise (Sulh) Over Defects in Sales
Rules for compromise between buyer and seller regarding defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on compromise (sulh) for defects in sales, distinguish valid from invalid compromises, and apply the rules regarding subsequent discovery of defects.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, compromise over defects is valid only if the seller pays the buyer; the buyer cannot pay to return the item.

This lesson covers additional rulings on compromise (sulh) related to defects in sales, which the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not include. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq furnishes several scenarios, clarifying when such compromises are valid, when the seller can recover compensation, and how subsequent discoveries of defects affect the settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of compromise
Determine whether the payment is from seller to buyer (reduction) or buyer to seller (to take back item). The first is valid, the second is not.
2
Apply the third-party rule
In cases involving a third party (like a fuller), check if their contribution is structured as a guarantee from the third party to the buyer first.
The text clarifies an apparent confusion.
3
Analyze subsequent events
When the defect later is found nonexistent or cured, the seller can recover the compensation. If another defect appears, the buyer has a choice.
Distinguish between seller's recourse and buyer's right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف الله تعالى الصلح عن العيب كما لم يذكر الكفالة به وقدمنا طرفاً منهما ولا بأس بذكرهما هنا تتميماً للفائدة. أما الأول فقدمنا أنه إن كان الدافع البائع والمبيع للمشتري كان جائزاً حطاً من الثمن وإن كان المشتري ليأخذه البائع لا . وفي فتح القدير: لو اصطلحا على أن يحط كل عشرة ويأخذ الأجنبي بما وراء المحطوط ورضي الأجنبي جاز، وجاز حط المشتري دون البائع. ولو قصر المشتري الثوب فإذا هو متخرق وقال المشتري لا أدري تحرق عند القصار أو عند البائع فاصطلحوا على أن يقبله المشتري ويرد عليه القصار درهماً والبائع درهماً جاز، وكذا لو اصطلحا على أن يقبله البائع ويدفع له القصار درهماً ويترك المشتري ذلك درهماً، قيل هذا غلط وتأويله أن يضمن القصار أولاً للمشتري ثم يدفع المشتري للبائع اهـ. وفي الصغرى : ادعى عيباً في جارية فأنكر فاصطلحا على مال على أن يبرئ المشتري البائع عن ذلك العيب ثم ظهر أنه لم يكن به هذا العيب أو كان بها لكن برئت وصاحت كان للبائع أن يرجع على المشتري ويأخذ ما أدى من البدل وعي القنية باع المشتري بعد الصلح عن العيب ثم زال العيب في يد المشتري الثاني ليس للبائع أن يرجع على مشتريه ببدل الصلح إن زال بمعالجة المشتري الأول وإلا فلا .اهـ. وفيها : اشترى حماراً ووجد به عيباً قديماً فأراد الرد فصولح بينهما بدينار وأخذه ثم وجد به عيباً آخر قديماً فله أن یرد الدينار وقيل مع يرجع بنقصان العيب .اهـ.
And the author (may Allah exalt him) did not mention compromise (sulh) about the defect, just as he did not mention suretyship (kafalah) for it, and we have presented a part of both, and there is no harm in mentioning them here to complete the benefit. As for the first: we have presented that if the payer is the seller and the sold item is with the buyer, it is valid as a reduction of the price; but if it is the buyer (giving something) so that the seller takes it back, it is not valid. And in Fath al-Qadir: If they compromised that he (the seller) would reduce every ten and a third party would take what is beyond the reduced amount, and the third party agrees, it is valid. And the reduction by the buyer is valid, not by the seller. And if the buyer shortened the cloth, and it turned out torn, and the buyer said 'I do not know if it was torn at the fuller's or with the seller', and they compromised that the buyer would accept it and the fuller would give him a dirham and the seller would give him a dirham, it is valid. Similarly, if they compromised that the seller would accept it and the fuller would pay him a dirham and the buyer would leave that dirham, it is said this is mistaken, and its interpretation is that the fuller first guarantees to the buyer, then the buyer pays to the seller. And in al-Sughra: He claimed a defect in a slave-girl and the seller denied, so they compromised for a sum of money on the condition that the buyer absolve the seller of that defect; then it appeared that the slave-girl did not have this defect, or she had it but recovered and became healthy, then the seller has the right to recourse against the buyer and take what he paid of the compensation. And if the buyer, after the compromise about the defect, sold (the slave-girl), then the defect disappeared in the hands of the second buyer, the seller does not have recourse against his buyer for the compensation if it disappeared due to the treatment of the first buyer; otherwise, he does. And in it: He bought a donkey and found an old defect, so he wanted to return it, so they compromised between them for a dinar, and he took it, then he found another old defect, then he has the right to return the dinar, and it is said: he recourses for the deficiency of the defect.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage adds rulings on compromise (sulh) regarding defects, which the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not include. The first ruling: if the seller pays the buyer a sum to settle a defect claim, it is valid as a reduction of price. If the buyer pays the seller to take back the item, it is not valid because the buyer cannot repurchase at a discount. Then from Fath al-Qadir: a case where the seller reduces by a fixed amount and a third party gets the surplus, it is valid with third party consent. The reduction is valid from buyer but not seller? Actually it says 'Jaz hatt al-mushtari duna al-ba'i': the buyer's reduction is valid, not the seller? That seems opposite to the first rule. Context: the case of cloth: if the buyer shortens it and it tears, they compromise with contributions from fuller and seller. Next from al-Sughra: a case where the buyer claims a defect, they compromise on payment by seller for absolution, then it turns out the defect did not exist or was cured. The seller can recover the compensation from the buyer. If the buyer later sells the item and the defect disappears due to the first buyer's treatment, the original seller cannot recover from the second buyer. If the defect disappears naturally, he can. Finally, if a compromise is made for one defect and another old defect is found, the buyer can return the compensation or claim deficiency.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Compromise: a transaction to settle a dispute by mutual agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer claims a defect and the seller denies, they compromise with the buyer absolving the seller for a sum, but later it appears the defect never existed. What happens?
A) The compromise stands as is
B) The seller can recover the sum from the buyer
C) The buyer can return the item for full refund
D) The compromise is void

Key Takeaway
A compromise over a defect in a sale can be valid or invalid depending on who pays whom, and subsequent discoveries of defect or cure can allow the seller to recover compensation or the buyer to seek further adjustment.

Summary of When Option of Defect Falls
Listing the grounds for forfeiture of the option of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four grounds that forfeit the option of defect and apply the Hanafi rules of acknowledgment to determine if the option is retained.

Core Idea
The option of defect is forfeited by prior knowledge, consent, exemption clause, or compromise; vague disclaimers do not waive it.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq summarizes the four clear-cut grounds that cause the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) to fall. It then delves into nuanced cases from Jami' al-Fusulayn regarding the buyer's acknowledgment and witness testimony, showing how general statements do not forfeit the option while specific disclaimers do.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four forfeiture grounds
Identify the four ways the option of defect is forfeited: knowledge at time of sale or receipt, consent after such knowledge, stipulating exemption from all defects, and compromise.
2
Distinguish general vs. specific acknowledgment
Note that if the buyer says 'there is no defect' without specification, it is not an acknowledgment; he may still return. But specifying a defect (e.g., 'it is not absconding') is an acknowledgment and forfeits the option for that defect.
Key distinction: specificity matters.
3
Apply the witness testimony rule
Understand that if witnesses testify that the seller sold with a general exemption from all defects, that testimony does not prevent the witness from later returning the item if he buys it and finds a defect. However, if the testimony is specific (e.g., free from absconding), the witness cannot later return for that defect.
The wording of the exemption in testimony affects the witness's rights as a buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى هنا ظهر أن خيار العيب يسقط بالعلم به وقت البيع أو وقت القبض والرضا به بعدهما أو اشتراط البراءة من كل عيب أو الصلح على شيء. وفي جامع الفصولين: لو اشتراه على أن عيبه حادث فظهر أنه قديم لا يرده أو الإقرار بأن لا عيب به إذا في الصغرى : إذا قال المشتري ليس به عيب لا يكون إقراراً بانتفاء العيوب حتى عينه . قال لو وجد به عيباً كان له أن يرده ولو عين فقال ليس بابق كان إقراراً بانتفاء الإباق، وكذا لو شهدوا أنه باع بشرط البراءة من كل عيب لا يكون إقراراً من الشهود بالعيب حتى لو اشتراه الشاهد فوجد به عيباً كان له أن يرد وكذا لو شهدوا على أنه باعه على أنه بريء من الإباق ثم اشتراه الشاهد فوجده آبقاً فله الرد ، ولو على أنه بريء من إباقه فليس للشاهد رده بإباقه
And up to here it has become apparent that the option of defect falls by: knowledge of it at the time of sale or at the time of receipt, and consent to it after them, or stipulating exemption from every defect, or compromise on something. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he bought it on the basis that its defect is recent (hadith), then it appears to be old (qadim), he does not return it. Or the acknowledgment that there is no defect in it, as in al-Sughra: When the buyer says 'there is no defect in it', it is not an acknowledgment of the absence of defects until he specifies. He said: if he finds a defect, he may return it. And if he specifies and says 'it is not absconding', it is an acknowledgment of the absence of absconding. Similarly, if witnesses testify that he sold with the condition of exemption from every defect, that is not an acknowledgment by the witnesses of the defect, so if the witness buys it and finds a defect, he may return it. And similarly, if they testify that he sold it on the basis that he is free from absconding, then the witness buys it and finds it an absconder, he has the right to return. But if (he testified that he sold it) on the basis that he is free from its absconding, then the witness does not have the right to return it due to its absconding.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a summary passage listing the four ways the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) is forfeited: (1) knowing the defect at the time of sale or receipt and still accepting, (2) consenting after knowledge, (3) stipulating exemption from all defects, (4) compromising on something. Then additional rules from Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the buyer buys on the understanding that a defect is recent but it turns out old, he cannot return. Also, if the buyer says 'there is no defect' without specification, it is not an acknowledgment; he can still return if a defect appears. But if he specifies, e.g., 'it is not absconding', that is an acknowledgment. Similarly for witnesses: testifying that the seller sold with a general exemption does not bar the witness from returning if he later buys and finds a defect. But if the witness testifies to a specific exemption (e.g., from absconding), and the wording links the exemption to the slave ('barī' min ibāqih'), then the witness cannot later return for that defect if he buys.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The option to return a defective item due to an unknown defect present at the time of sale.
إباق (ibāq): Absconding; the act of a slave running away. Mentioned as a specific defect that, if denied specifically, becomes an acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the summary, which of the following does NOT cause the option of defect to fall?
A) Knowledge of the defect at time of purchase
B) Stipulating exemption from all defects
C) The buyer's statement 'there is no defect' without specification
D) Compromise on something

Key Takeaway
Forfeiture of the option of defect requires specific knowledge, explicit consent, an exemption clause, or a compromise; vague disclaimers do not waive the right.

Items Whose Sale is Prohibited
List of prohibited items in sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the items whose sale is invalid in the Hanafi school and state the ruling that such sales are fasid.

Core Idea
Sale of impure or non-commodity items is invalid and has no legal effect.

This part of the passage lists specific items that cannot be sold under Islamic law. The Hanafi school considers these items not to be valid property (mal mutaqawwim), so any sale contract involving them is void (fasid).

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited items
Read the first part of the source text and list all eight items explicitly mentioned: dead meat, blood, pig, wine, free person, mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatib.
These items are either impure or not owned property.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
Each item lacks the quality of being 'mal mutaqawwim' (valuable property) according to the Hanafi school. Therefore, any sale contract is automatically invalid (fasid).
Prohibition is not merely for impurity but also for non-ownership.
3
Note the legal consequence
If such a sale occurs, the contract is void; the buyer must return the item and the seller must return the price.
No ownership transfer occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد لم يجز بيع الميتة والدم والخنزير والخمر والحر والمدبر وأم الولد والمكاتب فلو هلكوا عند اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : البائعة إذا تزوجت المشتري على أرش العيب صح وكان إقراراً منها بالعيب، وكذا البائع إذا اشترى منه أرش العيب كان إقراراً به بخلاف الصلح عنه لا يكون إقراراً به. وأما ضمانه ففي البزازية : اشترى عبداً وضمن له رجل عيوبه فاطلع على عيب فرده لا ضمان عليه عند الإمام لأنه ضمان العهدة، وعلى قول الثاني يضمن لأنه ضمان العيوب. وإن ضمن السرقة أو الحرية أو الجنون أو العمى فوجده كذلك ضمن الثمن للمشتري، وإن مات عنده قبل الرد قضى على البائع بالنقص ورجع به على الضامن. ولو ضمن له بحصة ما يجده من العيوب من الثمن فهو جائز عند الإمام، فإن رده المشتري رجع بكل الثمن على الضامن، وإن لم يرده وقضى بالنقص على البائع رجع على الضامن كما يرجع على البائع. وعن الثاني قال رجل للمشتري ضمنت لك عماه فكان أعمى فرده لم يرجع على الضامن بشيء، ولو قال إن كان أعمى فعلى حصة العمى من الثمن فرده ضمن حصة العمى، ولو وجد به عيباً فقال رجل للمشتري ضمنت لك هذا العيب فالضمان باطل اهـ والله أعلم.
Chapter of Invalid Sale: It is not permissible to sell dead meat, blood, pig, wine, a free person, a mudabbar, an umm walad, a mukatib. If they perish... etc. In al-Walwalijiyyah: If the female seller marries the buyer regarding the compensation for a defect, it is valid and is an admission of the defect by her. Similarly, if the seller buys the compensation for a defect from him, it is an admission, unlike settlement (sulh) which is not an admission. As for the guaranty (daman), in al-Bazzaziyyah: If one buys a slave and a man guarantees its defects for him, then he discovers a defect and returns him, there is no liability on the guarantor according to Imam Abu Hanifa because it is a guaranty of warranty (daman al-uhdah). According to the second (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is liable because it is a guaranty of defects. If he guarantees theft, freedom, insanity, or blindness, and finds it to be so, he is liable for the price to the buyer. If the slave dies with him before return, the seller is ordered to pay the deficiency and he recovers from the guarantor. If he guarantees for him a portion of the price corresponding to the defects he finds, it is permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa. If the buyer returns him, he recovers the entire price from the guarantor. If he does not return him and the seller is ordered to pay the deficiency, he recovers from the guarantor as he recovers from the seller. According to the second, a man said to the buyer: I guarantee for you his blindness; so he was blind and he returned him; he does not recover anything from the guarantor. If he said: If he is blind, then the portion of the price for blindness is on me; so he returned him; he guarantees the portion for blindness. If he found a defect and a man said to the buyer: I guarantee for you this defect; then the guaranty is void. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage contains two main sections. The first part lists items whose sale is prohibited: dead meat, blood, pig, wine, a free person, a mudabbar, an umm walad, and a mukatib. These sales are invalid. The second part discusses the ruling on a third party's guaranty of defects in a sale. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone guarantees the defects of a sold slave, and the buyer discovers a defect and returns the slave, the guarantor is not liable because it is considered a guaranty of warranty (daman al-uhdah) which is not binding. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it a guaranty of defects and thus binding. The text then details various scenarios: if the guaranty covers specific defects like theft or freedom, the guarantor becomes liable for the price if the defect is found. Also, if the guarantor stipulates a portion of the price corresponding to defects, that is valid according to Abu Hanifa, and the buyer can recover the full price from the guarantor upon return of the slave. However, if the guarantor merely says 'I guarantee this defect' without specifying money, the guaranty is void.

Key Terms
الميتة (al-maytah): Dead animal not slaughtered Islamically; its sale is prohibited.
الحر (al-hurr): Free person; cannot be sold.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death; cannot be sold.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave mother of her master's child; cannot be sold.
المكاتب (al-mukatib): A slave who has a contract of manumission; cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a prohibited sale in the passage?
A) Dead meat
B) Blood
C) Forbidden meat from a not-slaughtered animal
D) Pork

Key Takeaway
Sale of impure or non-owned items is invalid (fasid).

Third-Party Guaranty of Defects
Rulings on third-party guaranty of defects with intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a binding and non-binding guaranty of defects according to the Hanafi school, and apply the rulings to specific scenarios.

Core Idea
A general guarantee of defects is not binding per Abu Hanifa, but a specific guarantee of a particular defect is binding.

This part of the passage discusses the legal effect of a third party's guaranty of defects in a sale. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa considers a general guarantee (daman al-uhdah) as non-binding, while the Sahibayn consider it binding as a guarantee of defects (daman al-uyub). The text provides several scenarios to illustrate the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of guaranty
Distinguish between 'daman al-uhdah' (non-binding warranty) and 'daman al-uyub' (binding guarantee of defects). The text shows that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a general guarantee of 'defects' is considered daman al-uhdah and thus not binding.
The Sahibayn treat it as daman al-uyub, so it is binding.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage states: 'no liability on the guarantor according to Imam... because it is daman al-uhdah; according to the second he is liable because it is daman al-uyub.' The 'second' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Apply to specific scenarios
Read the details: if the guarantor specifically guarantees theft, freedom, insanity, or blindness, he is liable (that is binding). If he guarantees a portion of the price corresponding to defects, it is valid according to Imam. If he says 'I guarantee this defect' without specifying monetary compensation, the guaranty is void.
Specificity matters.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Though the text does not specify the relied-upon position, later Hanafi practice often adopts the Sahibayn's view for practical reasons.
Check later sources like Ibn Abidin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد لم يجز بيع الميتة والدم والخنزير والخمر والحر والمدبر وأم الولد والمكاتب فلو هلكوا عند اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : البائعة إذا تزوجت المشتري على أرش العيب صح وكان إقراراً منها بالعيب، وكذا البائع إذا اشترى منه أرش العيب كان إقراراً به بخلاف الصلح عنه لا يكون إقراراً به. وأما ضمانه ففي البزازية : اشترى عبداً وضمن له رجل عيوبه فاطلع على عيب فرده لا ضمان عليه عند الإمام لأنه ضمان العهدة، وعلى قول الثاني يضمن لأنه ضمان العيوب. وإن ضمن السرقة أو الحرية أو الجنون أو العمى فوجده كذلك ضمن الثمن للمشتري، وإن مات عنده قبل الرد قضى على البائع بالنقص ورجع به على الضامن. ولو ضمن له بحصة ما يجده من العيوب من الثمن فهو جائز عند الإمام، فإن رده المشتري رجع بكل الثمن على الضامن، وإن لم يرده وقضى بالنقص على البائع رجع على الضامن كما يرجع على البائع. وعن الثاني قال رجل للمشتري ضمنت لك عماه فكان أعمى فرده لم يرجع على الضامن بشيء، ولو قال إن كان أعمى فعلى حصة العمى من الثمن فرده ضمن حصة العمى، ولو وجد به عيباً فقال رجل للمشتري ضمنت لك هذا العيب فالضمان باطل اهـ والله أعلم.
Chapter of Invalid Sale: It is not permissible to sell dead meat, blood, pig, wine, a free person, a mudabbar, an umm walad, a mukatib. If they perish... etc. In al-Walwalijiyyah: If the female seller marries the buyer regarding the compensation for a defect, it is valid and is an admission of the defect by her. Similarly, if the seller buys the compensation for a defect from him, it is an admission, unlike settlement (sulh) which is not an admission. As for the guaranty (daman), in al-Bazzaziyyah: If one buys a slave and a man guarantees its defects for him, then he discovers a defect and returns him, there is no liability on the guarantor according to Imam Abu Hanifa because it is a guaranty of warranty (daman al-uhdah). According to the second (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is liable because it is a guaranty of defects. If he guarantees theft, freedom, insanity, or blindness, and finds it to be so, he is liable for the price to the buyer. If the slave dies with him before return, the seller is ordered to pay the deficiency and he recovers from the guarantor. If he guarantees for him a portion of the price corresponding to the defects he finds, it is permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa. If the buyer returns him, he recovers the entire price from the guarantor. If he does not return him and the seller is ordered to pay the deficiency, he recovers from the guarantor as he recovers from the seller. According to the second, a man said to the buyer: I guarantee for you his blindness; so he was blind and he returned him; he does not recover anything from the guarantor. If he said: If he is blind, then the portion of the price for blindness is on me; so he returned him; he guarantees the portion for blindness. If he found a defect and a man said to the buyer: I guarantee for you this defect; then the guaranty is void. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage contains two main sections. The first part lists items whose sale is prohibited: dead meat, blood, pig, wine, a free person, a mudabbar, an umm walad, and a mukatib. These sales are invalid. The second part discusses the ruling on a third party's guaranty of defects in a sale. The text then details various scenarios: if the guaranty covers specific defects like theft or freedom, the guarantor becomes liable for the price if the defect is found. Also, if the guarantor stipulates a portion of the price corresponding to defects, that is valid according to Abu Hanifa, and the buyer can recover the full price from the guarantor upon return of the slave. However, if the guarantor merely says 'I guarantee this defect' without specifying money, the guaranty is void.

Key Terms
ضمان العهدة (daman al-uhdah): A guaranty of warranty that is not binding according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
ضمان العيوب (daman al-uyub): A binding guaranty of defects; considered binding by the Sahibayn.
أرش العيب (arsh al-ayb): Compensation paid for a defect in the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on a general guaranty of defects for a sold slave?
A) The guarantor is liable for the entire price.
B) The guarantor is liable only for the portion of the price corresponding to the defect.
C) The guarantor is not liable because it is a guaranty of warranty (daman al-uhdah).
D) The guaranty is void.

Key Takeaway
A general guarantee of defects is non-binding per Abu Hanifa, but specific guarantees are binding.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
The Three Categories of Prohibited Sales: Fasid, Batil, and Makruh Tahrimi
Definitions of fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi with linguistic and terminological analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi sales and distinguish between them based on legitimacy of origin and description.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh uniquely distinguishes fasid (valid in essence, flawed in description) from batil (void in both) and makruh tahrimi (valid but externally prohibited).

In this lesson, we dissect the Hanafi threefold classification of prohibited sales. The source text provides linguistic and terminological definitions of fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi. Understanding these categories is essential because the legal effects differ: fasid may confer ownership upon possession, batil does not, and makruh tahrimi is valid but sinful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning of fasada
Start with the linguistic root: 'fasada' means to spoil, used for meat that goes bad but is still usable.
2
Define fasid terminologically
A fasid sale is legitimate in its origin (asl) because the subject matter is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), but invalid in its description (wasf) due to a defect.
This intermediate category is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Contrast with batil
A batil sale is illegitimate in both origin and description; it confers no ownership even with possession. Linguistically, 'batila' means to lose effect.
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) treat fasid and batil as synonymous.
4
Define makruh tahrimi
This is a sale legitimate in origin and description but prohibited due to an external factor, e.g., selling during Friday prayer call. It is valid but sinful.
It can be considered a subset of fasid in a broader sense.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school does not differentiate between fasid and batil; both are void. The Hanafi distinction has practical legal consequences.
This shows the importance of madhhab-specific terminology.

Source Text

باب البيع الفاسد أخره لكونه عقداً مخالفاً للدين كما في فتح القدير. وصرح الولوالجي رحمه الله تعالى من الفصل السابع بأنه معصية يجب رفعها وسيأتي في باب الربا أن كل عقد فاسد فهو ربا. والفاسد له معنيان: لغوي واصطلاحي . فالأول فسد كنصر وعقد وكرم فساداً وفسوداً ضد صلح فهو فاسد وفسيد من فسدي ولم يسمع انفسد والفساد أخذ المال ظلماً والجدب. والمفسدة ضد المصلحة، وفسده تفسيداً أفسده وتفاسدوا قطعوا أرحامهم، واستفسد ضد كذا في القاموس. وفي المصباح واعلم أن الفساد إلى الحيوان أسرع منه إلى النبات، وإلى النبات أسرع منه إلى الجماد لأن الرطوبة في الحيوان أكثر من الرطوبة في النبات، وقد يعرض للطبيعة عارض فتعجز الحرارة بسببه عن جريانها في المجاري الطبيعية الدافعة لعوارض العفونة فتكون العفونة بالحيوان أشد تثبتاً منها بالنبات فيسرع إليه الفساد الحكمة في قول الفقهاء يقدم القاضي ما يتسارع إليه الفساد فيبدأ ببيع الحيوان . استصلح؛ فهذه هي باب البيع الفاسد قوله : (وهو الحق) ينبغي أن يستثني من ذلك بيع المكره فإنه موقوف على إجازته مع أنه فاسد يصح أصلا ويتعدى بالهمزة والتضعيف والمفسدة خلاف المصلحة وجمعها المفاسد اهـ. وحاصله أنه ما تغير وصفه ويمكن الانتفاع به لما في البناية : يقال فسد اللحم إذا أنتن مع بقاء الانتفاع به . وأما الثاني قالوا هو ما كان مشروعاً بأصله لا بوصفه ولا يخفى مناسبته للمعنى اللغوي. و مرادهم من مشروعية أصله كونه مالاً متقوماً لا جوازه وصحته فإن كونه فاسداً يمنع صحته. ولقد تسمح في البناية حيث عرفه بأنه ما لا يصح وصفاً فإنه يفيد أنه ولا صحة للفاسد وإنما أطلقوا المشروعية على الأصل نظراً إلى أنه لو خلى عن الوصف لكان مشروعاً وإلا فمع اتصافه بالوصف المنهي عنه لا يبقى مشروعاً أصلاً. والمراد بالفاسد هنا ما يعم الباطل لأنهم يذكرون في هذا الباب ما يعم الباطل أيضاً فالمراد به ما لم يكن مشروعاً بوصفه أعم من أن يكون مشروعاً بأصله أو لا والبياعات المنهي عنها ثلاثة : فاسد وباطل ومكروه تحريماً. فالفاسد بيناه، وأما الباطل فله معنيان: لغوي واصطلاحي. فالأول يقال بطل الشيء يبطل بطلاً وبطولاً وبطلاناً بضم الأوائل فسد أو سقط حكمه فهو باطل والجمع بواطل أو أباطيل على غير قياس؛ كذا في المصباح. ويقال للحم إذا صار بحيث لا ينتفع به للدود أو للسوس بطل، وإذا أنتن فسد كما في فتح القدير وأما الثاني فهو ما لا يكون مشروعاً لا بأصله ولا بوصفه وحكمه عدم إفادة الحكم وهو الملك قبضه أو لا، وفيه مناسبة للمعنى للغوي لأنه بمعنى ما سقط حكمه، وحكم الفاسد ما لا يفيده بمجرده بل بالقبض. وأما المكروه فهو لغة خلاف المحبوب واصطلاحاً ما نهي عنه لمجاور كالبيع عند أذان الجمعة نهي عنه للصلاة. وعرفه في البناية بما كان مشروعاً بأصله ووصفه لكن نهى عنه لمجاور اهـ . نهي ويمكن إدخاله تحت الفاسد أيضاً على إرادة الأعم وهو ما نهي عنه فيشمل الثلاثة. والفساد بالمعنى الأعم يثبت بأسباب منها : الجهالة المفضية إلى المنازعة في المبيع أو الثمن، ومنه العجز عن التسليم إلا بضرر ومنها الغرر، ومنها شرط خارج عن الشرع، ومنها عدم المالية أو التقوم، ومنها عدم الوجود ومنها عدم القدرة على التسليم. وأما البيع الجائز الذي فيه فثلاثة : نافذ لازم ونافذ ليس بلازم وموقوف. فالأول ما كان مشروعاً بأصله ووصفه ولم يتعلق به حق الغير ولا خيار فيه والثاني ما لم يتعلق به حق الغير وفيه خيار والموقوف ما تعلق به حق الغير، وهو إما ملك الغير أو حق بالبيع لغير المالك. وحصره في الخلاصة في خمسة عشر : بيع العبد والصبي المحجورين موقوف على إجازة المولى والأب أو الوصي وبيع غير الرشيد موقوف على إجازة القاضي وبيع المرهون والمستأجر. وما في مزارعة الغير موقوف على إجازة المرتهن والمستأجر والمزارع وبيع البائع المبيع بعد القبض من غير المشتري موقوف على إجازة المشتري وقبل القبض في المنقول لا ينعقد أصلاً، وبيع المرتد عند الإمام والبيع برقمه وبما باع فلآن والمشتري لا يعلم موقوف على العلم في المجلس، وبيع فيه البحر الرائق/ ج ٦/ م٨
Chapter of Invalid Sale: It is placed here because an invalid contract is contrary to religion, as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Walwaliji stated in the seventh section that it is a sin that must be removed, and it will come in the chapter of riba that every invalid contract is riba. Al-Fasid has two meanings: linguistic and terminological. Linguistically, it is like 'fasada' (to spoil), opposite of 'salih' (good). It means taking wealth unjustly and drought. Al-Mafsadah is opposite of maslahah. In al-Misbah: corruption occurs faster in animals than plants, and faster in plants than inanimate objects, because moisture is greater in animals. The scholars' wisdom in prioritizing sales of animals is due to rapid corruption. Then: The saying (and it is the truth) should except the sale of a coerced person, which is suspended on his consent, even though it is invalid. It is transitive with hamzah and tashdid. Al-Mafsadah is opposite of maslahah, its plural is mafasid. Its gist is that its description has changed but benefit is possible, as in al-Binayah: meat is called fasid when it stinks while still usable. The second (terminological) meaning: it is what is legitimate in its origin but not in its description. Their intent by 'legitimate in origin' is that it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility or validity. The inherent description prevents validity. In al-Binayah it is defined as what is not valid in description, implying no validity for the fasid. They attribute legitimacy to the origin because if stripped of the description it would be legitimate. Here, fasid is used broadly to include batil, because this chapter covers both. So fasid is what is not legitimate in its description, whether or not legitimate in its origin. Prohibited sales are three: fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi. Fasid has been discussed. As for batil, it has two meanings. Linguistically: 'batila' means to become invalid or to lose its effect; its plural is bawatil or abatil. Terminologically: it is what is not legitimate in either origin or description, and its ruling is that it does not bring about the legal effect (ownership), whether possession is taken or not. This aligns with the linguistic meaning of having no effect. The ruling of fasid is that it does not bring effect by itself, but only with possession. As for makruh, linguistically it is opposite of mahbub, and terminologically it is what is prohibited due to an external factor, like selling during Friday prayer call. In al-Binayah, it is defined as what is legitimate in origin and description but prohibited due to an external factor. It can be included under fasid in a general sense as 'what is prohibited', encompassing all three. Causes of fasad in the general sense include: ambiguity leading to dispute, inability to deliver without harm, gharar, condition contrary to Sharia, lack of monetary value, non-existence, and inability to deliver. As for valid sales, they are three: binding and effective, effective but not binding, and suspended. The first is legitimate in origin and description, with no third-party right and no option. The second has no third-party right but has an option. The suspended is one with a third-party right, either ownership of another or a right to sell. Al-Khulasa enumerates fifteen: sale of a slave or minor under guardianship is suspended on permission of the guardian; sale of a non-discerning person is suspended on permission of the judge; sale of a mortgaged or leased item or one in sharecropping is suspended on permission of the mortgagee, lessee, or sharecropper; sale by the seller of the sold item after possession but not to the buyer is suspended on the buyer's permission; before possession of movables, it does not take effect at all; sale of an apostate according to Imam Abu Hanifa, sale with its number, and sale of 'what so-and-so sold' where the buyer is unaware are suspended on knowledge during the session; and a sale with the reference to al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol.6 p.8.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi classification of prohibited sales. It begins by explaining why the chapter on invalid sales is placed after other chapters: because an invalid contract opposes the religion. The author then comprehensively defines the terms 'fasid' (invalid), 'batil' (void), and 'makruh tahrimi' (prohibitively disliked). Linguistically, 'fasada' means to spoil, and is used for meat that goes bad but can still be used. Terminologically, a fasid sale is one that is valid in its essence (asl) because the subject matter is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), but invalid in its description (wasf) due to a defect in the contract, such as an illegal condition. A batil sale, on the other hand, is invalid in both essence and description, meaning it confers no ownership even if possession is taken. The third category, makruh tahrimi, is a sale that is valid in itself but prohibited due to an external factor, like selling during the Friday prayer call. The text then lists six causes of fasad: ambiguity leading to dispute, inability to deliver without harm, gharar (uncertainty), a condition contrary to Sharia, lack of monetary value, non-existence of the object, and inability to deliver. Finally, it classifies valid sales into three types: binding (nāfidh lāzim), non-binding but effective (nāfidh ghayr lāzim), and suspended (mawqūf). The suspended sale is one that depends on the permission of a third party, and fifteen examples are given from al-Khulasa, such as the sale of a minor's property pending guardian's approval, or a mortgaged property pending the mortgagee's consent.

Key Terms
فاسد (fasid): A contract valid in its essence (mal mutaqawwim) but invalid in its description (due to a defect).
باطل (batil): A contract invalid in both essence and description; confers no ownership even with possession.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahrimi): A contract valid in itself but prohibited due to an external factor (e.g., during Friday prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a fasid sale from a batil sale?
A) Fasid is valid in origin, batil is not.
B) Fasid is prohibited by an external factor, batil is inherently prohibited.
C) Fasid requires possession for ownership, batil never gives ownership.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh uniquely categorizes prohibited sales into fasid (flawed description), batil (void in essence), and makruh tahrimi (valid but prohibited externally).

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Causes of Fasad and Classification of Valid Sales
Causes of fasad and classification of valid sales (binding, non-binding, suspended).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six causes of fasad in a sale and classify valid sales into binding, non-binding, and suspended.

Core Idea
The causes of fasad (like ambiguity and inability to deliver) invalidate the description; valid sales are either binding, optional, or suspended pending third-party consent.

After defining the categories of prohibited sales, the source text enumerates the specific causes that make a sale fasid and then turns to valid sales. In this lesson, you will learn the six causes of fasad and understand how valid sales are divided into three types: binding (nāfidh lāzim), non-binding but effective (nāfidh ghayr lāzim), and suspended (mawqūf). Mastering these classifications helps you analyze real sales contracts.

Guided Path
1
List the six causes of fasad
From the text: jahalah (ambiguity leading to dispute), inability to deliver without harm, gharar (uncertainty), a condition contrary to Sharia, lack of monetary value or non-valuable object, non-existence of the object, and inability to deliver.
Some causes overlap; e.g., lack of existence also implies inability to deliver.
2
Explain each cause briefly
Ambiguity in price or item leads to dispute; inability to deliver causes loss; gharar creates uncertainty; illegal condition contradicts Sharia; lack of monetary value means the object is not mal mutaqawwim; non-existence means the object does not exist at the time of contract.
These causes are not exhaustive; they are the main ones.
3
Identify the three types of valid sales
Binding (nāfidh lāzim): no third-party right, no option. Non-binding (nāfidh ghayr lāzim): no third-party right, but option exists (e.g., khiyar al-shart). Suspended (mawqūf): dependent on third-party consent.
Suspended sales have many subcategories as listed in al-Khulasa.
4
Understand the concept of suspended sales
A suspended sale is valid in origin and description but lacks full effect until a third party (e.g., guardian, mortgagee) gives permission. Fifteen examples are cited: sale of minor's property, mortgaged property, etc.
The sale of a minor's property is suspended on the guardian's permission.

Source Text
