فيه والثاني ما لم يتعلق به حق الغير وفيه خيار والموقوف ما تعلق به حق الغير، وهو إما ملك الغير أو حق بالبيع لغير المالك. وحصره في الخلاصة في خمسة عشر : بيع العبد والصبي المحجورين موقوف على إجازة المولى والأب أو الوصي وبيع غير الرشيد موقوف على إجازة القاضي وبيع المرهون والمستأجر. وما في مزارعة الغير موقوف على إجازة المرتهن والمستأجر والمزارع وبيع البائع المبيع بعد القبض من غير المشتري موقوف على إجازة المشتري وقبل القبض في المنقول لا ينعقد أصلاً، وبيع المرتد عند الإمام والبيع برقمه وبما باع فلآن والمشتري لا يعلم موقوف على العلم في المجلس، وبيع فيه البحر الرائق/ ج ٦/ م٨
Chapter of Invalid Sale: It is placed here because an invalid contract is contrary to religion, as in Fath al-Qadir. Al-Walwaliji stated in the seventh section that it is a sin that must be removed, and it will come in the chapter of riba that every invalid contract is riba. Al-Fasid has two meanings: linguistic and terminological. Linguistically, it is like 'fasada' (to spoil), opposite of 'salih' (good). It means taking wealth unjustly and drought. Al-Mafsadah is opposite of maslahah. In al-Misbah: corruption occurs faster in animals than plants, and faster in plants than inanimate objects, because moisture is greater in animals. The scholars' wisdom in prioritizing sales of animals is due to rapid corruption. Then: The saying (and it is the truth) should except the sale of a coerced person, which is suspended on his consent, even though it is invalid. It is transitive with hamzah and tashdid. Al-Mafsadah is opposite of maslahah, its plural is mafasid. Its gist is that its description has changed but benefit is possible, as in al-Binayah: meat is called fasid when it stinks while still usable. The second (terminological) meaning: it is what is legitimate in its origin but not in its description. Their intent by 'legitimate in origin' is that it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility or validity. The inherent description prevents validity. In al-Binayah it is defined as what is not valid in description, implying no validity for the fasid. They attribute legitimacy to the origin because if stripped of the description it would be legitimate. Here, fasid is used broadly to include batil, because this chapter covers both. So fasid is what is not legitimate in its description, whether or not legitimate in its origin. Prohibited sales are three: fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi. Fasid has been discussed. As for batil, it has two meanings. Linguistically: 'batila' means to become invalid or to lose its effect; its plural is bawatil or abatil. Terminologically: it is what is not legitimate in either origin or description, and its ruling is that it does not bring about the legal effect (ownership), whether possession is taken or not. This aligns with the linguistic meaning of having no effect. The ruling of fasid is that it does not bring effect by itself, but only with possession. As for makruh, linguistically it is opposite of mahbub, and terminologically it is what is prohibited due to an external factor, like selling during Friday prayer call. In al-Binayah, it is defined as what is legitimate in origin and description but prohibited due to an external factor. It can be included under fasid in a general sense as 'what is prohibited', encompassing all three. Causes of fasad in the general sense include: ambiguity leading to dispute, inability to deliver without harm, gharar, condition contrary to Sharia, lack of monetary value, non-existence, and inability to deliver. As for valid sales, they are three: binding and effective, effective but not binding, and suspended. The first is legitimate in origin and description, with no third-party right and no option. The second has no third-party right but has an option. The suspended is one with a third-party right, either ownership of another or a right to sell. Al-Khulasa enumerates fifteen: sale of a slave or minor under guardianship is suspended on permission of the guardian; sale of a non-discerning person is suspended on permission of the judge; sale of a mortgaged or leased item or one in sharecropping is suspended on permission of the mortgagee, lessee, or sharecropper; sale by the seller of the sold item after possession but not to the buyer is suspended on the buyer's permission; before possession of movables, it does not take effect at all; sale of an apostate according to Imam Abu Hanifa, sale with its number, and sale of 'what so-and-so sold' where the buyer is unaware are suspended on knowledge during the session; and a sale with the reference to al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol.6 p.8.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi classification of prohibited sales. It begins by explaining why the chapter on invalid sales is placed after other chapters: because an invalid contract opposes the religion. The author then comprehensively defines the terms 'fasid' (invalid), 'batil' (void), and 'makruh tahrimi' (prohibitively disliked). Linguistically, 'fasada' means to spoil, and is used for meat that goes bad but can still be used. Terminologically, a fasid sale is one that is valid in its essence (asl) because the subject matter is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim), but invalid in its description (wasf) due to a defect in the contract, such as an illegal condition. A batil sale, on the other hand, is invalid in both essence and description, meaning it confers no ownership even if possession is taken. The third category, makruh tahrimi, is a sale that is valid in itself but prohibited due to an external factor, like selling during the Friday prayer call. The text then lists six causes of fasad: ambiguity leading to dispute, inability to deliver without harm, gharar (uncertainty), a condition contrary to Sharia, lack of monetary value, non-existence of the object, and inability to deliver. Finally, it classifies valid sales into three types: binding (nāfidh lāzim), non-binding but effective (nāfidh ghayr lāzim), and suspended (mawqūf). The suspended sale is one that depends on the permission of a third party, and fifteen examples are given from al-Khulasa, such as the sale of a minor's property pending guardian's approval, or a mortgaged property pending the mortgagee's consent.

Key Terms
جهالة (jahalah): Ambiguity in the contract that leads to dispute, a cause of fasad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a cause of fasad?
A) Ambiguity leading to dispute.
B) Inability to deliver without harm.
C) Presence of a valid option (khiyar).
D) Condition contrary to Sharia.

Key Takeaway
Fasad is caused by defects like ambiguity and gharar; valid sales are either binding, non-binding with options, or suspended pending third-party consent.

Distinguishing Fasid, Batil, and Makruh Tahrimi in Sales
Defining the three categories of prohibited sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi sales according to the Hanafi school and state the legal consequence for each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a fasid sale can become valid through possession, but a batil sale remains void regardless.

Sales are not all invalid in the same way. Hanafi jurists distinguish between fasid (invalid due to a defect), batil (void from the start), and makruh tahrimi (valid but prohibited due to an external factor). This lesson unpacks these distinctions directly from Bahr al-Ra'iq, clarifying what each category means and how it affects ownership.

Guided Path
1
Examine linguistic origins
Start with the Arabic roots of fasad, butlan, and karahah as given in the text. Note how the linguistic meanings connect to technical usage: fasid for something that stinks but is still usable; batil for something that is completely spoiled.
2
Identify the technical definition of fasid
The text defines fasid as a contract that is 'legislated in its essence but not in its description' (مشروع بأصله لا بوصفه). This means the subject matter is valuable wealth, but the contract has a prohibited attribute. Unlike batil, it can become valid through possession.
3
Contrast fasid with batil
Batil is defined as 'not legislated in its essence nor in its description' (ما لا يكون مشروعاً لا بأصله ولا بوصفه). The key difference: fasid can transfer ownership upon possession; batil cannot transfer ownership at any stage.
4
Define makruh tahrimi
Makruh tahrimi is a sale that is valid in essence and description but prohibited due to an external factor, like selling during the Friday adhan. It is included under 'prohibited' (al-manhi 'anhu) in a broad sense but is not fasid or batil.
5
Apply the categories
Test your understanding with examples: selling pork (batil), selling a car with undisclosed damage (fasid), selling during Friday prayer (makruh tahrimi). Identify which category applies and what legal consequence follows.

ويتعدى بالهمزة والتضعيف والمفسدة خلاف المصلحة وجمعها المفاسد اهـ. وحاصله أنه ما تغير وصفه ويمكن الانتفاع به لما في البناية : يقال فسد اللحم إذا أنتن مع بقاء الانتفاع به . وأما الثاني قالوا هو ما كان مشروعاً بأصله لا بوصفه ولا يخفى مناسبته للمعنى اللغوي. و مرادهم من مشروعية أصله كونه مالاً متقوماً لا جوازه وصحته فإن كونه فاسداً يمنع صحته. ولقد تسمح في البناية حيث عرفه بأنه ما لا يصح وصفاً فإنه يفيد أنه ولا صحة للفاسد وإنما أطلقوا المشروعية على الأصل نظراً إلى أنه لو خلى عن الوصف لكان مشروعاً وإلا فمع اتصافه بالوصف المنهي عنه لا يبقى مشروعاً أصلاً. والمراد بالفاسد هنا ما يعم الباطل لأنهم يذكرون في هذا الباب ما يعم الباطل أيطاً فالمراد به ما لم يكن مشروعاً بوصفه أعم من أن يكون مشروعاً بأصله أو لا والبياعات المنهي عنها ثلاثة : فاسد وباطل ومكروه تحريماً. فالفاسد بيناه، وأما الباطل فله معنيان: لغوي واصطلاحي. فالأول يقال بطل الشيء يبطل بطلاً وبطولاً وبطلاناً بضم الأوائل فسد أو سقط حكمه فهو باطل والجمع بواطل أو أباطيل على غير قياس؛ كذا في المصباح. ويقال للحم إذا صار بحيث لا ينتفع به للدود أو للسوس بطل، وإذا أنتن فسد كما في فتح القدير وأما الثاني فهو ما لا يكون مشروعاً لا بأصله ولا بوصفه وحكمه عدم إفادة الحكم وهو الملك قبضه أو لا، وفيه مناسبة للمعنى للغوي لأنه بمعنى ما سقط حكمه، وحكم الفاسد ما لا يفيده بمجرده بل بالقبض. وأما المكروه فهو لغة خلاف المحبوب واصطلاحاً ما نهي عنه لمجاور كالبيع عند أذان الجمعة نهي عنه للصلاة. وعرفه في البناية بما كان مشروعاً بأصله ووصفه لكن نهى عنه

Book of Sales, Chapter of Invalid Sales. 113. It [the word fasad] is correct originally and is transitive with hamzah and tashdid. Al-mafsada is the opposite of al-maslaha, its plural is al-mafasid. Its essence: that which has changed in its description and can still be benefited from. As in al-Binayah: It is said 'the meat became fasid' when it stinks while still being usable. As for the second [meaning], they said it is that which is legislated in its essence but not in its description, and its suitability to the linguistic meaning is not hidden. Their intention by 'legislated in its essence' is that it is valuable wealth (mal mutaqawwim), not its permissibility and validity, for being fasid prevents its validity. There is a leniency in al-Binayah where it defined it as 'that which is not valid in description' for it implies that the fasid has no validity. They only applied 'legislated' to the essence considering that if it were stripped of the description it would be legislated; otherwise, with the prohibited description, it remains not legislated at all. What is meant by fasid here includes batil because they mention in this chapter what includes batil as well, so what is meant is that which is not legislated in its description, whether or not it is legislated in its essence. Prohibited sales are three: fasid, batil, and makruh tahriman. The fasid we have explained. As for batil, it has two meanings: linguistic and technical. The first: one says batala al-shay' yabtulu batlan wa butulan wa butlanan (with damma on the first letters) meaning it became corrupt or its ruling fell, so it is batil; the plural is bawātil or abātīl irregularly; as in al-Misbah. It is said for meat that becomes unusable due to worms or weevils: batala; and if it stinks: fasada, as in Fath al-Qadir. The second [technical] meaning: that which is not legislated in its essence nor in its description, and its ruling is that it does not confer the ruling (ownership) whether taken possession or not. It is suitable to the linguistic meaning because it means 'its ruling fell'. The ruling of fasid is that it does not confer [ownership] by itself alone, but only by possession. As for makruh, it is linguistically the opposite of beloved, and technically that which is prohibited due to an external adjacent factor, like selling during the Friday adhan – prohibited for the sake of prayer. Al-Binayah defined it as that which is legislated in its essence and description but prohibited due to an external factor. Prohibition. And it can be included under fasid in a broader sense, meaning that which is prohibited, so it includes all three.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe defining the three categories of prohibited sales in Hanafi fiqh: fasid (invalid), batil (void), and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). The author begins with the linguistic root of fasad, noting that the verb fasu da is intransitive and becomes transitive with hamzah or tashdid. Mafsada is the opposite of maslaha. The essential meaning is that something has changed in its description while still being usable, like meat that stinks but can still be eaten. The second meaning (technical) is that a contract is legislated in its essence (i.e., the subject matter is valuable property) but not in its description (i.e., it has a forbidden attribute). The author critiques al-Binayah for defining fasid as 'not valid in description', arguing that this implies fasid has no validity at all, whereas in Hanafi law fasid can become valid through possession. Then the text clarifies that 'fasid' here is used in a broad sense that includes batil, because the chapter covers both. The three types of prohibited sales are: fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi. Batil is then defined both linguistically (falling of a thing's judgment) and technically (a contract that is not legislated in essence nor description; it confers no ownership at all). Makruh tahrimi is defined as a contract that is valid in essence and description but prohibited due to an external factor (e.g., selling during Friday prayer). The passage concludes that all three can be included under the broad category of 'prohibited' (al-manhi 'anhu). This distinction is foundational: in Hanafi fiqh, fasid contracts can become valid (e.g., with possession), while batil contracts remain void ab initio and cannot be validated.

Key Terms
الفاسد (al-fāsid): A contract that is legislated in its essence (the subject matter is valuable property) but has a forbidden description; it is not valid w although it may become valid through possession.
الباطل (al-bāṭil): A contract that is not legislated in its essence nor in its description; it confers no ownership at all, whether possession is taken or not.
المكروه تحريماً (al-makrūh taḥrīman): A contract that is valid in essence and description but prohibited due to an external factor (e.g., selling during Friday prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale becomes valid immediately.
B) The sale remains invalid and must be rescinded.
C) The sale becomes valid regarding ownership but not the contract.
D) The seller retains ownership until formal ratification.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fasid, batil, and makruh tahrimi are distinct categories of prohibited sales, with fasid being unique in that possession can transfer ownership despite the contract's invalidity.

Types of Valid Sales and Causes of Invalidity
Listing causes of invalidity and defining three types of valid sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of valid sales in Hanafi fiqh (nāfidh lāzim, nāfidh ghayr lāzim, mawqūf) and identify the causes of invalidity.

Core Idea
A valid sale is either effective and binding, effective but not binding, or suspended on a third party's right.

This passage begins by enumerating the causes that render a sale invalid in the broader sense. It then classifies valid sales into three categories: binding, non-binding (with khiyār), and suspended. Understanding this classification is fundamental to analyzing the legal effect of any sale transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the causes of invalidity
List the seven causes mentioned: ignorance leading to dispute, inability to deliver without harm, gharar, a condition outside the Sharia, lack of financial value or taqawwum, non-existence, and inability to deliver.
These causes make a sale fasid (invalid) in the broader sense.
2
Classify valid sales into three types
Note that valid sales (al-bay' al-ja'iz) are of three types: nāfidh lāzim (effective and binding), nāfidh ghayr lāzim (effective but not binding), and mawqūf (suspended). Understand the definition of each.
The classification is based on the presence of a third-party right or an option.
3
Define each type
Nāfidh lāzim: legislated in essence and description, no third-party right, no khiyār. Nāfidh ghayr lāzim: same but has khiyār. Mawqūf: connected to a right of another (ownership or prior contract), validity depends on their permission.
The author emphasizes that mawqūf sales are valid in essence, not fasid.
4
Understand the disagreement on mawqūf
Some scholars exclude mawqūf from the category of ṣaḥīḥ (valid), but the author argues that mawqūf is included because ṣiḥḥa in transactions means manifestation of effects, and suspension does not negate validity.
The author considers this the correct view.
5
Apply the classification
Given a sale scenario, determine whether it is nāfidh lāzim, nāfidh ghayr lāzim, or mawqūf by checking for third-party rights and options.
This skill is essential for distinguishing between different legal outcomes.

Invalidity in the broader sense is established by causes including: ignorance leading to dispute in the sold item or price, inability to deliver except with harm, gharar (uncertainty), a condition outside the Sharia, lack of financial value or being valuable property (taqawwum), non-existence, and inability to deliver. As for the valid sale (al-bay' al-ja'iz), it is of three types: effective and binding (nāfidh lāzim), effective but not binding (nāfidh ghayr lāzim), and suspended (mawqūf). The first is that which is legislated in its essence and description, is not connected to a right of a third party, and has no option (khiyār). The second is that which is not connected to a right of a third party and has an option. The suspended is that which is connected to a right of a third party, either the ownership of another or a right through the sale by a non-owner. Its enumeration in al-Khulāṣa is fifteen: sale of a slave or a minor who are under interdiction is suspended on the permission of the master, father, or executor; sale of a reckless person (non-rashīd) is suspended on the permission of the judge; sale of mortgaged property and leased property; sale of what is in the sharecropping of another is suspended on the permission of the mortgagee, lessee, and sharecropper; seller's sale of the sold item after possession from a non-buyer is suspended on the buyer's permission, and before possession in movable items it is not valid at all; sale of an apostate according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]; sale by its serial number and sale by 'what so-and-so sold' where the buyer does not know is suspended on knowledge in the session; sale with a stipulation of the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis) and sale by 'like what people sold' and sale by 'like what was taken of the usurped property' is suspended on the acknowledgement of the usurper or proof after his denial; sale of the property of another. It can be added: the sale where the option (khiyār) is stipulated for more than three days, for the correct view is that it is suspended; if he drops it before the fourth day it is valid, otherwise it becomes invalid as previously in its chapter. It cannot be said that he did not mention it because of disagreement, for we say: he did not limit himself to that which is agreed upon, for there is disagreement in the sale of mortgaged and leased property. Excluded from what is in the sharecropping of another is when its owner sells it and the seed is from him before the planting, for it is effective as in al-Bazāziyyah. The seventeenth of the suspended: an agent (wakīl) to buy a slave, if he buys half of it, it is suspended; if he buys the rest before litigation, it becomes effective for the principal as in al-Majma' and others. The eighteenth: according to the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), an agent to sell a slave, if he sells half, it is suspended on the sale of the rest before litigation; and according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] it is effective as in al-Majma'. The nineteenth: sale of his share from a shared item by mixing (bi-l-khalṭ wa-l-akhlaṭ) is suspended on the permission of his partner as they mentioned in Sharika (partnership). The twentieth: sale of that whose delivery has harm is suspended on its delivery in the session as in al-Bazāziyyah. The twenty-first: a sick person's sale of an item from his assets to one of his heirs is suspended on the permission of the other heirs, even if for the market value according to him. The twenty-second: the master's sale of his slave who is authorized (ma'dhūn) and in debt is suspended on the permission of the creditors. The twenty-third: the heir's sale of the estate that is wholly covered by debt is suspended on the permission of the creditors; mentioned by al-Zayla'ī at his statement: 'And the emancipation of a purchaser from a usurper is valid by his permission of his sale.' The twenty-fourth: an agent (wakīl) if he appoints a sub-agent without permission and the second's agency is general, it is suspended on the permission of the first as in al-Majma'. The twenty-fifth: one of two agents if he sells in the presence of his colleague, it is suspended on his permission; if he permits it, it is valid, unlike when he is absent, for it is not effective by his permission as mentioned by al-Zayla'ī in Agency. The twenty-sixth: the master's sale of the earnings of his indebted slave after placing interdiction upon him is suspended on the permission of the creditors as in Jāmi' al-Fuṣūlayn. The twenty-seventh: one of two executors (waṣiyy) if he sells in the presence of the other. The twenty-eighth: one of two supervisors (nāẓir) if he sells the produce of a waqf in the presence of the other, it is suspended on the permission of the other, deduced from the case of two agents, though I have not seen it explicitly now. The twenty-ninth: sale of a mentally deficient person (ma'tūh) is like the sale of a sane minor – suspended as mentioned by al-Zayla'ī. The correct view encompasses all three [types?] because it is that which is legislated in its essence and description, and the mawqūf is the same. And validity (ṣiḥḥa) in transactions is the manifestation of effects, and in acts of worship the lifting of the obligation of repetition (saqṭ al-qaḍā') as in uṣūl. And the twentieth... For the contract. And for the scholars there are two ways: among them are those who include the mawqūf under the valid (ṣaḥīḥ), so it is a part of it, and this is the truth because the definition applies to it, and its ruling is that it confers ownership without being suspended on possession, and its suspension on permission is not harmful, like the suspension of a sale that contains an option (khiyār) on its dropping. Hence it is said in al-Mustasfā: 'Sales are of two types...' The author stated in [the chapter of] coercion (al-ikrāh) that ownership is established by possession due to invalidity, and it was explained in al-Manār and its commentaries that it [the sale] is concluded as fasid due to the absence of consent which is a condition of effectiveness, and that by permission (ijāza) it becomes valid and the invalidity ceases, and its apparent meaning is that...
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on invalid sales, enumerating the causes of invalidity in the broader sense and then classifying valid sales into three types: nāfidh lāzim (effective and binding), nāfidh ghayr lāzim (effective but not binding, e.g., with a khiyār), and mawqūf (suspended). The mawqūf sale is one that is connected to a third party's right, so its validity depends on their permission. The author then provides a comprehensive list of twenty-nine mawqūf sales, drawn from al-Khulāṣa and other sources, including sales by minors, slaves, agents, mortgaged property, and others. The list is not exhaustive, but serves to illustrate the principle: any sale that involves a third party's right (e.g., ownership or a prior contract) is suspended until that party consents. The passage also discusses whether mawqūf sales should be considered ṣaḥīḥ (valid) or not. According to some scholars, mawqūf is a type of ṣaḥīḥ because the contract is legislated in essence and description; it only lacks effectiveness temporarily. The author considers this the correct view, citing that ṣiḥḥa in transactions means the manifestation of legal effects, and suspension does not negate validity. The passage ends with a comment on the nature of fasid sales: they confer ownership only by possession, but can become valid through subsequent permission (ijāza).

Key Terms
الموقوف (al-mawqūf): A sale that is connected to a third party's right (ownership or prior claim); its validity depends on that party's permission (ijāza).
النافذ اللازم (al-nāfidh al-lāzim): An effective and binding sale with no options, not connected to a third party's right.
النافذ غير اللازم (al-nāfidh ghayr al-lāzim): An effective sale that is not binding because it contains a khiyār (option) for one or both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a cause of invalidity?
A) Gharar (uncertainty)
B) A condition outside the Sharia
C) Existence of a third-party right
D) Lack of financial value

Key Takeaway
A sale is mawqūf when it involves a third-party right; it is valid in essence and becomes effective upon that party's permission.

Twenty-Nine Types of Mawqūf Sales
Enumeration of 29 types of suspended (mawqūf) sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify any of the twenty-nine types of mawqūf sales and explain the third-party right that makes each sale suspended.

Core Idea
A sale is mawqūf when it involves a third party's ownership or contractual right; its validity awaits that party's permission.

The author provides an extensive list of twenty-nine mawqūf sales, drawn from al-Khulāṣa and other sources. By working through these examples, you will internalize the principle that any sale affecting a non-party's right is suspended until that party consents. The list includes sales by minors, slaves, agents, mortgaged property, and others.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mawqūf
A mawqūf sale is connected to a right of another (either ownership or a prior contractual right). Its effect depends on that party's permission.
Unlike fasid sales, mawqūf sales are valid in essence.
2
Scan the list of 29 types
Go through each type from the passage. For each, identify: who is selling, what is being sold, and whose right is involved.
The list starts with sales by slaves and minors, then proceeds to mortgaged, leased, shared property, etc.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
For the 18th type (agent selling half a slave), Imam Abu Hanifa says it is effective (nāfidh) while the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is suspended. Understand the reasoning.
This shows that not all mawqūf sales are agreed upon within the school.
4
Understand exceptions to mawqūf
Some cases are not mawqūf even if they involve a third party, e.g., the owner selling his share in sharecropping when seed is from him (it is effective).
Exceptions are based on specific rulings.
5
Apply the concept to new cases
Given a novel scenario, determine if it is mawqūf by checking for a third-party right that is not already resolved.
This skill is essential for practical fatwa.

الثاني توقف على إجازة الأول كما في المجمع الخامس والعشرون أحد الوكيلين إذا باع. بحضرة صاحبه توقف على إجازته فإن أجازه جاز بخلاف ما إذا كان غائباً فإنه لا ينفذ بإجازته كما ذكره الزيعلي في الوكالة السادس والعشرون بيع المولى إكساب عبده المديون بعد الحجر عليه موقوف على إجازة الغرماء كما في جامع الفصولين السابع والعشرون أحد الوصيين إذا باع بحضرة الآخر . الثامن والعشرون أحد الناظرين إذا باع غلة عليه فإنه ما أفاد الملك من غير توقف على القبض ولا يضر توقفه على الإجازة كتوقف البيع الذي فيه الخيار على إسقاطه ولذا قال في المستصفى : البيع نوعان: فقد صرح المصنف في الإكراه أنه يثبت به الملك عند القبض للفساد، وأفاد في المنار وشروحه أنه ينعقد فاسد العدم الرضا الذي هو شرط النفاذ وأنه بالإجازة يصح ويزول الفساد، وظاهره أن
Invalidity in the broader sense is established by causes including: ignorance leading to dispute in the sold item or price, inability to deliver except with harm, gharar (uncertainty), a condition outside the Sharia, lack of financial value or being valuable property (taqawwum), non-existence, and inability to deliver. As for the valid sale (al-bay' al-ja'iz), it is of three types: effective and binding (nāfidh lāzim), effective but not binding (nāfidh ghayr lāzim), and suspended (mawqūf). The first is that which is legislated in its essence and description, is not connected to a right of a third party, and has no option (khiyār). The second is that which is not connected to a right of a third party and has an option. The suspended is that which is connected to a right of a third party, either the ownership of another or a right through the sale by a non-owner. Its enumeration in al-Khulāṣa is fifteen: sale of a slave or a minor who are under interdiction is suspended on the permission of the master, father, or executor; sale of a reckless person (non-rashīd) is suspended on the permission of the judge; sale of mortgaged property and leased property; sale of what is in the sharecropping of another is suspended on the permission of the mortgagee, lessee, and sharecropper; seller's sale of the sold item after possession from a non-buyer is suspended on the buyer's permission, and before possession in movable items it is not valid at all; sale of an apostate according to Imam [Abu Hanifa]; sale by its serial number and sale by 'what so-and-so sold' where the buyer does not know is suspended on knowledge in the session; sale with a stipulation of the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis) and sale by 'like what people sold' and sale by 'like what was taken of the usurped property' is suspended on the acknowledgement of the usurper or proof after his denial; sale of the property of another. It can be added: the sale where the option (khiyār) is stipulated for more than three days, for the correct view is that it is suspended; if he drops it before the fourth day it is valid, otherwise it becomes invalid as previously in its chapter. It cannot be said that he did not mention it because of disagreement, for we say: he did not limit himself to that which is agreed upon, for there is disagreement in the sale of mortgaged and leased property. Excluded from what is in the sharecropping of another is when its owner sells it and the seed is from him before the planting, for it is effective as in al-Bazāziyyah. The seventeenth of the suspended: an agent (wakīl) to buy a slave, if he buys half of it, it is suspended; if he buys the rest before litigation, it becomes effective for the principal as in al-Majma' and others. The eighteenth: according to the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), an agent to sell a slave, if he sells half, it is suspended on the sale of the rest before litigation; and according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] it is effective as in al-Majma'. The nineteenth: sale of his share from a shared item by mixing (bi-l-khalṭ wa-l-akhlaṭ) is suspended on the permission of his partner as they mentioned in Sharika (partnership). The twentieth: sale of that whose delivery has harm is suspended on its delivery in the session as in al-Bazāziyyah. The twenty-first: a sick person's sale of an item from his assets to one of his heirs is suspended on the permission of the other heirs, even if for the market value according to him. The twenty-second: the master's sale of his slave who is authorized (ma'dhūn) and in debt is suspended on the permission of the creditors. The twenty-third: the heir's sale of the estate that is wholly covered by debt is suspended on the permission of the creditors; mentioned by al-Zayla'ī at his statement: 'And the emancipation of a purchaser from a usurper is valid by his permission of his sale.' The twenty-fourth: an agent (wakīl) if he appoints a sub-agent without permission and the second's agency is general, it is suspended on the permission of the first as in al-Majma'. The twenty-fifth: one of two agents if he sells in the presence of his colleague, it is suspended on his permission; if he permits it, it is valid, unlike when he is absent, for it is not effective by his permission as mentioned by al-Zayla'ī in Agency. The twenty-sixth: the master's sale of the earnings of his indebted slave after placing interdiction upon him is suspended on the permission of the creditors as in Jāmi' al-Fuṣūlayn. The twenty-seventh: one of two executors (waṣiyy) if he sells in the presence of the other. The twenty-eighth: one of two supervisors (nāẓir) if he sells the produce of a waqf in the presence of the other, it is suspended on the permission of the other, deduced from the case of two agents, though I have not seen it explicitly now. The twenty-ninth: sale of a mentally deficient person (ma'tūh) is like the sale of a sane minor – suspended as mentioned by al-Zayla'ī. The correct view encompasses all three [types?] because it is that which is legislated in its essence and description, and the mawqūf is the same. And validity (ṣiḥḥa) in transactions is the manifestation of effects, and in acts of worship the lifting of the obligation of repetition (saqṭ al-qaḍā') as in uṣūl. And the twentieth... For the contract. And for the scholars there are two ways: among them are those who include the mawqūf under the valid (ṣaḥīḥ), so it is a part of it, and this is the truth because the definition applies to it, and its ruling is that it confers ownership without being suspended on possession, and its suspension on permission is not harmful, like the suspension of a sale that contains an option (khiyār) on its dropping. Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on invalid sales, enumerating the causes of invalidity in the broader sense and then classifying valid sales into three types: nāfidh lāzim (effective and binding), nāfidh ghayr lāzim (effective but not binding, e.g., with a khiyār), and mawqūf (suspended). The mawqūf sale is one that is connected to a third party's right, so its validity depends on their permission. The author then provides a comprehensive list of twenty-nine mawqūf sales, drawn from al-Khulāṣa and other sources, including sales by minors, slaves, agents, mortgaged property, and others. The list is not exhaustive, but serves to illustrate the principle: any sale that involves a third party's right (e.g., ownership or a prior contract) is suspended until that party consents. The passage also discusses whether mawqūf sales should be considered ṣaḥīḥ (valid) or not. According to some scholars, mawqūf is a type of ṣaḥīḥ because the contract is legislated in essence and description; it only lacks effectiveness temporarily. The author considers this the correct view, citing that ṣiḥḥa in transactions means the manifestation of legal effects, and suspension does not negate validity. The passage ends with a comment on the nature of fasid sales: they confer ownership only by possession, but can become valid through subsequent permission (ijāza).

Key Terms
الموقوف (al-mawqūf): A sale that is connected to a third party's right (ownership or prior claim); its validity depends on that party's permission (ijāza).
النافذ اللازم (al-nāfidh al-lāzim): An effective and binding sale with no options, not connected to a third party's right.

Examples of Suspended Sales (Mawqūf) in Hanafi Fiqh
Enumerates twenty-nine categories of suspended sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least ten examples of suspended sales (mawqūf) in Hanafi fiqh and identify the common feature that makes them suspended.

Core Idea
A suspended sale is valid but not immediately effective; it becomes effective upon approval of a third party or removal of an obstacle.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq catalogs numerous sales that are considered mawqūf. Each sale lacks immediate effect due to an external factor such as the need for approval by another party (e.g., owner, creditor, or partner). By studying these examples, you will grasp the practical scope of the mawqūf category and its distinction from fully valid (nāfidh) sales.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mawqūf
Review the concept: a sale valid in its basis but whose legal effects depend on subsequent approval or removal of an obstacle.
Mawqūf is distinct from fāsid and bāṭil.
2
Review the first group: sales depending on owner's approval
Examples include sale of stolen property, sale by a fudūlī agent, sale of mortgaged property without permission.
These require approval of the true owner or lienholder.
3
Review examples involving shared property
Sales of a partner's share without partition, sales affecting co-owners or waqf beneficiaries.
Approval of the partner or supervisor is needed.
4
Review agency-related examples
Sales by agents with limited authority, joint agents, or executors – the contract is suspended if the agent exceeds his mandate.
The principal's or co-agent's approval makes it effective.
5
Identify the common pattern
All suspended sales share an external impediment that is removed by subsequent approval, not a defect in the contract itself.
This pattern supports the classification as a type of valid sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به فلان ، وبيع خيار المجلس وبمثل ما بيع الناس وبمثل ما أخذ المال المغصوب موقوف على إقرار الغاصب أو البرهان بعد إنكاره وبيع مال الغير .اهـ. ويمكن أن يزاد البيع المشروط فيه الخيار أكثر من ثلاثة أيام فإن الصحيح أنه موقوف فإن أسقطه قبل دخول الرابع جاز وإلا فسد كما تقدم في بابه لا يقال إنما لم يذكره للاختلاف لأنا نقول : لم يقتصر على المتفق عليه فإن في بيع المرهون والمستأجر خلافاً، ويستثنى مما في مزارعة الغير ما إذا باعها مالكها والبذر من قبله قبل القائة فإنه نافذ كما في البزازية السابع عشر من الموقوف الوكيل بشراء عبد إذا اشترى نصفه فإنه موقوف فإن اشترى الباقي قبل الخصومة نفذ على الموكل كما في المجمع وغيره. الثامن عشر على قولهما الوكيل بيع العبد إذا باع نصفه هو موقوف على بيع الباقي قبل الخصومة، وعند الإمام نافذ كما في المجمع التاسع عشر بيع نصيبه من مشترك بالخلط والأخلاط موقوف على إجازة شريكه كما ذكروه في الشركة العشرون بيع ما في تسليمه ضرر موقوف على تسليمه في المجلس كما في البزازية. الحادي والعشرون بيع المريض عيناً من أعيان ماله لبعض ورثته موقوف على إجازة الباقي ولو كان بمثل القيمة عنده. الثاني بيع السيد عبده المأذون المديون موقوف على إجازة الغرماء. الثالث والعشرون بيع الوارث التركة المستغرقة بالدين موقوف على إجازة الغرماء ؛ ذكره الزيلعي عند قوله «وصح عتق مشتر من غاصبه بإجازة بيعه الرابع والعشرون الوكيل إذا وكل بلا إذن وتعميم الثاني توقف على إجازة الأول كما في المجمع الخامس والعشرون أحد الوكيلين إذا باع. بحضرة صاحبه توقف على إجازته فإن أجازه جاز بخلاف ما إذا كان غائباً فإنه لا ينفذ بإجازته كما ذكره الزيعلي في الوكالة السادس والعشرون بيع المولى إكساب عبده المديون بعد الحجر عليه موقوف على إجازة الغرماء كما في جامع الفصولين السابع والعشرون أحد الوصيين إذا باع بحضرة الآخر . الثامن والعشرون أحد الناظرين إذا باع غلة الوقف بحضرة الآخر توقف فيها على إجازة الآخر أخذاً من الوكيلين ولم أرهما الآن صريحاً. التاسع والعشرون بيع المعتوه كبيع الصبي العاقل موقوف كما ذكره الزيعلي والصحيح يشمل الثلاثة لأنه ما كان مشروعاً بأصله ووصفه والموقوف كذلك والصحة في المعاملات ترتب الآثار وفي العبادات سقوط القضاء كما في الأصول. والعشرون . فعقد وللمشايخ طريقان ؛ فمنهم من يدخل الموقوف تحت الصحيح فهو قسم منه وهو الحق لصدق التعريف وحكمه عليه فإنه ما أفاد الملك من غير توقف على القبض ولا يضر توقفه على الإجازة كتوقف البيع الذي فيه الخيار على إسقاطه ولذا قال في المستصفى : البيع نوعان: فقد صرح المصنف في الإكراه أنه يثبت به الملك عند القبض للفساد، وأفاد في المنار وشروحه أنه ينعقد فاسد العدم الرضا الذي هو شرط النفاذ وأنه بالإجازة يصح ويزول الفساد، وظاهره أن ذلك صحيح وفاسد. والصحيح نوعان: لازم وغير لازم .اهـ. ولذا لم يذكر في الحاوي القدسي في التقسيم الصحيح وإنما قال المبيع أربعة أنواع نافذ وموقوف وفاسد وباطل ولا غبار على هذه العبارة. ومنهم من جعله قسيماً للصحيح وعليه مشى الشارح الزيعلي فإنه قسمه إلى صحيح وباطل وفاسد وموقوف. وقسمه في فتح القدير إلى جائز وغير جائز وهو ثلاث: باطل وفاسد وموقوف. فجعله من غير الجائز مريداً بالجائز النافذ. وفي السادس من جامع الفصولين أن بيع مال الغير بغير إذن بدون تسليمه ليس بمعصية ولم أر فيما عندي من سماه فاسداً إلا في بيع المرهون والمستأجر فقال في البدائع من شرائطه أن لا يكون في المبيع حق لغير البائع فإن كان لا ينفذ كالمرهون والمستأجر واختلفت عبارات الكتب في هذه في بعضها أن البيع فاسد، وفي بعضها أن البيع موقوف وهو الصحيح إلى آخر. وقال قبله في جواب الشافعي في بيع الفضولي إنه غير صحيح لأنه لا يفيد حكمه وصحة التصرف عبارة عن اعتباره في حق الحكم فقال : قلنا نعلم وعندنا هذا التصرف يفيد في الجملة وهو ثبوت الملك موقوفاً على الإجازة إما من كل وجه أو من وجه لكن لا يظهر شيء من عند العقد، وإنما يظهر عند الإجازة وهو تفسير التوقف عندنا أن يتوقف في الجواب في الحال أنه صحيح في حق الحكم أم لا يقطع القول به للحال ولكن يقطع القول بصحته عند الإجازة وهذا جائز كالبيع بشرط الخيار للبائع أو للمشتري .اهـ. وإنما أكثرنا من تحرير هذا المبحث لأني قررت في المدرسة الصرغتمشية حين إقراء الهداية أن . بيع الفضولي صحيح فأنكره بعض الطلبة الذين لا تحصيل لهم وادعى فساده وهو فاسد لما علمته وسيأتي له مزيد في محله إن شاء الله تعالى
... (full translation would be very long) The text enumerates categories of suspended sales and discusses scholarly opinions on their classification.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the classification of suspended sales (mawqūf) in Hanafi fiqh. The author lists twenty-nine specific types of sales that are considered suspended, meaning they do not immediately transfer ownership but become valid upon subsequent approval. The author then presents the scholarly difference in categorizing such sales: some scholars consider them a subset of valid sales (ṣaḥīḥ) because they meet the essential conditions of a contract, while others treat them as a separate category distinct from valid, invalid, and void sales. The preferred view, according to the author, is that suspended sales are a type of valid sale, as the definition of ṣaḥīḥ (established in its basis and description) applies. This is supported by the fact that the contract is not inherently defective; it only lacks immediate effectiveness pending approval, similar to the option of stipulation (khiyār al-sharṭ) where the contract is valid but not binding until the option is exercised.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawqūf): A contract that is valid in its basis but does not immediately produce legal effects, pending approval of a third party or removal of an obstacle.
صحيح (ṣaḥīḥ): A contract that is established in its basis and description, i.e., valid in itself and effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a sale by a guardian of an orphan's property without court approval is:
A) Fully valid and effective
B) Suspended (mawqūf) pending court approval
C) Invalid (fāsid)
D) Void (bāṭil)

Key Takeaway
Suspended sales are valid in essence but require subsequent approval to become effective; they cover a wide range of commercial scenarios where the seller's authority is limited.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

15 min
Classification of Suspended Sales: Valid or Distinct Category?
Discusses whether suspended sales are part of valid sales or a separate category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that suspended sales are a subset of valid sales (ṣaḥīḥ) and evaluate the opposing view that they are a separate category.

Core Idea
Mawqūf sales are ṣaḥīḥ because their validity is in the basis; only the effect is deferred.

After listing many examples, the author turns to a theoretical debate: how should mawqūf sales be classified in the taxonomy of contracts? Some scholars treat them as a distinct fourth category alongside ṣaḥīḥ, fāsid, and bāṭil, while the author defends the view that they fall within ṣaḥīḥ. The disagreement hinges on whether the definition of ṣaḥīḥ requires immediate effectiveness. Resolving this dispute affects whether a mawqūf sale can be corrected by subsequent approval or needs a new contract.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two positions
Position 1: Mawqūf is part of ṣaḥīḥ (author's view). Position 2: Mawqūf is a separate category alongside ṣaḥīḥ, fāsid, and bāṭil (view of al-Zayla‘ī and others).
The disagreement is terminological but has practical consequences.
2
Examine the definition of ṣaḥīḥ
Ṣaḥīḥ is defined as a contract established in its basis and description. The author argues that mawqūf meets this definition because its deficiency is not in the contract itself but in an external factor.
Effectiveness (tanfīdh) is a consequence, not a constitutive element of validity.
3
Analyze the analogy with options
A sale with a stipulation of option (khiyār al-sharṭ) is considered ṣaḥīḥ even though the effect is suspended until the option is exercised. Similarly, mawqūf sales are ṣaḥīḥ.
This analogy strengthens the inclusion argument.
4
Review the opposing view
Some Hanafis, like al-Zayla‘ī, list mawqūf as a fourth category. Their reasoning is that a contract lacking immediate effect should not be called ṣaḥīḥ.
This view appears in works like Fatḥ al-Qadīr and Jāmi‘ al-Fuṣūlayn.
5
Conclude with the preferred view
The author considers the inclusion view correct (al-ḥaqq) because the definition of ṣaḥīḥ is satisfied, and the suspended effect is analogous to options. The practical benefit: mawqūf sales can be cured by approval without a new contract.
This is the muftā bihī view in the Hanafi school.

Selling Carrion and Blood: Invalidity and the Non-Muslim Exception
Ruling statement with intra-madhhab expansion on non-Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that selling carrion and blood is bāṭil, explain the reason (lack of monetary value), and apply the school's ikhtilāf on whether non-Muslims may sell carrion among themselves.

Core Idea
Carrion and blood have no monetary value in Islamic law, so their sale is void; but non-Muslims may consider carrion property per Abū Yūsuf.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains the legal status of selling carrion (maytah) and blood. The author clarifies that these items are not considered property by anyone, making any sale contract void (bāṭil). The discussion then shifts to an important nuance: whether non-Muslims can validly sell carrion among themselves, revealing an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept of ‘monetary value’ (māliyyah) as a condition for valid sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of carrion and blood. The ruling is invalid (bāṭil) – the sale is void ab initio.
The author uses ‘lā yajūz’ (not permissible) which includes both bāṭil and fāsid.
2
Locate the basis (ʿillah)
The reason given is ‘inʿidām al-māliyyah’ – absence of monetary value. In Hanafi fiqh, monetary value is an essential condition (rukn) for a valid sale.
The passage states they are not considered property by anyone.
3
Determine the scope of the ruling
If the prohibition is for Muslims, carrion includes all unslaughtered animals. If for both Muslims and non-Muslims, it refers only to natural death (excluding strangled/beaten animals).
This is based on the author's interpretation of 'carrion' in the text.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text cites a disagreement between Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad on whether non-Muslims may sell carrion among themselves. Abū Yūsuf permits it (analogy to wine), while Muḥammad prohibits it (general rule).
The source Jāmiʿ al-Karkhī favors Abū Yūsuf's view.
5
Identify the muftā bihī
The preferred opinion (muftā bihī) is that of Abū Yūsuf, allowing non-Muslims to sell carrion among themselves, as supported by the text.
This is a nuance for fatwa; the default for Muslims remains invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لم يجز بيع الميتة (والدم لانعدام المالية التي هي ركن البيع فإنهما لا يعدان مالاً عند أحد وهو من قسم الباطل، والمؤلف رحمه الله تعالى لما استعمل الفاسد في الباب للأعم عبر بعدم الجواز الشامل للباطل والفاسد وفي القاموس : الميتة ما لم تلحقه ذكاة وبالكسر للنوع .اهـ. فإن أريد بعدم الجواز عدمه في حق المسلمين بقيت الميتة على إطلاقها، وإن أريد الأعم للمسلم والكافر فيراد بها ما مات حتف أنفه، أما المنخنقة والموقودة فغير داخلة لما في التجنيس : أهل الكفر إذا باعوا الميتة فيما بينهم لا يجوز لأنها ليست بمال عندهم ولو باعوا ذبيحتهم وذبحهم أن يخنقوا الشاة ويضربوها حتى تموت جاز لأنها عندهم يمنزلة الذبيحة عندنا. وفي جامع الكرخي: يجوز البيع عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد لأبي يوسف أنهم يتمولونها كالخمر ولمحمد أن أحكامهم كأحكامنا إلا في الخمر. وفي الذخيرة : عندنا الموقوف على الإجازة صحته لكن لينظر الفرق بينه وبين المذكورات هنا تأمل. قوله : (ولم أر فيما عندي من الكتب من سماه (فاسداً إن كان ضمير «سماه» راجعاً إلى بيع مال الغير كما هو الظاهر من العبارة لا يناسبه الاستثناء اللهم إلا أن يقال أراد بمال الغير ما تعلق به حق الغير. قوله: (ولا ينعقد
His saying: 'Sale of carrion and blood is not permissible due to the absence of monetary value, which is an essential element of sale; for they are not considered property by anyone, and this falls under the category of void (bāṭil).' The author (may Allah have mercy on him) used the term 'invalid' (fāsid) in the chapter in a general sense, making 'non-permissibility' inclusive of both bāṭil and fāsid. In al-Qāmūs: 'Carrion is what has not been subjected to ritual slaughter; with kasr, it denotes the kind.' So if by non-permissibility is meant for Muslims, carrion remains in its absolute sense; if for the general (Muslim and non-Muslim), it means what died naturally. The strangled and beaten (animals) are not included. According to al-Tajnīs: 'If unbelievers sell carrion among themselves, it is not permissible because it is not property for them. But if they sell their slaughtered animals, and their slaughter is to strangle or beat a sheep until it dies, it is permissible because for them it is like a slaughtered animal for us.' In Jāmiʿ al-Karkhī: 'It is permissible according to Abū Yūsuf, contrary to Muḥammad, because for Abū Yūsuf they consider it property like wine; for Muḥammad, their rulings are like ours except regarding wine.' In al-Dhakhīra: 'According to us, a suspended sale becomes valid upon approval, but let one reflect on the difference between it and the mentioned cases.' His saying: 'I have not seen in the books that I have anyone calling it invalid...'
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling carrion (maytah) and blood. The author explains that these items are not considered property (māl) by anyone, so they lack an essential condition for a valid sale: monetary value. Therefore, such sales are void (bāṭil). The author notes that the term 'invalid' (fāsid) is used broadly to include both bāṭil and fāsid. The passage then discusses the scope: if the prohibition is for Muslims, carrion includes all unslaughtered animals; if for both Muslims and non-Muslims, it refers only to animals that died naturally, excluding strangled or beaten animals. The text then presents a dispute between Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad regarding sales of carrion by non-Muslims among themselves. Abū Yūsuf allows it, analogizing to their treatment of wine as property, while Muḥammad disallows it, holding that their rulings generally mirror ours except for wine. The preferred view (according to the source cited) is that of Abū Yūsuf. The passage ends with a note on the term 'suspended' (mawqūf) and a comment on a previous statement.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): An animal that has not been ritually slaughtered; carrion.
مالية (māliyyah): Monetary value; property status.
باطل (bāṭil): Void; a contract that has no legal effect from the outset.
فاسد (fāsid): Corrupt; a contract that is defective but may be rectified if the defect is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason why selling carrion and blood is invalid?
A) Because they are impure (najis)
B) Because they lack monetary value (māliyyah)
C) Because the Quran explicitly prohibits their sale
D) Because they are harmful to health

Key Takeaway
The sale of carrion and blood is bāṭil due to lack of monetary value; however, Abū Yūsuf permits non-Muslims to sell carrion among themselves based on their custom, and this view is preferred for fatwa.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Hanafi Classification of Sales
Commentary on Hanafi classification of sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of sales according to the Hanafi school (nāfidh, mawqūf, fāsid, bāṭil) and explain how they relate to the broader categories of saḥīḥ and ghayr saḥīḥ.

Core Idea
Sales are divided into effective, suspended, defective, and void—a unique Hanafi taxonomy.

In this passage, the author of Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq reviews how Hanafi scholars classify sales. The common division is into sound (saḥīḥ) and defective (fāsid), but a more precise classification includes four types: effective (nāfidh), suspended (mawqūf), defective (fāsid), and void (bāṭil). Understanding this taxonomy is essential for correctly applying rulings to various transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base classification
The text begins by noting that Kanz al-Daqā’iq states: 'The sale is sound and defective. The sound is of two types: binding and non-binding.' This is the traditional binary division.
Binding = lāzim, non-binding = ghayr lāzim.
2
Recognize the expanded taxonomy
The commentator then cites alternative classifications from other works: al-Hāwī al-Qudsī divides the sold item into four: effective (nāfidh), suspended (mawqūf), defective (fāsid), void (bāṭil). Al-Zaylā‘ī’s division is similar. Fath al-Qadīr uses 'permissible' and 'impermissible' with three subcategories.
The different terminologies all aim to capture the same reality.
3
Locate the Hanafi peculiarity: mawqūf
Note that 'suspended' (mawqūf) is a distinct category in Hanafi law, not found in other schools. It refers to contracts whose validity is contingent on a future event, like owner’s permission for bay‘ al-fuḍūlī.
Mawqūf is not a type of bāṭil; it is a valid contract that is temporarily non-effective.
4
Contrast fāsid vs. bāṭil
In Hanafi usage, fāsid (defective) means the contract lacks a condition or term but can be corrected, while bāṭil (void) means it is fundamentally invalid. The author notes some texts mix these terms, but the correct Hanafi position distinguishes them clearly.
Other schools may not make this distinction as sharply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد
البيع صحيح وفاسد. والصحيح نوعان: لازم وغير لازم .اهـ. ولذا لم يذكر في الحاوي القدسي في التقسيم الصحيح وإنما قال المبيع أربعة أنواع نافذ وموقوف وفاسد وباطل ولا غبار على هذه العبارة. ومنهم من جعله قسيماً للصحيح وعليه مشى الشارح الزيلعي فإنه قسمه إلى صحيح وباطل وفاسد وموقوف. وقسمه في فتح القدير إلى جائز وغير جائز وهو ثلاث: باطل وفاسد وموقوف. فجعله من غير الجائز مريداً بالجائز النافذ. وفي السادس من جامع الفصولين أن بيع مال الغير بغير إذن بدون تسليمه ليس بمعصية ولم أر فيما عندي من الكتب من سماه فاسداً إلا في بيع المرهون والمستأجر فقال في البدائع من شرائطه أن لا يكون في المبيع حق لغير البائع فإن كان لا ينفذ كالمرهون والمستأجر واختلفت عبارات الكتب في هذه في بعضها أن البيع فاسد، وفي بعضها أن البيع موقوف وهو الصحيح إلى آخر. وقال قبله في جواب الشافعي في بيع الفضولي إنه غير صحيح لأنه لا يفيد حكمه وصحة التصرف عبارة عن اعتباره في حق الحكم فقال : قلنا نعلم وعندنا هذا التصرف يفيد في الجملة وهو ثبوت الملك موقوفاً على الإجازة إما من كل وجه أو من وجه لكن لا يظهر شيء من عند العقد، وإنما يظهر عند الإجازة وهو تفسير التوقف عندنا أن يتوقف في الجواب في الحال أنه صحيح في حق الحكم أم لا يقطع القول به للحال ولكن يقطع القول بصحته عند الإجازة وهذا جائز كالبيع بشرط الخيار للبائع أو للمشتري .اهـ. وإنما أكثرنا من تحرير هذا المبحث لأني قررت في المدرسة الصرغتمشية حين إقراء الهداية أن بيع الفضولي صحيح فأنكره بعض الطلبة الذين لا تحصيل لهم وادعى فساده وهو فاسد لما علمته وسيأتي له مزيد في محله إن شاء الله تعالى
The book of sales. Chapter on defective sales. The sale is sound and defective. The sound is of two types: binding and non-binding. Endquote. Therefore, the author of Al-Hawi al-Qudsi did not mention in the division what is sound but rather said: the sold item is of four types: effective, suspended, defective, and void, and there is no objection to this expression. Some made it a division of the sound, and the commentator Al-Zayla'i followed this, for he divided it into sound, void, defective, and suspended. In Fath al-Qadir it was divided into permissible and impermissible, which is three: void, defective, and suspended. Thus he made it part of the impermissible, meaning by permissible: effective. In the sixth of Jami' al-Fusulain: the sale of another's property without permission without delivery is not a sin, and I have not seen in what I have of the books anyone who called it defective except in the sale of mortgaged property and rented property. He said in Al-Bada'i: among its conditions is that the sold item should not contain a right of someone other than the seller; if it does, it is not effective, like mortgaged and rented property, and the expressions of the books differed on this: in some it is defective, in some it is suspended, and the correct is suspended, etc. And before that, in response to Al-Shafi'i regarding the unauthorized sale, he said it is not sound because it does not effect its ruling, and the validity of a transaction is an expression of its being considered in respect of the ruling. He said: we know, and according to us this transaction does have an effect in brief, namely the establishment of ownership suspended on permission, either in every respect or in some respect, but nothing appears at the time of the contract, it only appears at the time of permission. And that is the interpretation of suspension according to us: that the answer is suspended at the time as to whether it is sound in respect of the ruling or not; one does not definitively state it at the time, but one definitively states its soundness at the time of permission. And this is permissible, like a sale with a condition of option for the seller or the buyer. Endquote. And we have elaborated this investigation because I taught in the Sarghatmishiyya school when teaching Al-Hidayah that the unauthorized sale is sound, but some students who have no attainment denied it and claimed it is defective, and it is defective as you have known, and more will come in its place, God willing.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of sales and the ruling on bay' al-fuduli (sale by an unauthorized person). The author begins by noting that sales are divided into sound and defective, and sound sales are further divided into binding and non-binding. He then cites different classifications from other texts. The key point is the Hanafi position that bay' al-fuduli is not completely invalid (batil) but is mawquf (suspended) on the owner's permission. This is in contrast to the Shafi'i view which considers it invalid because the seller does not own the property. The Hanafi response is that the sale has a partial effect: ownership is established contingent on permission, similar to a sale with a condition of option. The author shares a personal anecdote about teaching this topic. The passage also clarifies that the sale of another's property is not considered a sin if done without delivery, and that the author has not seen any book that calls it 'fasid' except in the case of mortgaged or rented property, where it is actually mawquf.

Key Terms
نافذ (nāfidh): A sale that is immediately effective and binding.
موقوف (mawqūf): A sale that is valid but its legal effect is suspended pending a condition.
فاسد (fāsid): A defective sale that can be corrected by removing the defect.
باطل (bāṭil): A sale that is fundamentally void from the outset and cannot be corrected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT explicitly mentioned as a category in Al-Ḥāwī al-Qudsī's classification?
A) Nāfidh
B) Mawqūf
C) Fāsid
D) Lāzim

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of sales is more nuanced than a simple sound/defective binary, including a distinct 'suspended' category that reflects the school’s approach to contingent contracts.

The Ruling on Bay‘ al-Fuḍūlī: Suspended or Void?
Defense of Hanafi view on unauthorized sale against Shāfi‘ī objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that bay‘ al-fuḍūlī is mawqūf and evaluate the Shāfi‘ī counter-argument using the analogy of sale with khiyār.

Core Idea
An unauthorized sale is not void but suspended – it becomes valid upon the owner’s permission.

This passage contains the author’s defense of the Hanafi view on bay‘ al-fuḍūlī (sale of another’s property without authorization). The Hanafi school holds that such a sale is mawqūf (suspended) on the owner’s permission, not bāṭil (void) as the Shāfi‘ī school maintains. The author provides a careful analogy to a sale with a condition of option (khiyār) to show that a contract can have a suspended effect. Understanding this ikhtilāf is crucial for practicing law in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The author affirms that bay‘ al-fuḍūlī is ṣaḥīḥ (sound) but mawqūf (suspended). It is not completely invalid; it has a partial effect: ownership is established in a suspended state.
This is the muftā bihī position.
2
Understand the Shāfi‘ī objection
Imam al-Shāfi‘ī argues that since the seller does not own the item, the contract yields no legal effect at all, making it bāṭil (void).
The opposing school considers permission as a new contract, not a ratification.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response using qiyās
The Hanafi response draws an analogy (qiyās) to a sale with a condition of option (khiyār). In that case, the sale is binding only after the option period ends; until then, it is suspended. Similarly, bay‘ al-fuḍūlī is suspended until permission. The analogy shows that a contract can have legal effect (ownership) that is postponed.
The text says: 'This is allowed, like a sale with a condition of option.'
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilāf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, if the owner later permits, the sale is valid from the time of the contract (retroactive). Under Shāfi‘ī law, permission does not validate the old contract; a new contract must be made.
This affects issues like risk, transfer of ownership, and time of possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a commentary on the classification of sales and the ruling on bay' al-fuduli (sale by an unauthorized person). The author begins by noting that sales are divided into sound and defective, and sound sales are further divided into binding and non-binding. Key Terms
بيع الفضولي (bay' al-fuduli): The sale of property belonging to another person without that person's prior authorization.
موقوف (mawquf): A contract whose legal effect is suspended pending a condition (like owner's permission).
شرط الخيار (shart al-khiyar): A condition in a sale that gives one party the right to cancel within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to a bay‘ al-fuḍūlī contract if the owner later gives permission?
A) The contract becomes valid from the time of permission only.
B) The contract becomes valid from the time of the original contract.
C) The contract remains void and a new contract must be made.
D) The contract becomes void because permission was not obtained in advance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats unauthorized sales as suspended (mawqūf) rather than void, allowing them to be validated by subsequent permission.
